Causality and Created Things
beginner
5 min
Reward and Punishment as Divine Signs
Sanusi declares reward/punishment as signs, not caused by actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that reward and punishment have no rational cause but are indicated by obedience and disobedience as created signs from Allah.

Core Idea
Reward and punishment are signs, not effects of our actions.

Sanusi begins by clarifying a foundational principle: human actions do not cause divine reward or punishment. Instead, obedience and disobedience are mere signs (amārāt) created by Allah to indicate what He has decreed. This guards against rationalist views that make God's judgment predictable or constrained by human deeds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on reward and punishment
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Note the key claim: 'لا سبب لهما عقلاً' (they have no rational cause). Reflect on what this means for moral responsibility.
The Quranic verse quoted (21:23) reinforces God's absolute sovereignty.
2
Understand the role of signs
Identify that obedience and disobedience are described as 'أمارتان مخلوقتان' (two created signs). They do not cause reward/punishment but indicate it by divine choice.
3
Consider the hypothetical reversal
Sanusi states that if Allah reversed the indication or gave reward/punishment without a sign, it would still be good. This shows that signs are not necessary for His justice.
This refutes the idea that God must reward obedience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثواب والعقاب لا سبب لهما عقلاً ، وإنَّما الطاعةُ والمعصية أمارتان مخلوقتانِ اللهِ تعالى بلا واسطةٍ مُعِينَةٍ مِنَ العبد ، تدلانِ شرعاً على ما اختار سبحانه وتعالى مِنَ الثواب والعقاب ، ولو عَكَسَ سبحانه في دلالتهما ، أو أثابَ أو عاقب بدءاً بلا سَبْقِ أمارة . . لحسُنَ ذلكَ منهُ جلَّ وعزَّ ، لَا يُسْلُ عَمَّا يَفْعَلُ ﴾ [الأنبياء : ٢٣] وكسب العبدِ : عبارة عن إيجادِ اللهِ تعالى المقدور فيهِ - كالحركة والسكون مثلاً - مصاحباً لقدرة حادثة فيه تتعلَّقُ بذلكَ المقدورِ مِنْ غيرِ تأثيرٍ لها فيه أصلاً ، وهذا الكسب هو مُتعلَّقُ التكليف الشرعي ، وأمارة الثواب والعقاب شرعاً لا عقلاً. والذي يدلُّ على مصاحبة هذه القدرة الحادثة للفعل - وإن لم يكن لها فيه تأثير البتة - : إدراكنا الفَرْقَ ضرورةً بينَ حركة الارتعاش ونحوها مِنَ الحركات الاضطرارية ، وبينَ غيرها مِنَ الحركاتِ الاختياريَّةِ ، ولا فَرْقَ بينَهما بعدَ السَّبْرِ التام إلا كَوْنُ هذهِ الاختياريَّةِ مقرونة بقدرة حادثة في العبدِ يُحِسُّ بها تيشر الفعل عليه ، بخلافِ الأُولى الاضطرارِيَّةِ فخرجَ لكَ مِنْ هذا : أنَّ بقولنا : ( إنَّ مع الفعل الذي لم يُحِسَّ صاحبُهُ فِيهِ الاضطرار قدرةً حادثة في العبدِ هي
Reward and punishment have no rational cause; rather obedience and disobedience are two created signs by God, Exalted, without any mediating assistance from the servant, indicating by the Law what He, Glorified and Exalted, has chosen of reward and punishment. And if He reversed their indication, or rewarded or punished initially without a preceding sign, that would be good from Him, Mighty and Majestic. 'He is not questioned about what He does' [Quran 21:23]. And the kasb (acquisition) of the servant is an expression of God's creation of that which is within the servant's power — such as movement and stillness — accompanied by a newly originated power in the servant relating to that which is within his power without any effect for it in it at all. And this kasb is the subject of legal obligation and a sign of reward and punishment by the Law, not by reason. And that which indicates the accompaniment of this newly originated power to the act — though it has no effect in it at all — is our necessary perception of the difference between the movement of tremor and similar involuntary movements and other voluntary movements; and there is no difference between them after complete investigation except that these voluntary ones are accompanied by a newly originated power in the servant which he senses facilitates the act for him, unlike the first involuntary ones. So it results from this: that by our saying: 'There is with the act in which its agent does not feel compulsion a newly originated power in the servant which is...'
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage covers three interconnected topics. First, reward and punishment are not rationally determined but based on God's will; obedience and disobedience are mere signs (amārāt) created by God to indicate His judgment. God is not questioned about His actions. Second, the definition of kasb (acquisition): it is God's creation of the act within the servant's sphere of power (al-maqdūr), accompanied by a newly originated power (qudra ḥāditha) in the servant that relates to the act without affecting it. This kasb is the basis of legal obligation and the sign for reward/punishment in sharīʿa, not in reason. Third, evidence for this accompanying power: we necessarily perceive a difference between involuntary movements (e.g., tremor) and voluntary ones. The only discernible difference is that voluntary movements are accompanied by a power in the servant that facilitates the act. This proves the existence of a power that has no causal effect but serves as a condition for moral responsibility.

Key Terms
ثواب
عقاب
أمارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One earns reward or punishment by their own actions as a direct causal consequence.
Correction: Sanusi says reward/punishment have no rational cause (عقلاً); actions are only signs (أمارات) that indicate what Allah chooses.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the relationship between obedience/disobedience and reward/punishment?
A) Obedience causes reward, disobedience causes punishment.
B) They are created signs that indicate what Allah has chosen.
C) They are the rational basis for God's judgment.
D) They are illusions with no connection to reward.

Key Takeaway
Reward and punishment are not caused by our actions but are divine signs that depend solely on Allah's will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you obey Allah, do you secretly feel He owes you something? How does this lesson challenge that assumption and call you to pure worship?
?
If your good deeds are not the cause of Paradise, are you still motivated to obey? What does that reveal about the state of your heart and your love for Allah?
?
Has there been a moment you questioned Allah's justice because you didn't receive what you thought you earned? How does Sanusi's teaching that reward and punishment are signs, not effects, reshape your trust in Him?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
5 min
The Definition of Kasb (Acquisition)
Sanusi defines kasb as God's creation accompanied by an ineffi cacious power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definition of kasb as God's creation of the act accompanied by a non-effi cacious power in the servant.

Core Idea
Kasb is God's act plus a powerless attribute in us.

Having established that actions are signs, Sanusi now defines the central Ash'ari concept of kasb (acquisition). This term reconciles human responsibility with divine omnipotence: the act is wholly created by God, but it is accompanied by a power (qudra ḥāditha) in the servant that has no causal effect. This power is the basis for reward and punishment in the Law.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of kasb
Examine the Arabic phrase: 'كسب العبد : عبارة عن إيجاد الله تعالى المقدور فيه مصاحباً لقدرة حادثة'. Note the key components: God's creation (إيجاد), the object of power (المقدور), and the accompanying power (قدرة حادثة).
The word 'مصاحباً' indicates accompaniment without causation.
2
Understand the role of the accompanying power
Sanusi stresses that the power has no effect (لا تأثير لها فيه أصلاً). Its sole function is to make the act the servant's acquisition for moral accountability.
3
Connect to legal obligation
The passage says 'وهذا الكسب هو متعلق التكليف الشرعي'. The kasb is what the Law addresses, not the act itself as created by God directly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثواب والعقاب لا سبب لهما عقلاً ، وإنَّما الطاعةُ والمعصية أمارتان مخلوقتانِ اللهِ تعالى بلا واسطةٍ مُعِينَةٍ مِنَ العبد ، تدلانِ شرعاً على ما اختار سبحانه وتعالى مِنَ الثواب والعقاب ، ولو عَكَسَ سبحانه في دلالتهما ، أو أثابَ أو عاقب بدءاً بلا سَبْقِ أمارة . . لحسُنَ ذلكَ منهُ جلَّ وعزَّ ، لَا يُسْلُ عَمَّا يَفْعَلُ ﴾ [الأنبياء : ٢٣] وكسب العبدِ : عبارة عن إيجادِ اللهِ تعالى المقدور فيهِ - كالحركة والسكون مثلاً - مصاحباً لقدرة حادثة فيه تتعلَّقُ بذلكَ المقدورِ مِنْ غيرِ تأثيرٍ لها فيه أصلاً ، وهذا الكسب هو مُتعلَّقُ التكليف الشرعي ، وأمارة الثواب والعقاب شرعاً لا عقلاً. والذي يدلُّ على مصاحبة هذه القدرة الحادثة للفعل - وإن لم يكن لها فيه تأثير البتة - : إدراكنا الفَرْقَ ضرورةً بينَ حركة الارتعاش ونحوها مِنَ الحركات الاضطرارية ، وبينَ غيرها مِنَ الحركاتِ الاختياريَّةِ ، ولا فَرْقَ بينَهما بعدَ السَّبْرِ التام إلا كَوْنُ هذهِ الاختياريَّةِ مقرونة بقدرة حادثة في العبدِ يُحِسُّ بها تيشر الفعل عليه ، بخلافِ الأُولى الاضطرارِيَّةِ فخرجَ لكَ مِنْ هذا : أنَّ بقولنا : ( إنَّ مع الفعل الذي لم يُحِسَّ صاحبُهُ فِيهِ الاضطرار قدرةً حادثة في العبدِ هي
Reward and punishment have no rational cause; rather obedience and disobedience are two created signs by God, Exalted, without any mediating assistance from the servant, indicating by the Law what He, Glorified and Exalted, has chosen of reward and punishment. And if He reversed their indication, or rewarded or punished initially without a preceding sign, that would be good from Him, Mighty and Majestic. 'He is not questioned about what He does' [Quran 21:23]. And the kasb (acquisition) of the servant is an expression of God's creation of that which is within the servant's power — such as movement and stillness — accompanied by a newly originated power in the servant relating to that which is within his power without any effect for it in it at all. And this kasb is the subject of legal obligation and a sign of reward and punishment by the Law, not by reason. And that which indicates the accompaniment of this newly originated power to the act — though it has no effect in it at all — is our necessary perception of the difference between the movement of tremor and similar involuntary movements and other voluntary movements; and there is no difference between them after complete investigation except that these voluntary ones are accompanied by a newly originated power in the servant which he senses facilitates the act for him, unlike the first involuntary ones. So it results from this: that by our saying: 'There is with the act in which its agent does not feel compulsion a newly originated power in the servant which is...'

Commentary
This passage covers three interconnected topics. First, reward and punishment are not rationally determined but based on God's will; obedience and disobedience are mere signs (amārāt) created by God to indicate His judgment. God is not questioned about His actions. Second, the definition of kasb (acquisition): it is God's creation of the act within the servant's sphere of power (al-maqdūr), accompanied by a newly originated power (qudra ḥāditha) in the servant that relates to the act without affecting it. This kasb is the basis of legal obligation and the sign for reward/punishment in sharīʿa, not in reason. Third, evidence for this accompanying power: we necessarily perceive a difference between involuntary movements (e.g., tremor) and voluntary ones. The only discernible difference is that voluntary movements are accompanied by a power in the servant that facilitates the act. This proves the existence of a power that has no causal effect but serves as a condition for moral responsibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The accompanying power (qudra ḥāditha) is a real cause of the act.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly says it has no effect (لا تأثير لها فيه أصلاً). It merely accompanies the act to denote acquisition.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi mean by 'kasb'?
A) The servant creates his own actions.
B) God creates the act and the servant acquires it through a non-effi cacious power.
C) The servant and God jointly create the act.
D) The act is an illusion.

Key Takeaway
Kasb is God's sole creation of the act, but the servant acquires it through an accompanying power that does not cause the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you truly believe that God created that act and your power had no effect? How does this knowledge transform your repentance and your view of your own weakness?
?
If your power is merely an accompaniment without effect, what remains of your pride in good deeds? Can you still feel ownership without falling into self-praise, and how would that change your gratitude to God?
?
The passage says kasb is a sign for reward and punishment by Sharia, not reason. Does your heart accept that? Are you willing to be held accountable for acts you did not fully cause, and what does that demand of your trust in God's justice?

Proof of the Accompanying Power
Sanusi proves the accompanying power via voluntary vs. involuntary difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi proves the existence of a non-causal accompanying power by comparing voluntary and involuntary movements.

Core Idea
We sense a power in voluntary acts absent in tremors.

Sanusi now provides empirical evidence for the accompanying power (qudra ḥāditha). He argues that we necessarily perceive a difference between involuntary movements (like a tremor) and voluntary actions. The only discernible difference is that voluntary acts are accompanied by a power we sense which facilitates the act. This power, though causally inert, is real and the basis for moral responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proof structure
Read the passage carefully. Sanusi states that the indicator (الدليل) is our necessary perception of the difference between involuntary movements (حركة الارتعاش) and voluntary ones (الحركات الاختيارية).
'اضطرارية' means involuntary, 'اختيارية' means voluntary.
2
Analyze the comparison
After complete investigation (بعد السبر التام), the only difference is that voluntary movements are accompanied by a newly originated power that the person senses facilitates the act (يُحِسُّ بها تيسَّر الفعل عليه).
The OCR has 'تيشر' which is likely 'تيسَّر' (facilitates).
3
Draw the conclusion
From this, Sanusi concludes that there is a power with the act when no compulsion is felt. This power is the kasb's basis.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the conclusion is that this power exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثواب والعقاب لا سبب لهما عقلاً ، وإنَّما الطاعةُ والمعصية أمارتان مخلوقتانِ اللهِ تعالى بلا واسطةٍ مُعِينَةٍ مِنَ العبد ، تدلانِ شرعاً على ما اختار سبحانه وتعالى مِنَ الثواب والعقاب ، ولو عَكَسَ سبحانه في دلالتهما ، أو أثابَ أو عاقب بدءاً بلا سَبْقِ أمارة . . لحسُنَ ذلكَ منهُ جلَّ وعزَّ ، لَا يُسْلُ عَمَّا يَفْعَلُ ﴾ [الأنبياء : ٢٣] وكسب العبدِ : عبارة عن إيجادِ اللهِ تعالى المقدور فيهِ - كالحركة والسكون مثلاً - مصاحباً لقدرة حادثة فيه تتعلَّقُ بذلكَ المقدورِ مِنْ غيرِ تأثيرٍ لها فيه أصلاً ، وهذا الكسب هو مُتعلَّقُ التكليف الشرعي ، وأمارة الثواب والعقاب شرعاً لا عقلاً. والذي يدلُّ على مصاحبة هذه القدرة الحادثة للفعل - وإن لم يكن لها فيه تأثير البتة - : إدراكنا الفَرْقَ ضرورةً بينَ حركة الارتعاش ونحوها مِنَ الحركات الاضطرارية ، وبينَ غيرها مِنَ الحركاتِ الاختياريَّةِ ، ولا فَرْقَ بينَهما بعدَ السَّبْرِ التام إلا كَوْنُ هذهِ الاختياريَّةِ مقرونة بقدرة حادثة في العبدِ يُحِسُّ بها تيشر الفعل عليه ، بخلافِ الأُولى الاضطرارِيَّةِ فخرجَ لكَ مِنْ هذا : أنَّ بقولنا : ( إنَّ مع الفعل الذي لم يُحِسَّ صاحبُهُ فِيهِ الاضطرار قدرةً حادثة في العبدِ هي
Reward and punishment have no rational cause; rather obedience and disobedience are two created signs by God, Exalted, without any mediating assistance from the servant, indicating by the Law what He, Glorified and Exalted, has chosen of reward and punishment. And if He reversed their indication, or rewarded or punished initially without a preceding sign, that would be good from Him, Mighty and Majestic. 'He is not questioned about what He does' [Quran 21:23]. And the kasb (acquisition) of the servant is an expression of God's creation of that which is within the servant's power — such as movement and stillness — accompanied by a newly originated power in the servant relating to that which is within his power without any effect for it in it at all. And this kasb is the subject of legal obligation and a sign of reward and punishment by the Law, not by reason. And that which indicates the accompaniment of this newly originated power to the act — though it has no effect in it at all — is our necessary perception of the difference between the movement of tremor and similar involuntary movements and other voluntary movements; and there is no difference between them after complete investigation except that these voluntary ones are accompanied by a newly originated power in the servant which he senses facilitates the act for him, unlike the first involuntary ones. So it results from this: that by our saying: 'There is with the act in which its agent does not feel compulsion a newly originated power in the servant which is...'

Commentary

First, reward and punishment are not rationally determined but based on God's will; obedience and disobedience are mere signs (amārāt) created by God to indicate His judgment. God is not questioned about His actions. This kasb is the basis of legal obligation and the sign for reward/punishment in sharīʿa, not in reason. The only discernible difference is that voluntary movements are accompanied by a power in the servant that facilitates the act. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Voluntary and involuntary movements are essentially the same with no real distinction.
Correction: Sanusi argues we necessarily perceive a difference, and that difference is the presence of an accompanying power in voluntary acts.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the only difference between a tremor (involuntary) and a voluntary movement?
A) One is created by God, the other by the servant.
B) The voluntary movement is accompanied by a power the agent senses facilitating it.
C) The involuntary movement is not real.
D) There is no difference; both are the same.

Key Takeaway
The perceived difference between a tremor and a voluntary act reveals an accompanying power that, though causally inert, grounds moral responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you feel a sense of personal power and ownership? How does this lesson's teaching that your sensed power is not the true cause challenge that feeling? Will you still take pride in your deeds knowing God alone creates them?
?
Think of a time you succeeded at something difficult. Did you attribute that success to your own ability? After reading that the accompanying power only facilitates but does not cause, how must your gratitude towards Allah change? What will you now say to yourself when you feel capable?
?
Your voluntary acts are accompanied by a power you sense, but it has no effect. Does this truth humble you or frighten you? Does it make you more reliant on Allah or more confused? How will you live differently starting today, knowing your will is a sign of His facilitation, not your independence?

Causality and Created Things
beginner
5 min
Denial of Causal Efficacy in Physical Objects
Sanusi enumerates common objects to deny their causal efficacy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash‘arī doctrine that no created thing has intrinsic causal effect, and that all effects are directly from Allah.

Core Idea
Nothing in creation truly causes anything—Allah alone is the causer.

In this passage, Sanusi drives home the radical dependence of all creation on Allah. He provides a list of everyday examples—food, fire, water, clothing, etc.—to show that none of them have any real effect in producing their typical outcomes. This is not a denial of observable regularities, but a denial that any created thing has inherent power to cause. Let's read the passage and see how Sanusi systematically denies causal efficacy to a range of physical objects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, noticing how each example follows the same pattern: 'no effect for X in Y'. The English translation is provided to help.
The passage ends abruptly with 'nor for water'—the next page likely continued the list.
2
Reflect on the list
Consider why Sanusi includes so many examples. What is he emphasizing? Think about how each object is commonly believed to cause its typical effect, and how Sanusi denies that.
3
Connect to the broader principle
This passage is part of the Ash‘arī doctrine of occasionalism. If objects have no real effect, what accounts for the regular patterns we observe? The answer is Allah's custom (ʿāda).
Ash‘arīs affirm regular correlation but deny necessary causal connection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا لا أثر للطعام في الشبع ، ولا للماء في الري والنبات والنظافة ، ولا للنار في الإحراق أو التسخين أو نضْحِ الطعامِ ، ولا للثوبِ أوِ الجدارِ في السَّتْرِ أو دفع الحرّ والبرد ، ولا للشجرةِ في الظل ، ولا للشمس وسائرِ الكواكب في الضوء ، ولا للسكين في القطع ، ولا للماء
And likewise, there is no effect for food in satiety, nor for water in quenching thirst, growth, and cleanliness, nor for fire in burning or heating or cooking food, nor for clothing or the wall in covering or repelling heat and cold, nor for the tree in shade, nor for the sun and other planets in light, nor for the knife in cutting, nor for water...
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is an extension of the principle of occasionalism: the denial of causal efficacy in created things. The author provides a list of common examples to illustrate that no physical object has intrinsic power to produce its typical effect. Food does not cause satiety, water does not cause quenching or growth, fire does not cause burning, clothing does not cause covering, etc. This reinforces the earlier argument about human action: just as the servant's power has no effect, so too natural objects have no real effect. All effects are directly from Allah. The list is incomplete in the OCR, ending mid-sentence at 'nor for water'. This doctrinal point is a hallmark of Ash‘arī theology, emphasizing divine omnipotence and the constant creation of cause-effect correlations by Allah.

Key Terms
أثر
طعام
نار
سببية
عادات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash‘arīs deny any regular correlation between causes and effects, implying randomness.
Correction: Ash‘arīs affirm regular correlation as Allah's custom (ʿāda), but deny that created things have necessary causal power. The patterns are consistent because Allah creates them consistently.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the relationship between fire and burning?
A) Fire causes burning by its nature
B) Fire has no effect in burning
C) Fire and burning are both created by Allah with no connection
D) Fire is the primary cause of burning

Key Takeaway
Physical objects have no causal efficacy; Allah alone creates all effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel full after a meal, do you thank Allah for creating the satiety directly, or do you unconsciously attribute it to the food? How can you train your heart to see Allah's hand in every effect, even the most ordinary ones?
?
If you truly believed that no created thing—no person, no object—has any independent power, would you still fear people's opinions or rely on material means for security? What would change in your daily reliance on Allah?
?
This passage says the knife has no effect in cutting. When you use a knife, do you consider that Allah is the One actually cutting? How would that awareness transform your sense of dependence on Allah in even the simplest actions?

Distinguishing the True Doctrine from Jabriyya and Qadariyya
Sanusi distinguishes the true doctrine from Jabriyya and Qadariyya regarding created power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the nature of created power (qudra haditha) distinguishes Ahl al-Haqq from Jabriyya and Qadariyya, and why reward and punishment are conventional signs, not rational consequences.

Core Idea
Created power has no effect—it merely accompanies action.

Sanusi now clarifies a subtle but critical point: the nature of created power in the servant. By defining it as an accident that relates to actions without causing them, he separates the true doctrine from two extreme positions. This passage will help you see why neither pure determinism nor free-will invention is correct.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp Sanusi's overall argument.
Notice how the passage begins by defining created power as an accident.
2
Identify Sanusi's definition of created power
Look for the description of created power (qudra haditha) as 'an accident among accidents' that 'relates to the action even if we see no effect in it at all.' This is the key claim.
This definition is the foundation for refuting both deviant sects.
3
See how this refutes Jabriyya and Qadariyya
Sanusi explicitly separates from Jabriyya (who deny any created power) and Qadariyya (who claim the servant invents actions by this power). Notice the reasoning: power has no effect, so invention is false; but it exists, so total denial is false.
The phrase 'we separate' (انفصلنا) marks the refutation points.
4
Understand the conventional nature of reward and punishment
Sanusi reminds that reward and punishment have no rational cause; they are conventional signs (amarat jal'iyya). This reinforces that actions are not independent causes of divine recompense.
This counters the Qadariyya claim that reward/punishment follow from the servant's own creation of actions.
5
Reflect on why this distinction is subtle
The conclusion states that distinguishing the true doctrine from these two is 'ambiguous to many' (يلتبس على كثير). Consider why this nuance is easily misunderstood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عَرَضُ مِنَ الأَعراضِ ; كالعلم ونحوه ، تتعلَّق بالفعل وإنْ لم نرَ لها تأثيراً فيهِ أصلاً ) .. انفصلنا عن مذهب الجبريَّةِ القائلين بنفي قدرةٍ حادثة في العبدِ مطلقاً وبقولنا : ( ليس لتلك القدرةِ الحادثة تأثير في الفعل عن أصلاً ، وإِنَّما تتعلَّقُ بهِ وتصاحبه فقط ) .. انفصلنا مذهب القدريَّةِ مجوس هذهِ الأمَّةِ القائلينَ بأنَّ تلك القدرة الحادثة في العبدِ بها يخترعُ العبد أفعاله على حسَبِ إرادتِهِ ، قالوا : وبذلك أطاع وعصى ، وعليهِ أُثيب وعُوقبَ ! وقد سبقَ لكَ : أنَّ الثواب والعقاب لا سبب لهما عقلاً عند أهل الحقِّ ، وإنَّما الطاعات والمعاصي أمارات جَعْليَّةٌ ، لا علل عقليَّةٌ فتحقق بهذا : تمييز المذهب الحقِّ عن المذهبينِ الفاسدين ؛ وهما مذهبا الجبريَّةِ والقدريَّةِ ؛ فإنَّ تمييزه عنهما مما يلتبس على كثيرٍ
It is an accident among accidents, like knowledge and the like, related to the action even if we see no effect in it at all. So we separate from the doctrine of the Jabriyya who assert the absolute negation of a created power in the servant. And by our saying: 'That created power has no effect on the action at all, but only relates to it and accompanies it' we separate from the doctrine of the Qadariyya, the Magians of this nation, who assert that by that created power in the servant he invents his actions according to his will. They said: By that he obeyed and disobeyed, and upon that he is rewarded and punished! And you have already learned that reward and punishment have no rational cause according to the people of truth, but rather acts of obedience and disobedience are conventional signs (amarat jal'iyya), not rational causes. So it is ascertained by this: the distinction of the true doctrine from the two corrupt doctrines; they are the doctrines of Jabriyya and Qadariyya; indeed distinguishing it from them is something ambiguous to many.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of created power (qudra haditha) and uses it to distinguish the Ahl al-Haqq from two deviant sects. The author first states that created power is an accident that relates to action but has no causal effect. This refutes the Jabriyya, who deny any created power in the servant, and the Qadariyya, who claim that the servant invents his own actions by this power. The author then clarifies that reward and punishment are not rational consequences of actions but conventional signs (amarat jal'iyya) established by Allah. The conclusion emphasizes that this distinction is subtle and often confusing, but crucial for correct doctrine.

قدرية
ثواب وعقاب

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Created power has a real effect on actions, or reward/punishment are natural consequences of actions.
Correction: Created power has no effect; it only relates and accompanies. Reward and punishment are conventional signs (amarat jal'iyya), not rational consequences.

Denial of Causal Efficacy in Natural Objects
Sanusi enumerates examples denying causal effect of natural objects and affirms Allah's direct action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to Ash'ari theology, no created object has any causal effect, and that all regularities are Allah's direct creation.

Core Idea
No created thing has causal power — Allah alone is the true agent.

Sanusi presents a series of everyday examples to illustrate a foundational Ash'ari doctrine: all causal relationships we observe are merely habitual conjunctions, not genuine causation. In this passage, he systematically denies causal efficacy to food, water, fire, clothing, trees, sun, knife, and even cold water. Then he reveals the underlying principle: all action belongs to Allah directly, without mediation from natural properties or powers.

Guided Path
1
Read the examples carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, noting each example. Then read the translation to grasp the pattern of denial.
The list is extensive – it covers many common objects.
2
Identify the pattern
Each example pairs an object (e.g., food, fire) with a typical effect (satiety, burning). Sanusi denies that the object has any 'effect' (أثر) on that outcome.
3
Understand the denial
The denial is absolute: the object does not cause the effect in any way – not by nature, not by a power placed in it. The effect occurs only 'at the time' of the object, by Allah's direct action.
This is the core of occasionalism.
4
Grasp the principle
After the examples, Sanusi states the general rule: all such regularities are Allah's custom (عادة). You must analogize (قس) every similar case to these examples.
5
Note the refutation
The passage ends by refuting the ignorant belief that objects have inherent powers or properties. This is a direct challenge to common sense and naturalism.
Reflect on how this doctrine impacts your view of everyday causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا لا أثر للطعام في الشبع ، ولا للماء في الرّي والنبات والنظافة ، ولا للنار في الإحراق أو التسخين أو نضْحِ الطعامِ ، ولا للثوبِ أوِ الجدارِ في السَّتْرِ أو دفع الحرّ والبرد ، ولا للشجرةِ في الظل ، ولا للشمس وسائرِ الكواكب في الضوء ، ولا للسكين في القطع ، ولا للماء البارد في كسْرِ قوَّةِ حرارة ماءٍ آخر ، كما لا أثر لذلك الآخر في كَسْرِ قوَّةِ بردِهِ ، وقس على هذا كل ما أجرى الله تعالى عادته أنْ يُوجِدَ عندَهُ شيئاً ولتعلم أنَّهُ مِنَ الله تعالى بدءاً ، بلا واسطة ولا أثر فيه لتلك الأشياء المقارنة ، لا بطبعها ، ولا بقوَّةٍ أو خاصة جعلها الله تعالى فيها كما يعتقده كثيرٌ مِنَ الجهلةِ .
Likewise, food has no effect on satiety, nor water on quenching thirst, plant growth, and cleanliness, nor fire on burning, heating, or cooking food, nor the garment or wall on covering or repelling heat and cold, nor the tree on shade, nor the sun and other stars on light, nor the knife on cutting, nor cold water on breaking the heat of another water, just as that other has no effect on breaking its cold. And analogize to this everything that Allah Almighty has made habit to bring into existence at the time of it. And know that it is from Allah Almighty from the beginning, without mediation and no effect in it for those accompanying things, neither by their nature nor by a power or property that Allah Almighty placed in them, as many ignorant people believe.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage presents a series of concrete examples to illustrate the Ash'ari doctrine of occasionalism: no created object has any causal efficacy. Each example (food, water, fire, knife, etc.) is a case where humans commonly assume causality, but the author asserts that the effect occurs merely at the time of the object, not because of it. The principle is then stated: all such regularities are Allah's custom (ada). The passage concludes by firmly stating that all action originates from Allah without any mediation, and denies both natural properties and any power or property supposedly placed in things by Allah, as the ignorant believe.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many people believe that fire burns by its own nature, or that food causes satiety by a power placed in it by Allah.
Correction: Fire has no effect on burning; Allah creates burning at the time of fire. Similarly, food does not cause satiety; Allah creates satiety at the time of eating. No created object has any causal power whatsoever.

Checkpoint

B) Fire can cause burning only if Allah gives it power.
C) Fire has no effect on burning; Allah creates burning at the time of fire.
D) Fire and burning are the same thing.

Key Takeaway
All created objects are devoid of causal power; every effect is directly from Allah alone.

Ruling on Belief in Natural Causality and Placed Power
Sanusi states consensus on kufr of belief in natural causality and disagreement on placed power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic theological ruling on natural causality and the controversy over belief in placed powers.

Core Idea
Belief in natural causality is kufr by consensus; placed power is disputed.

This short but crucial passage from Sanusi addresses a key point of Islamic theology: how to properly attribute causality. The passage clarifies that ascribing effect to the nature of created things is unanimously considered disbelief, but there is a scholarly disagreement on whether belief in a power placed by Allah (which He could remove) is also disbelief. Understanding this distinction is essential for a correct understanding of tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the meaning.
Pay attention to the terms 'طبع' (nature) and 'قوة' (power).
2
Identify the two beliefs
The passage mentions two beliefs: that things affect by their own nature, and that they affect by a power or property placed by Allah. Note the different rulings on each.
The first is unanimously kufr; the second is disputed.
3
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider why attributing effect to nature is kufr, and why the placed power case is debatable. This ties to the attributes of Allah (wajib and mustahil).
The conclusion links back to what is necessary and impossible for Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد ذكر غيرُ واحدٍ مِنْ مُحققي الأئمة الاتفاق على كفْرِ مَن اعتقد تأثير تلك الأشياء بطبعها ، والخلافُ في كُفْرِ اعتقد أنَّ تأثيرها بقوَّةٍ أو خاصَّةٍ جعلَها الله تعالى فيها ، وإِنْ نزعها لم تُؤثر فقد عرفت بهذهِ الجُمَلِ ما يجب في حقه تعالى وما يستحيل .
And more than one of the verifying imams has mentioned the consensus on the disbelief of whoever believes that those things have an effect by their nature, and the disagreement regarding the disbelief of whoever believes that their effect is by a power or property that Allah Almighty placed in them, even if He removes it they do not effect. So you have come to know by these sentences what is necessary (wajib) with respect to Him and what is impossible.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage provides a theological juristic ruling. It reports a consensus among verifying imams that believing created things have effect by their own nature constitutes disbelief (kufr). However, there is disagreement on whether believing that they have effect by a power or property placed by Allah (which He could remove) is also disbelief. The passage ends by linking this discussion to the broader theological framework of what is necessary (wajib) and impossible (mustahil) for Allah, implying that correct belief in tawhid requires denying any form of causal independence to creation.

Correction: The passage shows that there is disagreement among scholars on this point; it is not unanimously considered kufr.

The Ruling on Belief in Causal Efficacy: Nature vs. Divinely Placed Property
Sanusi states the ruling on belief in causal efficacy and the disagreement regarding efficacy by a property placed by God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between belief in causal efficacy by nature (which is kufr by consensus) and belief in efficacy by a property placed by God (which is subject to scholarly disagreement).

Core Idea
Believing that created things cause effects by themselves is disbelief, but there is debate when a divinely placed property is involved.

Sanusi addresses a critical theological issue: the relationship between Allah's action and apparent causal interactions in the world. After illustrating with the example of cold and heat, he states the correct doctrine that all effects come directly from Allah. He then reports the scholarly stance—consensus on the disbelief of natural causation, but disagreement when a 'property' (khāṣṣa) is invoked as the cause. This lesson clarifies the ruling and its implications for Islamic belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage with the literal translation. Notice the example of cold and heat and how it sets up the argument.
2
Identify the correct belief
Find the sentence stating that everything is from Allah directly, without any effect from accompanying things—neither by nature nor by a power or property. This is the correct Sunni position.
3
Distinguish the two types of causal belief
Locate where Sanusi reports the consensus on disbelief for belief in natural causation, and then the disagreement regarding belief in efficacy by a property placed by God.
Note the nuance: some scholars do not consider the latter as disbelief because the efficacy is still attributed to God's placement.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider how this ruling safeguards divine transcendence and upholds tawhid. Ask yourself: Why does the disagreement exist? What does it mean to believe that a property placed by God has effect?
The key is that God is the sole creator and sustainer; any belief that implies an independent causal agent is problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البارد في كسْرِ قوَّةِ حرارة ماءٍ آخر ، كما لا أثر لذلك الآخر في كَسْرِ قوَّةِ بردِهِ ، وقس على هذا كل ما أجرى الله تعالى عادتهُ أنْ يُوجِدَ عندَهُ شيئاً
ولتعلم أنَّهُ مِنَ الله تعالى بدءاً ، بلا واسطة ولا أثر فيه لتلك الأشياء المقارنة ، لا بطبعها ، ولا بقوَّةٍ أو خاصة جعلها الله تعالى فيها كما يعتقده كثيرٌ مِنَ الجهلة .

وقد ذكر غيرُ واحدٍ مِنْ مُحققي الأئمة الاتفاق على كفْرِ مَن اعتقد تأثير تلك الأشياء بطبعها ، والخلافُ في كُفْرِ اعتقد أنَّ تأثيرها بقوَّةٍ أو خاصَّةٍ جعلَها الله تعالى فيها ، وإن نزعها لم تُؤثر

The cold in breaking the power of heat of another water, just as that other has no effect in breaking the power of its cold. And more than one of the verifying imams have mentioned the consensus on disbelief of whoever believes in the influence of those things by their nature, and the disagreement concerning the disbelief of one who believes that their influence is by a power or property that God Almighty placed in them, and if He removed it, they would not affect. So by these sentences you have known what is necessary for Him Almighty and what is impossible.

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling on the theological status of belief in causal efficacy. Sanusi first gives an example: cold water breaking the power of heat in another water, and vice versa, to illustrate that there is no intrinsic causal effect between created things. He then states the correct doctrine: whatever habitual occurrence God brings about, it is from Him directly, without any effect from the accompanying objects—neither by their nature nor by any power or property placed in them, contrary to the ignorant. Next, he reports the scholarly consensus: belief that things affect by nature is kufr (disbelief). However, there is disagreement regarding whether belief that things affect by a power or property that God placed in them is also kufr; if God were to remove that power, the thing would have no effect. The passage concludes that from these statements one learns what is necessary for God (wajib) and what is impossible (mustahil), presumably setting the stage for the discussion of the possible (ja'iz) on the next page.

Correction: Sanusi clarifies that any belief in causal efficacy independent of God is problematic; natural causation is outright disbelief, while efficacy via a divinely placed property is a matter of scholarly disagreement—still opposed to the correct view that God is the sole creator of all effects.

Key Takeaway
Belief that created things cause effects by their own nature is kufr by consensus, while belief that they cause effects by a property placed by God is a point of scholarly debate; in either case, all actual causation is from Allah alone.

Philosophers' Claim and the Promise of Refutation
Philosophers claim reasoning alone is independent cause; refutation is promised

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' position that reasoning alone is an independent cause of knowledge, and how it contrasts with the Ash'ari and Mu'tazili views.

Core Idea
Reasoning alone cannot create knowledge; only Allah is the true cause.

In this passage, Imam Sanusi presents the view of the philosophers (falasifa) who assert that human reasoning can produce knowledge independently. He then reminds us that both this view and that of the Mu'tazila will be refuted based on the principle that all beings must be directly attributed to Allah. The full proof is promised later, but the foundation is laid here.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' claim
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note that the philosophers claim reasoning alone is an independent cause.
The term 'illa mustaqilla' means independent cause.
2
Identify the contrast
Contrast this view with the Ash'ari (reasoning as condition) and Mu'tazili (human power involved) positions discussed earlier.
The philosophers go further than both by making reasoning fully causal.
3
Understand the refutation promise
The author says the refutation is based on what has been established and will come later. Look for the key principle mentioned: attributing all beings to Allah without mediation.
4
Reflect on the key principle
Consider why the necessity of attributing all beings directly to Allah refutes the idea that anything else, like reasoning, can be an independent cause.
This principle will be proven in later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالَتِ الفلاسفةُ : النظرُ وحدَهُ علَّةٌ مُستقِلَّةٌ لوجودِ العلم والردُّ على هذين المذهبين بما تقرَّرَ ، وسيأتي برهانه مِنْ وجوب إسنادِ الكائناتِ كلَّها إلى الله تعالى ابتداءً بلا واسطة
And the philosophers said: Reasoning alone is an independent cause for the existence of knowledge. And the refutation of these two schools is by what has been established, and its proof will come from the necessity of attributing all beings entirely to Allah, may He be exalted, originally without mediation.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This short passage presents the view of the philosophers (falasifa) that reasoning (nazar) by itself is an independent cause (illa mustaqilla) for the production of knowledge. This contrasts with both the Ash'ari position (which sees a rational connection but not independent causation) and the Mu'tazili position (which involves human power). The author then states that the refutation of both the Mu'tazila and the philosophers is based on what has already been established, and its proof will come later from the principle that all created beings must be directly attributed to Allah without any intervening cause.

Key Terms
فلاسفة (philosophers)
علة مستقلة (independent cause)
إسناد (attribution)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage contains the full refutation of the philosophers.
Correction: The passage only presents the philosophers' claim and promises a refutation that will come later.

Checkpoint
What do the philosophers claim about reasoning?
A) It is a condition for knowledge
B) It is an independent cause of knowledge
C) It is generated by human power
D) It never leads to knowledge

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' claim that reasoning alone causes knowledge is denied by the fact that all beings must be directly attributed to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever rely on your own reasoning as if it were an independent source of certainty, forgetting that even your ability to think is created and sustained by Allah? How can you actively attribute your understanding back to Him today?
?
When you study or learn, do you feel a sense of ownership over knowledge, or do you recognize that knowledge itself is a gift from Allah? What would change in your approach if you truly believed that every insight comes directly from Him without mediation?
?
Are there areas in your life where you act as if you are an independent cause—your career, relationships, or personal growth—ignoring that all beings are directly attributed to Allah? What is one concrete step you can take this week to live out the belief that Allah alone is the true cause of all things?

The Impossibility of an Infinite Past
Sanusi proves that an infinite regress of temporal events is impossible via a contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's logical proof that an infinite series of originated events is impossible because it leads to a contradiction.

Core Idea
An infinite regress of events collapses into self-contradiction.

Sanusi now tackles a crucial question: could the chain of temporal events stretch back forever without a beginning? At first glance, an infinite past might seem plausible, but reason demands otherwise. In this lesson, we will walk through his neat proof that an infinite series of originated events leads to a logical impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify the key elements: the premise about events being originated, the concept of pre-eternal non-existence, and the two branching possibilities.
Notice the two 'either-or' branches – they form the core of the argument.
2
Understand the first branch (coexistence)
The first possibility: an event coexists with pre-eternal non-existence. Follow why that would force existence and non-existence together – a direct contradiction.
3
Understand the second branch (no coexistence)
The second possibility: no event coexists with pre-eternal non-existence. See why this implies that events have a beginning (the first event appeared after pre-eternity).
4
Connect to the impossibility of infinite regress
Both branches lead to a denial of an infinite series: either a contradiction arises, or a first event is required. Conclude that an infinite series of events is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السابع - وهو إثباتُ استحالة حوادث لا أوَّلَ لها - فله أدلة كثيرة ، ذكرناها في « عقيدتنا الكبرى » و « شرحها » (١) ، وأقربُ الأدلَّةِ أن نقول : إذا كانَ كلَّ فردٍ مِنْ أفرادِ الحوادثِ حادثاً في نفسِهِ .. فعدمُ جميعها ثابت في الأزلِ ، ثم لا يخلو : إمَّا أَنْ يقارنَ ذلكَ العدمَ فردٌ مِنَ الأفراد الحادثة أو لا ؛ فإن قارنَهُ لزم اجتماع وجودِ الشيء وعدمِهِ ؛ إِذْ ذلكَ الفردُ مِنْ جملةِ الأفرادِ التي تقرَّرَ عدمُها في الأزلِ ، واجتماع وجودِ الشيء وعدمِهِ محال بضرورة العقل ، وإنْ لم يقارن ذلكَ العدمَ شيءٌ مِنْ تلكَ الأفرادِ الحادثة.. لزمَ أنَّ لها أَوَّلاً ؛ لخلو الأزلِ على هذا الفَرْضِ عن جميعها .
And as for the seventh—which is the proof of the impossibility of events (ḥawādith) having no beginning—there are many proofs for it, which we have mentioned in our 'al-ʿAqīda al-Kubrā' and its 'Sharḥ' (1). The nearest of proofs is to say: if every individual of the individuals of events is originated in itself, then the non-existence of all of them is established in pre-eternity. Then it is inevitable: either an individual from the originated individuals coexists with that non-existence, or not. If it coexists, then the conjunction of the existence of a thing and its non-existence would follow; because that individual is among the individuals whose non-existence was determined in pre-eternity; and the conjunction of the existence of a thing and its non-existence is impossible by necessity of reason. And if nothing from those originated individuals coexists with that non-existence, then they must have a beginning; because pre-eternity is empty of all of them on this assumption.
Source pages p. 212

Commentary
This passage provides a proof that there cannot be an infinite regress of temporal events (ḥawādith). Sanusi first notes that there are many proofs, but he gives the nearest one. The argument: if every event is originated (ḥādith), then before all events existed (in pre-eternity, al-azal), their non-existence was established. Now, either some event coexisted with that pre-eternal non-existence or not. If an event coexisted, then that event would both exist and not exist at the same time (since its non-existence was fixed in pre-eternity), which is a contradiction. If no event coexisted, then pre-eternity was empty of all events, meaning that events began after pre-eternity, so they have a first event. Thus, an infinite series is impossible.

Key Terms
حوادث
لا أول لها
ازل
عدم
اجتماع وجود وعدم
بديهة عقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An infinite series of past events is rationally possible without contradiction.
Correction: Sanusi shows that if the series had no beginning, then either an event coexists with its own non-existence (contradiction) or no event exists in pre-eternity (implying a first event). Both outcomes refute the infinite series.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi prove about events that have no beginning?
A) They are possible
B) They would require an infinite regress of causes
C) They are impossible because of a contradiction
D) They exist in the mind only

Key Takeaway
An infinite series of temporal events is impossible because it would force a logical contradiction or imply a beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your own existence is a series of originated moments; if those moments cannot go back infinitely, then you are ultimately dependent on a First Cause. How does that change the way you view the urgency of your relationship with that Cause—especially if you have been living as if your life is self-sufficient?
?
The proof shows that it is impossible for events to have no beginning. What does that imply about the purpose of your life? If your existence was not accidental but brought into being by an eternal Creator, what specific goal should you pursue starting today?
?
You may have assumed that time stretches back forever, but Sanusi forces you to confront that every moment is contingent. Is there an area of your life where you act as though your habits or sins are just 'part of an endless cycle'? What would it mean to treat this moment as a new beginning with real accountability?

Three Divisions of the Efficient Cause
Sanusi enumerates the three divisions of the efficient cause and denies causal efficacy to creatures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three divisions of the efficient cause according to Sanusi and why only the voluntary agent (God) has true causal efficacy.

Core Idea
Only God is the true agent; creatures have no genuine causal power.

Sanusi now delves into the nature of causality, classifying the efficient cause into three types as understood by rational analysis. This passage explains each type and then presents the Ash'ari position that denies real causal efficacy to anything other than God. Understanding this classification is essential for grasping the Islamic theological stance on divine omnipotence and creaturely dependence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi first gives examples of the non-volitional causes (al-ʿillah and aṭ-ṭabīʿah) before listing the three divisions.
The examples help clarify each type.
2
Identify the three divisions
Identify the three types of agent (fāʿil) as listed: qādir (mukhtār), ʿillah, and ṭabīʿah. Understand the defining characteristics of each.
The first is voluntary, the second necessary, the third conditional.
3
Contrast the positions
Contrast the view of the atheist philosophers (who affirm all three) with the People of Truth (who deny causal effect to the last two). Note that even the first division (voluntary agent) truly applies only to God.
This is a key Ash'ari occasionalist doctrine.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Reflect on the final sentence: true agency is impossible for creatures. This reinforces tawḥīd in actions (tawḥīd al-afʿāl).
Ask yourself: Why does Sanusi say 'in general and in detail'?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العلَّة دونَ معلولها ؛ كحركة الإصبع بالنسبة إلى حركة الخاتم المجعولة فيه مثلاً وأما الطبيعة فقد يتوقَّفُ تأثيرها على شرط ونفي مانع ؛ كتأثير النارِ في الإحراق عندَهم ؛ فإِنَّهُ يتوقَّفُ على شرط ؛ وهو مماسة النارِ عندهم للشيء المحترق ، ونفي مانع ؛ وهو عدم بلل ذلك الشيء المحترق . فصارَتْ أقسامُ الفاعل بحسَبِ تقديرِ العقل ثلاثة : قادرٌ ويُسمَّى مختاراً ؛ وهو الذي تقدَّم ، وعلةٌ ، وطبيعة ، وكلُّها موجودة عند ملحدة الفلاسفة أهلكَهمُ الله تعالى ، ومذهب أهل الحق قاطبةً : بطلان تأثير القسمين الأخيرين ، وإنَّما الموجودُ القسم الأول ، ثم لا يصدق حقيقةً إلا على مولانا جل وعزَّ ؛ لاستحالةِ أنْ يكونَ لكلِّ ما سواه تعالى جملةً وتفصيلاً تأثير في أثر ما
The cause is [effective] without its effect, like the movement of the finger relative to the movement of the ring placed on it, for example. As for nature, its effect may depend on a condition and the absence of an obstacle, like the effect of fire in burning, according to them; for it depends on a condition, which is the fire's contact with the combustible thing, and the absence of an obstacle, which is the dryness of that combustible thing (i.e., not being wet). So the divisions of the agent according to the estimation of the intellect became three: an able [agent] (qādir), also called a voluntary agent (mukhtār), which is the one that preceded; a cause (ʿillah); and a nature (ṭabīʿah). All of these exist, according to the atheist philosophers (may Allah destroy them). The doctrine of the People of Truth, all of them, is the invalidity of the effect of the last two divisions, and only the first division exists. Then it is not truly applicable except to our Lord, Exalted and Glorified, because it is impossible that everything other than Him, in general and in detail, has any effect in any effect whatsoever.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage classifies the efficient cause (al-fāʿil) into three types according to intellectual analysis. First, it explains two non-volitional types: the necessary cause (al-ʿillah), which produces its effect without condition (e.g., the ring moves inevitably when the finger moves), and nature (aṭ-ṭabīʿah), which requires a condition and the absence of an obstacle (e.g., fire burns only upon contact and if the object is dry). Then it states that the intellect divides the agent into three: al-qādir (the able/voluntary agent, also called al-mukhtār), al-ʿillah (the necessary cause), and aṭ-ṭabīʿah (nature). The atheist philosophers affirm all three, but the People of Truth (Ahl al-Ḥaqq) hold that the last two have no real causal effect whatsoever—only the first division truly exists as an agent. Moreover, this true agency applies absolutely only to God, because it is impossible for any creature to have any causal influence, whether in general or in detail. The passage thus establishes a strict occasionalist position: God alone is the true agent.

Key Terms
قادر
مختار
علة
طبيعة
فاعل
تأثير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The world operates through inherent causal powers in creatures independent of God.
Correction: Sanusi affirms that creatures have no real causal influence; all effect is from God alone.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, which of the three divisions of the agent is considered to have real causal efficacy?
A) al-qādir (the able agent)
B) al-ʿillah (the cause)
C) aṭ-ṭabīʿah (nature)
D) both al-ʿillah and aṭ-ṭabīʿah

Key Takeaway
Only God is the true efficient cause; creatures possess no intrinsic causal power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a fire burn or a medicine heal, do you instinctively attribute the effect to the fire or medicine, or do you immediately recognize God as the sole agent? What would change in your heart if you consistently directed your gratitude for every outcome directly to Him?
?
Is there an area of your life where you still rely on 'secondary causes'—like your own effort, wealth, or relationships—as if they have independent power? How would you live differently today if you truly believed that only God has any causal influence?
?
If you truly internalized that no creature, not even you, has any real effect, would your sense of responsibility and dependence on God deepen? What would it look like to repent for attributing power to anything other than Him?

Refuting Cause and Nature Claims about God's Essence
Sanusi refutes the view that God's essence alone suffices as a cause or nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi argues that if God's essence were a cause or natural agent, the world would be eternal—a conclusion that contradicts its known temporal origination.

Core Idea
If God's essence were a cause, the world would be eternal—but it is not.

Sanusi now addresses a pair of objections that try to explain the world's existence without divine will. Some suggest God's essence itself suffices as a cause or that He acts by nature. Both views, if true, would force the world to be co-eternal with God. In this passage, Sanusi shows that these views are incompatible with the world's temporal origination, which has already been established.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the conditional structure: if X then Y.
2
Identify the two rejected views
The passage mentions two possibilities: essence as cause (ʿilla) and essence as natural agent (ṭabʿ). Mark them.
3
Understand the consequence
Both views lead to the world being pre-eternal because cause/effect and nature/product are inseparable.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Connect to known truth
The opponent already accepts the temporal origination of the world. So the premises are false.
This is key: the argument uses what the opponent admits.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإِنْ قُدِّرَتْ ذاتُهُ علَّةً لوجودِ العالم ، أو مُوجِدةً بالطبع ؛ حتى لا يحتاج في وجودِ العالم عنه إلى إرادة . . لزم حينئذٍ قدم العالم ؛ لوجوب اقتران العلة بمعلولها والطبيعة بمطبوعها ، وقد عرفت وجوب حدوثِهِ .
If His essence were supposed to be a cause for the existence of the world, or an agent by nature, such that the world's existence from Him does not require will, then the pre-eternity of the world would follow necessarily, because the cause must be concomitant with its effect, and nature with its natural product, and you have already known that the world's temporal origination is necessary.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage is a concise refutation of two alternative explanations for the origin of the world: that God's essence is a cause (ʿilla) or that He acts by nature (bi-l-ṭabʿ). These two views are proposed as alternatives that would dispense with the need for will. Sanusi shows that neither works. If God's essence were a cause, then the cause and its effect must be concomitant—since a cause necessitates its effect eternally, the world would be eternal. Similarly, if God acted by nature, the natural agent and its product are inseparable, also implying pre-eternity. But the opponent already admits that the world is temporally originated (ḥādith). Since the consequence contradicts this known fact, the premises (that the essence is a cause or nature) are false. Thus, the only remaining option is that God acts by will, which allows temporal origination. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.

Key Terms
علَّة (ʿilla)
طبع (ṭabʿ)
قدم العالم (qidam al-ʿālam)
حدوث (ḥudūth)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God's essence is a cause, the world is created without will and is not necessarily eternal.
Correction: A cause necessitates its effect eternally, so if God's essence were a cause, the world would be co-eternal with God.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of saying God's essence is a cause for the world?
A) The world would be temporally originated.
B) The world would be eternal.
C) God would need will to create.
D) The world would be impossible.

Key Takeaway
God's essence cannot be a cause or natural agent for the world because that would imply the world is eternal, contradicting its temporal origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever treat God's actions as automatic or predictable, as if He is a cause without personal will? How does this lesson challenge you to see His will in every moment of creation?
?
If you truly believe the world came into being by God's free will, why do you still live as if the world runs on fixed causes that don't need His constant involvement? What would change if you really lived in that awareness today?

Refutation of Creation by Nature or Cause
Sanusi refutes the view that God creates by nature or cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing creation to nature or cause would imply the world's eternity, and thus why God must be an agent by choice.

Core Idea
If God were a cause or nature, the world would be eternal—so He must be an agent by choice.

This passage tackles a crucial objection: why can't God create by nature or as a cause without will? Sanusi shows that such a view leads to the impossible conclusion that the world is eternal. By refuting this, he reaffirms that God is a free agent, whose will is essential to creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the objector challenges the need for divine will, suggesting that God could create by nature or as a cause.
Notice the objector's logic: if God is an agent by choice, will is needed; but why can't He be a cause or nature?
2
Identify Sanusi's answer
Read Sanusi's response: if God were a cause or nature, the world would be eternal. This is the key insight.
The answer is given in the second part, starting with 'والجواب'.
3
Understand the reasoning
Understand why cause/effect and nature/natural product must coexist. This is the core logical link.
If a cause exists, its effect must exist simultaneously; otherwise it's not an efficient cause.
4
Connect to the world's temporality
Recall that the world has been proven temporal. Since the supposition leads to eternity, it is false.
This contradiction shows God must be an agent by choice (fāʿil mukhtār).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإِنْ قُدِّرَتْ ذاتُهُ علَّةً لوجودِ العالم، أو مُوجِدةً بالطبع؛ حتى لا يحتاج في وجودِ العالم عنه إلى إرادة. . لزم حينئذٍ قدم العالم؛ لوجوب اقتران العلة بمعلولها والطبيعة بمطبوعها، وقد عرفت وجوب حدوثِهِ. له

هذا اعتراض على ما ذكرَ مِنْ وجوبِ اتصافِ مُوجِدِ العالم بالإرادة. وتقريرُ الاعتراض أنْ يُقالَ: لا تُسلَّمُ أَنَّ مُوجِدَ العالم إِنَّما يُربِّحُ الممكن ببعض الجائزات بالإرادةِ؟! لأنَّ ذلكَ إِنَّما يلزمُ أنْ لو كانَ فاعلاً بالاختيار، ولِمَ لا يجوز أنْ يكونَ مُرجحاً لذلك بطبعِهِ أو بذاتِهِ؛ بأنْ يكونَ علَّةً لوجودِ ما وُجِدَ مِنَ الممكناتِ على ما قدَّمنا لكَ مِنَ الفرق بين العلَّةِ والطبيعة

والجواب عن هذا الاعتراض: ما أشار إليه في العقيدةِ مِنْ أنَّ هذا التقدير يلزمُ عليهِ قدمُ العالمِ؛ لأنَّهُ إذا كان وجوده مستنداً لعلَّةٍ أو طبيعة لزمَ أنْ تكونَ تلكَ العلَّةُ أو الطبيعةُ قديمةً؛ لئلا يلزم الدَّورُ أَوِ التسلسل، على ما عرفت في وجوب قدمِ مُوجِدِ العالم، والمعلولُ أَوِ المطبوع يستحيلُ أنْ يتأخَرَ وجودُهُ عن وجودِ العلَّةِ أوِ الطبيعة، فوجبَ قدم العالم، كيف وقد عرفت بالبرهان القطعي وجوب حدوثِهِ؟! فتعيَّنَ: أنَّ صانع العالمِ فاعل مختار
If His essence were supposed as a cause for the existence of the world, or an originator by nature, so that the existence of the world from Him does not need will, then necessarily the world would be eternal, because of the necessity of the conjunction of the cause with its effect and nature with its natural product, and you have known the necessity of its temporality. This is an objection to what was mentioned of the necessity of the creator of the world being described with will. The formulation of the objection is to say: It is not conceded that the creator of the world only gives preponderance to one of the possibles by will?! Because that would only be necessary if He were an agent by choice, and why is it not permissible that He gives preponderance by His nature or by His essence, so that He is a cause for the existence of what exists among the possibles, based on what we have presented to you of the difference between cause and nature? The answer to this objection is what was indicated in the Creed: that this supposition necessitates the eternity of the world; because if its existence is based on a cause or nature, then that cause or nature must be eternal, to avoid circularity or infinite regress, as you have known regarding the necessity of the eternity of the creator of the world, and it is impossible for the effect or the natural product to be delayed in existence from the existence of the cause or nature, so the eternity of the world is necessary, how can that be when you have known by definitive proof the necessity of its temporality? So it is determined that the maker of the world is an agent by choice.
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer regarding the necessity of divine will. The objector says: Why must the Creator need will? Perhaps He gives preponderance by nature or essence. The answer, from the Creed, is that such a supposition would make the world eternal. If God were a cause or nature, the world would always coexist with Him, because cause and effect are simultaneous. Since we have proven the world is temporal, this is impossible. Therefore, God must be an agent by choice (fāʿil mukhtār).

Key Terms
العلة
الطبع
قدم العالم
حدوث العالم
الإرادة
الفاعل المختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God can be a cause or nature for the world without will and still be consistent with divine transcendence.
Correction: This belief would imply the world is eternal, which is false. God must be an agent by choice (fāʿil mukhtār).

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi argue would follow if God were a cause or nature for the world?
A) The world would be temporal.
B) The world would be eternal.
C) God would need will.
D) The world would not exist.

Key Takeaway
God must be an agent by choice because any other mode of creation would entail the world's eternity, which is false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Can you accept that every situation He brings is deliberately chosen for you, not forced by a blind nature? What would it mean for you to thank Him for that choice even in hardship?

beginner
4 min
Objection of an Eternal Obstacle
An objection suggesting an eternal obstacle prevented the world from existing in eternity with a nature-god.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that an eternal obstacle could prevent the world's creation by a nature-like creator, and why this challenges the necessity of a willing agent.

Core Idea
An eternal obstacle would make a nature-god dependent on removal of barriers.

In this passage, Sanusi presents an objection to the argument that the world must have a creator who acts by will. The objector suggests that the creator could be a nature, like fire, and the world only appears later because an eternal obstacle was removed. This sets up a challenge to the proof from temporality.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Note that the text cuts off mid-sentence.
The objection is incomplete; we only have the first part.
2
Identify the key components
Find the three main parts: the claim that the maker is a nature, the reason for the world's non-existence in eternity (an eternal obstacle), and the eventual removal of the obstacle leading to the world's existence.
3
Consider the logical implications
If the maker is a nature, it would produce its effect necessarily unless an obstacle prevents it. But if the obstacle is eternal, how can it be removed? What does that imply about the nature's power?
Think about the difference between a nature and a willing agent.
4
Anticipate the refutation
Since the answer is missing, try to predict how Sanusi might respond. Would he argue that an eternal obstacle contradicts God's perfection?
Reflect on the attributes of God in classical kalam.

First Objection: The Creator as Cause or Nature
Sanusi refutes the objection that the creator might be a cause or nature by showing it implies the world's eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the world's maker must be a free agent and not a mere cause or nature, based on the proof from the eternity of the world.

Core Idea
If the creator were a cause or nature, the world would be eternal, but it is proven originated, so He is a free agent.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a crucial objection: Could the creator be an impersonal cause or nature rather than a free agent? The objection seems plausible, but Sanusi shows it leads to an unacceptable consequence—the eternity of the world. Since we have already proven the world's origination, this objection fails, and the creator's free agency is affirmed.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening question: why must the creator prefer possibilities by will? Could it be by nature or essence? Grasp the objector's suggestion that preponderance might occur without choice.
The objector is not denying the world's order, but its source.
2
Trace Sanusi's answer
Examine the key argument: if the creator were a cause or nature, then that cause/nature must be eternal (to avoid circularity or regress), and its effect cannot be delayed. So the world would be co-eternal.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Connect to the broader proof
Note that the world's origination has already been decisively proven. Therefore the supposition of a cause/nature creator is false. Conclude that the creator is a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār).
This reinforces the earlier proof for the world's origination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الممكن ببعض الجائزات بالإرادةِ ؟! لأنَّ ذلكَ إِنَّما يلزمُ أنْ لو كانَ فاعلاً بالاختيار ، ولِمَ لا يجوز أنْ يكونَ مُرجحاً لذلك بطبعِهِ أو بذاتِهِ ؛ بأنْ يكونَ علَّةً لوجودِ ما وُجِدَ مِنَ الممكناتِ على ما قدَّمنا لكَ مِنَ الفرق بين العلَّةِ والطبيعة. والجواب عن هذا الاعتراض : ما أشار إليه في العقيدةِ مِنْ أنَّ هذا التقدير يلزمُ عليهِ قدمُ العالمِ؛ لأنَّهُ إذا كان وجوده مستنداً لعلَّةٍ أو طبيعة لزمَ أنْ تكونَ تلكَ العلَّةُ أو الطبيعةُ قديمةً؛ لئلا يلزم الدَّورُ أَوِ التسلسل ، على ما عرفت في وجوب قدم مُوجِدِ العالم ، والمعلولُ أَوِ المطبوع يستحيلُ أنْ يتأخَرَ وجودُهُ عن وجودِ العلَّةِ أوِ الطبيعة ، فوجب قدم العالم. كيف وقد عرفت بالبرهان القطعي وجوب حدوثِهِ ؟! فتعيَّنَ : أنَّ صانع العالمِ فاعل مختار.
Is it possible that he prefers some possibilities by will? Because that necessarily follows only if he were an agent by choice. So why is it not possible that he be a preponderator for that by his nature or by his essence; such that he is a cause for the existence of what exists among the possible things, according to what we have presented to you of the difference between cause and nature? And the answer to this objection is what was indicated in al-'Aqida: that this supposition entails the eternity of the world; because if its existence is based on a cause or nature, then that cause or nature must be eternal, so as to avoid circularity or infinite regress, as you have known regarding the necessity of the eternity of the world's originator, and the effect or the thing subjected to nature is impossible for its existence to be delayed from the existence of the cause or nature. So the eternity of the world would be necessary. How is that possible when you have known by decisive proof the necessity of its origination? So it is determined that the world's maker is a free agent.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage presents a first objection and its answer. The objector questions whether the world's creator must be a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār), suggesting instead that the world's order could be due to a cause (ʿilla) or nature (ṭabīʿa). The objector argues that preferring one possibility over another does not require will; it could be by nature. Sanusi responds with a decisive argument: if the world's existence were necessitated by an eternal cause or nature, then that cause or nature would have to be eternal to avoid circularity or infinite regress. Since the effect (maʿlūl) or that which is subject to nature (maṭbūʿ) cannot be delayed from its cause, the world would have to be co-eternal with that cause. But we have already proven by certain demonstration that the world is originated (ḥādith). Therefore, this supposition is false, and the creator must be a free agent acting by choice.

Correction: Sanusi refutes it decisively by showing it entails the eternity of the world, which contradicts the already proven origination of the world.

Key Takeaway
The world's maker must be a free agent because an impersonal cause would make the world eternal, contradicting its proven origination.

The Self-Defeating Eternal Obstacle Objection
Sanusi refutes the second objection by showing its logical inconsistency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why positing an eternal obstacle to explain the delay of creation is logically self-defeating.

Core Idea
An eternal obstacle can never be removed, so it cannot explain a delayed creation.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a second objection: perhaps the world's maker is a nature that was prevented from acting by an eternal obstacle. He refutes this by showing that an eternal obstacle is permanent and cannot cease, so the world would never exist. This reinforces the necessity of a freely acting creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and refutation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the objector proposes an eternal obstacle to save the idea of a nature-based creation.
The objector tries to avoid the earlier argument by adding an external hindrance.
2
Follow the refutation's logic
Sanusi points out that if the obstacle is eternal, it can never cease. Since the world exists now, the obstacle must have ceased, contradicting its eternality.
This is a short reductio ad absurdum.
3
Connect to divine transcendence
Understand why this argument supports the view that the creator is not a nature but a free agent who creates without external constraints.
The lesson reinforces that Allah's actions are not bound by eternal hindrances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاعتراض على هذا؛ بأنَّ صانع العالمِ طبيعةٌ، وإنَّما لم يُوجَدِ العالم معَها في الأزلِ لوجود مانع أزلي منعَ مِنْ وجودِهِ حينئذٍ، فلما انتفى المانع فيما لا يزالُ أوجدَتِ الطبيعةُ حينئذٍ العالم . فاسدٌ؛ لأنَّ هذا التقدير يستلزم ألا يُوجَدَ العالم أبداً؛ لأن مانعَهُ على هذا الفَرْضِ أَزلي . فيستحيل عدمُهُ؛ لما عرفْتَ أنَّ ما ثبت قدمُهُ استحال عدمه.
And the objection to this is: that the world's maker is a nature, and the world did not exist with it in eternity only because of an eternal obstacle that prevented its existence then. So when the obstacle ceases in the future, the nature brings the world into existence then. [This is] invalid; because this supposition entails that the world would never exist; because its obstacle according to this assumption is eternal. So its non-existence is impossible; as you have known that what is proven to be eternal is impossible to cease.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and its refutation. The objector attempts to salvage the idea of the creator as a nature by positing an eternal obstacle (māniʿ azalī) that prevented the world from existing in eternity. When that obstacle is removed in the future, the nature then produces the world. Sanusi refutes this by noting that an eternal obstacle is permanent and cannot cease to exist. As established earlier, anything proven to be eternal is impossible to cease. Therefore, if the obstacle is truly eternal, it can never be removed, and the world would never come into existence. This contradicts the observed fact that the world exists. Thus, the objection fails.

Misconception: That an eternal obstacle can be overcome or removed without contradiction.
Correction: If something is eternal, it cannot cease to exist. Therefore an eternal obstacle can never be removed, making the world's existence impossible.

D) Because the world was created from nothing and not from nature.

Key Takeaway
An eternal obstacle can never be removed, so positing one to delay creation actually prevents creation forever, contradicting the world's existence.
