Third Objection Refuted: The Condition Leads to Infinite Regress
Sanusi refutes the third objection by showing it leads to infinite regress, proving the creator is a free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why positing a missing condition for the world's creation leads to an infinite regress, and how this refutes the idea of a nature-creator.

Core Idea
A missing condition doesn't solve the problem—it only pushes it back infinitely.

In this passage, Sanusi tackles the third objection that the world's delay from its creator is due to a missing condition. He shows that this condition itself is created and requires explanation, leading to an impossible infinite regress. This forces the conclusion that the Creator must be a free choosing agent, not a blind nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the objection: The maker is a nature, and the world's delay is due to a condition that was absent in eternity. When condition appears, world comes into existence.
Notice they attribute creation to nature, not choice.
2
Sanusi's refutation
He argues that the condition itself is originated (hadith) and needs its own explanation. If you suppose an eternal obstacle, condition never exists. If you suppose another condition, infinite regress.
This is a classic infinite regress argument.
3
The conclusion
The only consistent conclusion is that the originator is a willing free agent (murid mukhtar), not a cause or nature.
This is the decisive conclusion of the whole discussion.
4
Reflect on the argument
Why does positing a condition lead to infinite regress? Because every conditional delay requires its own explanation, leading to an endless chain.
This shows the necessity of a free agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا الاعتراض؛ بأنَّ الصانع طبيعةٌ، وتأخُرَ العالم عنها في الأزلِ لتوقف وجودِهِ على شرط لم يُوجَدْ في الأزل، فلمَّا وُجِدَ الشرطُ فيما لا يزالُ وُجِدَ العالم عنِ الطبيعة حينَئِذٍ .. فاسد أيضاً؛ لأنَّ الكلام في حدوثِ ذلكَ الشرطِ وتأخُرِهِ عن الأزلِ كالكلام في العالم، فيحتاج هو أيضاً إلى تقديرِ مانع أزلي، فيلزم ألا يُوجَدَ شرطُ العالم أبداً، فلا يُوجَدَ العالم مشروطه أبداً، أو تقديرِ شرط آخر حادث، فيُنقَلُ الكلام إليهِ، ويلزم التسلسل. فثبت بهذا: أَنَّ مُوجِدَ العالم مريد مختار، لا علة ولا طبيعة.
And likewise the objection: that the maker is a nature, and the world is delayed from it in eternity because its existence depends on a condition that did not exist in eternity; so when the condition exists in the future, the world comes into existence from the nature then. [This] is also invalid; because the discussion about the origination of that condition and its delay from eternity is like the discussion about the world. So it also needs to suppose an eternal obstacle, which entails that the world's condition would never exist, so the world would never exist as a condition of it; or to suppose another originated condition, so the discussion is transferred to it, and infinite regress follows. And it is established by this: that the world's originator is a willing free agent, not a cause nor a nature.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage presents a third objection and its refutation, followed by the overall conclusion. The objector now suggests that the world's delay from its nature-creator is due to the absence of a condition (sharṭ) that was not present in eternity, and when that condition arises, the world appears. Sanusi refutes this by pointing out an infinite regress: the condition itself is originated (ḥādith) and requires its own explanation. One could posit an eternal obstacle to account for the condition's delay, but that would lead to the same problem as before. Alternatively, one could posit another originated condition, leading to an infinite chain (tasalsul), which is impossible. Thus, the only remaining conclusion is that the world's originator is a free agent (murīd mukhtār), not a cause or nature. This final sentence serves as the decisive conclusion of the entire argument against causality and nature.

Key Terms

تسلسل
طبيعة
مريد مختار
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a single condition avoids the problem of the world's origination without further explanation.
Correction: The condition itself must be explained; positing an eternal obstacle or another condition leads to infinite regress.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi show will occur if one tries to explain the world's delay by a condition that was absent in eternity?
A) The condition is eternal and explains everything.
B) The condition itself must be explained, leading to infinite regress.
C) The condition is the world itself.
D) The world would be eternal if the condition is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The world's originator must be a free agent (murid mukhtar), because any other explanation leads to infinite regress.

?
The objector invented a missing condition to avoid acknowledging God's free agency. Where in your own life are you making excuses that just push the real issue further back—like blaming circumstances or waiting for the 'right condition' to repent or change? Will you stop and face the real problem now?

Sanusi proves that the world's originator is a willing free agent. That means He is not bound by any condition—He can act without external cause. How does this change the way you pray to Him? Will you approach Him with the confidence that He is free to answer, not constrained by any chain?

Refutation of the Objection of an Originated Impediment
Sanusi refutes the second objection and concludes the proof for divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the second objection and establishes that the Creator must be a willing agent (muriid mukhtaar), not a cause or nature.

Core Idea
An originated impediment cannot explain why the world began without contradiction.

The second objection attempts to salvage the nature theory by positing an originated impediment that could cease to exist. Sanusi shows this leads to the absurd conclusion that the world would be eternal, and then exposes a deeper logical flaw. The refutation clears the way for the final conclusion: the Creator acts by will and choice.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and refutation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the flow of the argument. Notice the objector's proposal and Sanusi's response.
The objector suggests the impediment itself could be originated (hadith).
2
Trace the logical sequence
Follow the refutation step by step: first, if the impediment is originated, nature in pre-eternity was free of it, so the world would have existed from eternity. Second, an originated impediment requires a cause, but nature is pre-eternal, leading to an infinite regress.
Both steps show the proposal is impossible.
3
Recognize the final conclusion
The refutation leaves only one option: the world's Creator must be a willing agent (muriid mukhtaar) who chooses to create, not a blind cause or nature.
This conclusion applies to both objections, so the proof is complete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: نفرض المانع من وجود العالم حادثاً؛ ليصح عليه العدم. قلنا: فيلزم أن يكون العالم قديماً؛ لتجرد الطبيعة في الأزل من المانع على هذا التقدير. ثم فرض هذا المانع حادثاً مع قدم الطبيعة المؤثرة فيه.. لا يصح. فثبت بهذا: أن موجد العالم مريد مختار، لا علة ولا طبيعة.
For if it is said: We suppose the impediment to the world's existence to be originated, so that non-existence is possible for it. We say: Then it follows that the world would be eternal, because nature in pre-eternity would be free of the impediment according to this supposition. Then supposing this impediment to be originated while the nature that effects it is pre-eternal... is not valid. So it is established by this: that the Bringer into existence of the world is a willing, choosing agent, not a cause and not a nature.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection and its refutation, followed by the overall conclusion. The objector proposes that the impediment itself could be originated (hadith) rather than pre-eternal, so that it can cease. Sanusi refutes this by noting that if the impediment is originated, then before it existed, nature was free of any impediment in pre-eternity, which would mean the world should have existed from eternity—making it eternal, which is impossible for a contingent world. Furthermore, if the impediment is originated, it must have a cause, and since nature is pre-eternal, the impediment would have to come from something else, leading to an infinite regress. Therefore the assumption that the Creator is a nature or mere cause is refuted. The final sentence concludes that the Maker of the world must be a willing, choosing agent (muriid mukhtaar), not a blind cause or nature.

Key Terms
مانع حادث
مريد مختار
علة
طبيعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an originated impediment solves the problem of why the world began to exist at a specific time.
Correction: Sanusi shows this implies the world would have existed from eternity before the impediment appeared, and also leads to an infinite regress, so the objection fails.

Checkpoint
What is the core conclusion that Sanusi draws from refuting both objections?
A) The world could be created by a nature if conditions are met.
B) The Creator must be a willing agent (muriid mukhtaar), not a cause or nature.
C) The world is eternal and was not created.
D) Impediments are necessary for creation.

Key Takeaway
The Creator must be a willing agent (muriid mukhtaar), not a cause or nature, because no other explanation can account for the world's existence.

Refutation of the Objection of a Pre-Eternal Impediment
Sanusi refutes the objection that a pre-eternal impediment could delay the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a pre-eternal impediment cannot be used to object to the eternity of the world from a nature, and how Sanusi refutes this objection using the nature of eternity.

Core Idea
An eternal impediment cannot cease; thus it would block existence forever.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses the first objection against the argument that if the Creator were a nature, the world would be eternal. The objector cleverly suggests a pre-eternal impediment could delay creation. But Sanusi shows that this very suggestion undermines itself by leading to the impossibility of the world's existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the objector frames the argument: nature requires conditions and absence of impediments, so a pre-eternal impediment could explain delay.
Pay attention to the terms 'nature' (طبيعة) and 'pre-eternal impediment' (مانع أزلي).
2
Identify the refutation's core logic
Sanusi argues that if the impediment is pre-eternal (i.e., eternal), it cannot cease to exist. Understand why an eternal thing is necessary and cannot perish.
3
Reflect on the consequence
If the impediment cannot cease, the world would never exist. But the world does exist, so the objection is false. This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول من الاعتراضين: أَنَّ لا تُسَلَّمُ أَنَّهُ يلزم على تقدير كون الصانع طبيعةً قدم العالم؛ لما تقرر أن الطبيعة لا يلزم أن تقارن مطبوعها إلا إذا توفرت الشروط وانتفَت الموانع، ولم لا يقال: إنما لم يوجد العالم معها في الأزل لوجود مانع أزلي منع من وجوده في الأزل، فلما انتفى ذلك المانع فيما لا يزال أوجدت الطبيعة حينئذ العالم؟ وجوابه: ما أشرنا إليه في العقيدة: أن هذا الاعتراض فاسد؛ لأنه يستلزم ألا يوجد العالم أبداً؛ لما فرض من قدم المانع لوجوده، وإذا كان مانعه قديماً استحال عدمه، فيستحيل إذا وجود العالم، ومشاهدة وجوده تكذب ذلك الفرض.
The first of the two objections: that it is not conceded that it is necessary upon the supposition of the Maker being a nature that the world is eternal; because it is established that nature does not necessarily accompany its natural effect unless conditions are met and impediments are absent. And why is it not said that the world only did not exist with it in pre-eternity due to the existence of a pre-eternal impediment that prevented its existence in pre-eternity, then when that impediment ceased in the future, nature then brought the world into existence? Its answer: what we indicated in the Creed: that this objection is invalid because it entails that the world would never exist—since it assumed the impediment to its existence is pre-eternal, and if its impediment is pre-eternal, its non-existence is impossible, so the world's existence becomes impossible, and observing its existence falsifies that supposition.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection to the argument that if the Creator were a nature (rather than a willing agent), the world would necessarily be eternal. The objector argues that nature does not always produce its effect immediately; it requires conditions and absence of impediments. He suggests that there might have been a pre-eternal impediment preventing the world's existence alongside the nature, and when that impediment was removed later, the world came into existence. Sanusi's refutation is that this objection is invalid because if the impediment is pre-eternal (eternal), it cannot cease to exist—since eternal things cannot cease to be. Thus the world would never come into existence, which contradicts the observable fact that the world does exist. The argument relies on the principle that what is eternal (qadim) cannot cease to exist.

Key Terms
مانع أزلي
طبيعة
مطبوع
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An eternal impediment can later be removed, allowing the world to come into existence.
Correction: If something is pre-eternal (azali), it has no beginning and therefore cannot cease to exist. Thus if a pre-eternal impediment existed, it would prevent the world's existence forever, making the world's existence impossible — yet the world exists, so the objection fails.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi reject the objection that a pre-eternal impediment could delay the world's creation from a nature?
A) Because impediments cannot affect a nature.
B) Because a pre-eternal impediment would itself be eternal and cannot cease, so the world would never exist.
C) Because the nature would overcome any impediment immediately.
D) Because the objection assumes the world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
A pre-eternal impediment cannot be removed, so it would make the world's existence impossible, contradicting the observable fact that the world exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that a particular sin or weakness in yourself is so ingrained that it feels like an 'eternal impediment'—impossible to change? How does this lesson's proof that only Allah is eternal challenge that feeling, and what step can you take today to treat it as a temporal obstacle that can be removed by Allah's power?
?
The objector assumed that an eternal impediment could later be removed. In your relationship with Allah, are there any excuses or justifications you treat as permanent ('I'll never overcome this') that are actually temporal? Write down one such excuse and then write a counter-statement using the lesson's conclusion: 'If this were eternal, it could never cease; but since I can change, it is not eternal.'
?
When you pray for something but see no immediate change, do you sometimes assume there is a 'pre-eternal' barrier that cannot be removed? How does Sanusi's refutation remind you that any barrier to Allah's mercy is only temporary and that His will can remove it at any moment? What will you do to hold on to that hope in your next difficulty?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
The Obstacle Objection and Its Refutation
Sanusi presents the first objection and refutes it by showing an eternal obstacle leads to nonexistence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument that an eternal obstacle prevented the world's creation fails, and why such an obstacle would make the world's existence impossible.

Core Idea
An eternal obstacle cannot cease, so the world would never exist.

Sanusi presents the first objection against the eternity of the world from the nature hypothesis. The objector claims that an eternal obstacle could have prevented the world in pre-eternity, and later its removal allowed creation. Sanusi refutes this by showing that an eternal obstacle cannot be removed, so the world would never come to exist, contradicting observation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objector's claim that an eternal obstacle could have delayed the world's creation from nature.
Notice the objector uses the concept of conditions and obstacles from natural causation.
2
Understand the refutation
Sanusi argues that an eternal obstacle cannot cease to exist, so the world would never exist, which is false because we see the world exists.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Reflect on the principle
Consider why an eternal obstacle is impossible: its eternity means it cannot have a beginning of non-existence, so it always exists, preventing the world forever.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذان اعتراضانِ على جوابه عن تقدير أن صانع العالم علة أو طبيعة بأنَّه يلزمُ عليهِ قدم العالم الأوَّلُ مِنَ الاعتراضينِ : أَنَّ (۱) لا تُسلَّمُ أَنَّهُ يلزم على تقدير كونِ الصانع طبيعةً قدمُ العالم ؛ لما تقرَّرَ أنَّ الطبيعةَ لا يلزم أن تقارن مطبوعها إلا إذا توفّرَتِ الشروط وانتفَتِ الموانع ، ولمَ لا يُقالُ : إِنَّما لم يُوجَد العالم معَها في الأزلِ لوجودِ مانع أزلي منعَ مِنْ وجودِهِ في الأزلِ ، فلما انتفى ذلك المانعُ فيما لا يزالُ أوجدَتِ الطبيعة حينئذ العالم ؟! وجوابه : ما أشرنا إليهِ في العقيدةِ : أنَّ هذا الاعتراض فاسد ؛ لأنه يستلزم ألا يُوجَدَ العالم أبداً (۲) ؛ لما فُرِضَ مِنْ قدم المانع لوجودِهِ ، وإذا كانَ مانعُهُ قديماً استحالَ عدمُهُ ، فيستحيلُ إذا وجود العالم ، ومشاهدةُ وجودِهِ تُكَذِّبُ ذلكَ الفَرْضَ فإن قيل : نفرضُ المانعَ مِنْ وجودِ العالم حادثاً ؛ ليصح عليهِ العدم . قلنا : فيلزمُ أنْ يكونَ العالم قديماً ؛ لتجرُّدِ الطبيعة في الأزلِ مِنَ المانع على هذا التقدير ثم فَرْضُ هذا المانع حادثاً معَ قدم الطبيعة المؤثرة فيه.. لا يصح إلا إذا فُرِضَ أَنَّهُ توقَّفَ على عدمِ مانعِ آخر قبلَهُ ، ثم كذلك ، فيكونُ هذا الفَرْضُ مستحيلاً أيضاً ؛ لما فيهِ مِنْ لزومِ حوادث لا أَوَّلَ لها ؛ لأنَّهُ حينَئِذٍ يكون وجود كل حادث منها مسبوقاً بارتفاع حادث آخر إلى غير نهاية ، وإنَّهُ محال ، وقد تقدَّمَ بعضُ براهينه . ومِنْ أظرف ما استُدِلَ بهِ على استحالةِ حوادث لا أَوَّلَ لها : أنَّا إذا أخذنا الحوادث الماضية إلى زمن الطُّوفانِ جملةً واحدةً ، ثم أيضاً الحوادث الماضية إلى زماننا جملةً أخرى ، ثم طبقنا نهاية الجملة الأولى على نهاية الجملة الثانية .. فلا يخلو : إِمَّا أَنْ يظهر التفاوتُ مِنَ الجانب الآخر ، أو لا يظهرَ ؛ فإن لم يظهر فمحال ؛ لاستحالة أنْ تكونَ الجملةُ الناقصةُ مثل الجملة الزائدة ، وإِنْ ظهر لزم انقطاع الجملة الأولى وهي الجملة الناقصةُ ، فتكون متناهيةً ؛ لحصول مبدأ ومنتهى فيها ، وإذا تناهَتِ الجملةُ الأولى لزم تناهي الجملة الثانية أيضاً ؛ لأنَّ الثانية إنَّما زادَتْ على الأولى بقَدْرِ متناه ؛ وهو المقدار الذي مِنْ زمنِ الطُّوفانِ إلى زماننا ، والزائد على المتناهي بقدرٍ متناه يكون متناهياً ضرورةً . فقد ظهرَ لكَ بهذا : أنَّ تقدير المانع مطلقاً ـ أعني : قديماً أو حادثاً ـ مستحيل
These are two objections to his answer concerning the supposition that the Maker of the world is a cause or a nature, namely that the eternity of the world necessarily follows from it. The first of the two objections: that (1) it is not granted that it necessarily follows, on the supposition that the Maker is a nature, the eternity of the world; because it is established that nature does not necessarily accompany its impressed thing unless conditions are fulfilled and obstacles are removed. Why can it not be said: The world did only not come into existence with it in eternity due to the presence of an eternal obstacle that prevented it from existing in eternity, and when that obstacle is removed in what is to come, nature then brings the world into existence? And its answer: what we indicated in the Creed: that this objection is invalid because it entails that the world would never exist (2); because the obstacle to its existence is supposed to be eternal, and if its obstacle is eternal, its non-existence is impossible, thus the existence of the world is impossible, and the observation of its existence falsifies that supposition. If it is said: Let us suppose the obstacle to the existence of the world is originated so that non-existence is possible for it. We say: Then it follows that the world is eternal, because nature was free from the obstacle in eternity on this supposition. Then supposing this obstacle to be originated together with the eternity of the nature that causes it is not valid unless it is supposed that it depends on the removal of another obstacle before it, and so on. Thus this supposition is also impossible because it entails beginningless events (حوادث لا أول لها); because then the existence of each originated event among them would be preceded by the removal of another originated event ad infinitum, and that is impossible, and some of its proofs have preceded. And among the most elegant things used as proof for the impossibility of beginningless events is: If we take the past events up to the time of the Flood as one set, and also the past events up to our time as another set, then we align the end of the first set with the end of the second set... It cannot be but that either disparity appears from the other side or it does not appear. If it does not appear, it is impossible, because it is impossible for the deficient set to be like the augmented set. And if it appears, it necessitates that the first set, which is the deficient set, has an end, thus it is finite; because it has a beginning and an end. And if the first set is finite, then the second set is also finite; because the second set only exceeds the first by a finite amount, which is the amount from the time of the Flood to our time, and what exceeds the finite by a finite amount is necessarily finite. So it has become clear to you by this that the supposition of an obstacle absolutely—I mean old or originated—is impossible.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two objections against the claim that if the Maker is a nature, the world must be eternal. The objector argues that nature does not always produce its effect instantly; it requires conditions and the absence of obstacles. Therefore, it is possible that an eternal obstacle prevented the world in eternity, and when that obstacle is removed later, nature brings the world into existence. The answer is that this objection is invalid because an eternal obstacle cannot cease to exist, so the world would never exist, but we observe it does exist. A further objection: suppose the obstacle is originated. The answer: then the world would be eternal because nature was without obstacle in eternity. Also, supposing an originated obstacle from an eternal nature leads to an infinite regress of obstacles, which is impossible because it entails beginningless events. A clever proof against beginningless events is then given: comparing the set of past events up to the Flood and up to our time shows both sets must be finite. Thus, the supposition of any obstacle (eternal or originated) is impossible.

Key Terms
مانع (obstacle)
طبيعة (nature)
فَرْض (supposition)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: It is possible that an eternal obstacle prevented the world from being created in pre-eternity, and later the obstacle was removed allowing creation.
Correction: An eternal obstacle cannot cease to exist; if it is eternal, it always exists, so it would prevent creation forever. Thus the world would never exist, which is false.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's refutation, why is the objection that an eternal obstacle prevented the world's creation invalid?
A) Because an eternal obstacle cannot cease to exist, so the world would never exist, but we observe it does exist.
B) Because nature always produces its effect instantly without obstacles.
C) Because obstacles only affect originated beings, not eternal ones.
D) Because the world was created before the obstacle existed.

Key Takeaway
An eternal obstacle cannot be removed, so the world would never exist; thus the objection from an eternal obstacle fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this passage, the objector uses a hypothetical eternal obstacle to avoid accepting the logical conclusion. Are there areas in your faith where you invent 'eternal obstacles' to avoid acknowledging a truth that demands a change in your life? What is one excuse you need to abandon today?
?
Sanusi shows that a false supposition is contradicted by observable reality. Consider your own relationship with Allah: Is there a belief you hold that, if honestly examined, is contradicted by the reality of His creation or your own experience? Will you reexamine that belief now?
?
The argument hinges on the impossibility of an eternal thing ceasing. Reflect on the eternity of Allah: How does the absolute permanence of His attributes give you both comfort and accountability? Is there any area where you are relying on a temporary excuse rather than His eternal decree?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
9 min
The Originated Obstacle and the Impossibility of Beginningless Events
Sanusi refutes the originated obstacle and proves the impossibility of beginningless events using a set comparison.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why supposing an originated obstacle leads to an infinite regress and how the set comparison proof demonstrates the impossibility of beginningless events.

Core Idea
Every originated event requires a prior event, making an infinite regress impossible.

Sanusi now considers a second attempt: suppose the obstacle is originated rather than eternal. He shows this leads to an infinite regress of obstacles, which is impossible because it implies an infinite chain of events. He then provides a neat proof from the set of past events up to the Flood and up to our time to prove that past events must be finite.

Guided Path
1
Understand the sub-objection
The objector suggests that the obstacle is originated (hadath) so that it can cease to exist.
This is an attempt to avoid the previous problem.
2
Follow the refutation
Sanusi argues that if the obstacle is originated, then nature was free from it in pre-eternity, so the world would have been created then. Also, the originated obstacle would require a prior obstacle's removal, leading to an infinite chain.
This chain is impossible because it entails beginningless events.
3
Analyze the proof
Sanusi presents a proof from the set of past events up to the Flood and up to now. Compare the two sets: if they are equal, it's impossible; if one is longer, the shorter set must be finite, so both are finite.
This shows that the past cannot be infinite, so an infinite regress of obstacles is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذان اعتراضانِ على جوابه عن تقدير أن صانع العالم علة أو طبيعة بأنَّه يلزمُ عليهِ قدم العالم الأوَّلُ مِنَ الاعتراضينِ : أَنَّ (۱) لا تُسلَّمُ أَنَّهُ يلزم على تقدير كونِ الصانع طبيعةً قدمُ العالم ؛ لما تقرَّرَ أنَّ الطبيعةَ لا يلزم أن تقارن مطبوعها إلا إذا توفّرَتِ الشروط وانتفَتِ الموانع ، ولمَ لا يُقالُ : إِنَّما لم يُوجَد العالم معَها في الأزلِ لوجودِ مانع أزلي منعَ مِنْ وجودِهِ في الأزلِ ، فلما انتفى ذلك المانعُ فيما لا يزالُ أوجدَتِ الطبيعة حينئذ العالم ؟! وجوابه : ما أشرنا إليهِ في العقيدةِ : أنَّ هذا الاعتراض فاسد ؛ لأنه يستلزم ألا يُوجَدَ العالم أبداً (۲) ؛ لما فُرِضَ مِنْ قدم المانع لوجودِهِ ، وإذا كانَ مانعُهُ قديماً استحالَ عدمُهُ ، فيستحيلُ إذا وجود العالم ، ومشاهدةُ وجودِهِ تُكَذِّبُ ذلكَ الفَرْضَ فإن قيل : نفرضُ المانعَ مِنْ وجودِ العالم حادثاً ؛ ليصح عليهِ العدم . قلنا : فيلزمُ أنْ يكونَ العالم قديماً ؛ لتجرُّدِ الطبيعة في الأزلِ مِنَ المانع على هذا التقدير ثم فَرْضُ هذا المانع حادثاً معَ قدم الطبيعة المؤثرة فيه.. لا يصح إلا إذا فُرِضَ أَنَّهُ توقَّفَ على عدمِ مانعِ آخر قبلَهُ ، ثم كذلك ، فيكونُ هذا الفَرْضُ مستحيلاً أيضاً ؛ لما فيهِ مِنْ لزومِ حوادث لا أَوَّلَ لها ؛ لأنَّهُ حينَئِذٍ يكون وجود كل حادث منها مسبوقاً بارتفاع حادث آخر إلى غير نهاية ، وإنَّهُ محال ، وقد تقدَّمَ بعضُ براهينه . ومِنْ أظرف ما استُدِلَ بهِ على استحالةِ حوادث لا أَوَّلَ لها : أنَّا إذا أخذنا الحوادث الماضية إلى زمن الطُّوفانِ جملةً واحدةً ، ثم أيضاً الحوادث الماضية إلى زماننا جملةً أخرى ، ثم طبقنا نهاية الجملة الأولى على نهاية الجملة الثانية .. فلا يخلو : إِمَّا أَنْ يظهر التفاوتُ مِنَ الجانب الآخر ، أو لا يظهرَ ؛ فإن لم يظهر فمحال ؛ لاستحالة أنْ تكونَ الجملةُ الناقصةُ مثل الجملة الزائدة ، وإِنْ ظهر لزم انقطاع الجملة الأولى وهي الجملة الناقصةُ ، فتكون متناهيةً ؛ لحصول مبدأ ومنتهى فيها ، وإذا تناهَتِ الجملةُ الأولى لزم تناهي الجملة الثانية أيضاً ؛ لأنَّ الثانية إنَّما زادَتْ على الأولى بقَدْرِ متناه ؛ وهو المقدار الذي مِنْ زمنِ الطُّوفانِ إلى زماننا ، والزائد على المتناهي بقدرٍ متناه يكون متناهياً ضرورةً . فقد ظهرَ لكَ بهذا : أنَّ تقدير المانع مطلقاً ـ أعني : قديماً أو حادثاً ـ مستحيل
These are two objections to his answer concerning the supposition that the Maker of the world is a cause or a nature, namely that the eternity of the world necessarily follows from it. The first of the two objections: that (1) it is not granted that it necessarily follows, on the supposition that the Maker is a nature, the eternity of the world; because it is established that nature does not necessarily accompany its impressed thing unless conditions are fulfilled and obstacles are removed. Why can it not be said: The world did only not come into existence with it in eternity due to the presence of an eternal obstacle that prevented it from existing in eternity, and when that obstacle is removed in what is to come, nature then brings the world into existence? And its answer: what we indicated in the Creed: that this objection is invalid because it entails that the world would never exist (2); because the obstacle to its existence is supposed to be eternal, and if its obstacle is eternal, its non-existence is impossible, thus the existence of the world is impossible, and the observation of its existence falsifies that supposition. If it is said: Let us suppose the obstacle to the existence of the world is originated so that non-existence is possible for it. We say: Then it follows that the world is eternal, because nature was free from the obstacle in eternity on this supposition. Then supposing this obstacle to be originated together with the eternity of the nature that causes it is not valid unless it is supposed that it depends on the removal of another obstacle before it, and so on. Thus this supposition is also impossible because it entails beginningless events (حوادث لا أول لها); because then the existence of each originated event among them would be preceded by the removal of another originated event ad infinitum, and that is impossible, and some of its proofs have preceded. And among the most elegant things used as proof for the impossibility of beginningless events is: If we take the past events up to the time of the Flood as one set, and also the past events up to our time as another set, then we align the end of the first set with the end of the second set... It cannot be but that either disparity appears from the other side or it does not appear. If it does not appear, it is impossible, because it is impossible for the deficient set to be like the augmented set. And if it appears, it necessitates that the first set, which is the deficient set, has an end, thus it is finite; because it has a beginning and an end. And if the first set is finite, then the second set is also finite; because the second set only exceeds the first by a finite amount, which is the amount from the time of the Flood to our time, and what exceeds the finite by a finite amount is necessarily finite. So it has become clear to you by this that the supposition of an obstacle absolutely—I mean old or originated—is impossible.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two objections against the claim that if the Maker is a nature, the world must be eternal. The objector argues that nature does not always produce its effect instantly; it requires conditions and the absence of obstacles. Therefore, it is possible that an eternal obstacle prevented the world in eternity, and when that obstacle is removed later, nature brings the world into existence. The answer is that this objection is invalid because an eternal obstacle cannot cease to exist, so the world would never exist, but we observe it does exist. A further objection: suppose the obstacle is originated. The answer: then the world would be eternal because nature was without obstacle in eternity. Also, supposing an originated obstacle from an eternal nature leads to an infinite regress of obstacles, which is impossible because it entails beginningless events. A clever proof against beginningless events is then given: comparing the set of past events up to the Flood and up to our time shows both sets must be finite. Thus, the supposition of any obstacle (eternal or originated) is impossible.

Key Terms
حوادث لا أول لها (beginningless events)
متناهٍ (finite)
جملة (set)
مانع (obstacle)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the obstacle is originated, it can be removed without problem, allowing the world to be created after a delay.
Correction: An originated obstacle would still lead to an infinite regress of obstacles because its existence must be preceded by the removal of another obstacle. This regress is impossible because it entails an infinite chain of events, which contradicts the finiteness of the past.

Checkpoint
In the set comparison proof, why does Sanusi conclude that the set of past events up to the Flood must be finite?
A) Because it is shorter than the set up to our time, so it must have a beginning and end.
B) Because the Flood was a historical event that began time.
C) Because infinite sets cannot be compared.
D) Because the set up to our time is finite, so the Flood set must also be finite.

Key Takeaway
The supposition of any obstacle (eternal or originated) is impossible; the finite nature of the past proves that beginningless events cannot exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the past is finite and the world has a beginning, then your existence is not accidental. How does this truth change the way you view your purpose today? What will you do to align your life with that purpose?
?
The proof shows that any obstacle to the existence of the world is impossible. What obstacles—subtle excuses or doubts—do you put between yourself and fully submitting to the Creator? Are you willing to remove them today?
?
This lesson refutes the idea of an eternal world. Are you living as if this world is eternal and all-important, or as if it is a finite test? What specific change in your priorities does this require from you now?

Causality and Created Things
beginner
4 min
Second Objection: The Condition Missing in Eternity
Sanusi states the second objection: the world did not exist with nature due to a missing condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection to divine attribute causation, which posits a missing condition rather than an obstacle to explain the world's temporal origination.

Core Idea
A missing condition, not an obstacle, can delay nature's effect.

Sanusi presents a refined version of the objection against the world's eternity. While the first objection posited an eternal obstacle, this second objection suggests that the world's existence required a condition that was absent in eternity, allowing it to arise only when that condition later appears. This shifts the debate from hindrance to dependency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation side by side. Notice the structure: supposition of an eternal nature, then explanation of why the world did not exist in eternity, and finally when it does exist.
The passage is compact; each phrase carries weight.
2
Identify the key terms
Find 'shart' (condition) and 'tabi'ah azaliyyah' (eternal nature). Understand how 'condition' differs from 'obstacle' in the first objection.
The condition is something that must be present for the effect to occur.
3
Analyze the logical structure
The objection has two parts: (1) The Maker is an eternal nature, (2) The world's non-existence in eternity is due to a missing condition, not an intrinsic failure of nature. Once the condition appears, the world necessarily comes from nature.
This preserves the idea that nature always operates given the right conditions.
4
Compare with the first objection
Recall the first objection used an eternal obstacle. Here the obstacle is replaced by an absent condition. Both aim to explain temporal origination from an eternal cause.
The answer to this objection will come in later pages.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this objection is philosophically significant: it challenges the necessity of eternal creation even from an eternal nature, by introducing conditional dependence.
The answer must refute this conditional dependency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني مِنَ الاعتراضين : أنَّا نقدِّرُ الصانع طبيعة أزلية ، وإنَّما لم يُوجَدْ مَعَها العالم في الأزلِ لتوقُّفِ وجودِهِ على شرط لم يكن في الأزلِ ، وحينَ وُجِدَ الشرطُ فيما لا يزالُ وُجِدَ العالم عنِ الطبيعة
The second of the two objections: that we suppose the Maker to be an eternal nature, and the world did not exist with it in eternity only because its existence depended on a condition that was not present in eternity, and when the condition is present in what is to come, the world comes into existence from the nature.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection. The objector concedes the premise that if the Maker is a nature, the world might not be eternal if its existence requires a condition that was absent in eternity. This is analogous to the first objection but uses a condition instead of an obstacle. The answer to this objection is not present in this window but continues on subsequent pages.

Key Terms
شرط (condition)
طبيعة أزلية (eternal nature)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the Maker is an eternal nature, the world must exist eternally without any condition.
Correction: The objection shows that an eternal nature may produce its effect only when a certain condition is present, which may be absent in eternity, allowing the effect to be temporal.

Checkpoint
What does the second objection propose as the reason the world did not exist with nature in eternity?
A) An eternal obstacle prevented it.
B) Nature was not yet active.
C) A necessary condition for the world's existence was absent.
D) The world was created from nothing.

Key Takeaway
The second objection explains the world's temporal origination by positing a condition missing in eternity, rather than an obstacle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that God is distant or that His help is delayed? Could it be that you are neglecting a condition that He has placed for His mercy to appear? What condition might you need to fulfill today?
?
When you think about your struggles, do you blame God for not acting sooner, or do you honestly examine whether the necessary condition for His response is present in your life? What is that condition, and are you actively seeking it?
?
If the world's existence itself waited upon a condition set by the Maker, what does that teach you about patience and submission to divine wisdom? How can you apply this to your current waiting period?

Divine Actions Have No Cause; Known Only by Revelation
Sanusi summarizes the Ash'ari position on divine actions having no cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that divine actions and rulings have no cause and are known only through revelation, and how to interpret causal language in revelation.

Core Idea
God's actions have no cause; only revelation tells us what He does.

Sanusi presents a key Ash'ari principle: divine actions are not caused by anything external, and human reason cannot deduce them. This protects God's absolute freedom and power. The passage also explains how to understand causal language in scripture.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the concise, summary style of Sanusi.
Focus on the term 'ikhtiyār' (choice) and how it is defined.
2
Identify the two core claims
The passage makes two clear claims: (1) divine choice has no cause and is beyond reason, (2) all divine actions and rulings have no cause. Find these in the text.
The phrase 'lā ʿilla lahā' is key.
3
Understand the interpretive principle
Sanusi says any causal language in religious texts is to be interpreted figuratively as 'indicators' (amārāt), not as actual causes. Ask: why would scholars use causal language?
This prevents attributing necessity to God's actions.
4
Connect to the broader theological framework
Reflect on why this matters: if God's actions had causes, they would be necessitated, contradicting God's absolute freedom (ikhtiyār).
This is a cornerstone of Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاختيار ، لا سبب له ولا مجال للعقل فيه أصلاً ، وإنَّما يُعرَفُ بالشرع فقط وبالجملة : فأفعالُهُ تعالى وأحكامه لا علة لها ، وما يُوجَدُ مِنَ التعليلِ لذلكَ في كلام أهل الشرع فمُؤوّل بالأمارات أو نحوها مما يصح .
Choice (al-ikhtiyār) has no cause and no room for intellect in it at all; it is only known by revelation (al-sharʿ). In summary: His actions, exalted be He, and His rulings have no cause (ʿilla), and whatever is found of causation for that in the speech of the people of revelation is interpreted (muʾawwal) as indicators (al-amārāt) or similar things that are valid.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage states the Ash'ari position on divine actions and rulings. The author begins by asserting that 'choice' (al-ikhtiyār) in relation to God has no cause and is beyond intellectual deduction; it is only known through revelation. Then he summarizes that all of God's actions and rulings have no cause, and any apparent causal language in religious texts must be interpreted figuratively as mere indicators or similar, not as real causes. This guards against attributing necessity or independent causal power to anything besides God.

Key Terms
اختيار
علة
شرع
تأويل
أمارات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's actions are arbitrary because they have no cause.
Correction: The passage denies secondary causality, not divine wisdom or purpose. God's actions are free but not arbitrary; they are known through revelation.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, how are divine actions and rulings known?
A) Through rational deduction
B) Only through revelation
C) Through both reason and revelation
D) Through empirical observation

Key Takeaway
God's actions have no cause and can only be known through revelation; any causal language in revelation is figurative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When something difficult happens in your life, do you immediately try to figure out 'why' using your own reasoning, or do you turn to revelation? What does your instinct reveal about how much you really depend on your intellect versus divine guidance?
?
If you truly believed that every divine action has no cause and is known only through revelation, would you stop demanding explanations from God? Are you willing to accept not knowing the reasons behind His decrees, even when they hurt?
?
How often do you interpret life events by reading your own 'indicators' instead of submitting to what revelation says? This lesson says causal language is only figurative—are you ready to let go of your need to find a cause for everything and simply trust God's revealed will?

Refutation of Mu'tazilite Causality in Legal Rulings
Sanusi refutes Mu'tazilite causal explanation for prohibition of wine and retaliation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's argument against Mu'tazilite causality, specifically why intoxication and killing cannot be efficient causes for legal rulings.

Core Idea
Legal rulings are based on divine choice, not on causal properties of actions.

In this passage, Sanusi directly challenges the Mu'tazilite claim that specific attributes of actions, such as intoxication in wine or killing in retaliation, serve as the efficient cause (al-illah al-bā'ithah) for the Sharia's rulings. He argues that such a view undermines divine transcendence and sole agency in creation. The passage presents two objections to the wine example and then applies the same reasoning to retaliation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the literal translation. Let Sanusi's argument flow naturally before dissecting it.
Notice the phrase 'min wajhayn' indicating two aspects.
2
Identify the two objections against Mu'tazilite causality
The passage explicitly offers two objections (wajhan) against the Mu'tazilite claim. Locate each and understand how they differ: the first focuses on divine creation of the act, the second on the direct creation of the accident.
3
Examine the application to retaliation
Sanusi extends the same reasoning to the obligation of retaliation (qiṣāṣ). Notice how he argues that all elements of the killing—hand, sword, wound, death—are created directly by God, rejecting any causal chain.
Note the rhetorical question 'how could that be?'
4
Reflect on the broader implications
Sanusi concludes with 'measure all other rulings on this.' Consider how this principle applies to all Sharia rulings: they are based on divine command, not on inherent properties of actions.
This connects to the Ash'ari doctrine of occasionalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنَّ تحريم شرب الخمرِ مثلاً : لو جُعِلَتِ العلَّةُ الباعثة عليهِ الإسكار الذي اشتمل على إفسادِ العقل كما يقوله المعتزلة.. لكان ذلك فاسداً مِنْ وجهينِ أحدهما : أنَّ شرب الخمرِ فعلٌ مِنْ أفعالِ اللهِ تعالى ؛ بمعنى : أَنَّهُ مخلوق لهُ جلَّ وعزّ ، كما أنَّ شرب الماء كذلك ، ولا أثر للعبدِ في شيء منهما أصلاً ، فكون ذلكَ نُصِبَ أمارة على استحقاق العقاب ، وهذا لم يُنصَبْ لذلك .. لا مُوجِبَ له إلا محضُ الاختيار . الثاني : أنَّ الإسكار عندَ أهلِ الحقِّ ليس ناشئاً عن شرب الخمرِ ، ولا أثر للخمر فيهِ أصلاً ، لا بطبعه ولا بقوَّةٍ جُعِلَتْ فيهِ ، وإِنَّما الإسكارُ عَرَضٌ مخلوق لهُ تعالى بلا واسطة ، وقد أجرى سبحانه عادته أنْ يخلق هذا العَرَضَ عندَ شرب الخمرِ إِنْ شاءَ ، فالخمرُ بالنسبة إلى عدم التأثير في الإسكار وإفسادِ العقل . . كالماء سواء بسواء . ونحو هذا تعليلُ وجوب القصاص بالقتل العمد العدوان. . لا يصحُ أنْ يُفهَمَ على أنَّهُ الباعث للشارع على الحكم؛ لما عرفت مِنْ وجوبِ انفراده تعالى بإيجاد جميع الكائنات ، فلا محيي ولا مميت إلا الله تعالى ، فالقتل العمد والخطأُ كلاهما مخلوق الله تعالى متماثلان في أنْ لا أثر ألبتة لكلِّ ما سواه تعالى فيهما ، فكيف وحركة يد الضارب ، وحركة سيفِهِ ، وجَرْحُ المضروب ، والموتُ الكائن بعد ذلك .. مخلوقة كلُّها الله تعالى بدءاً بلا واسطةٍ ، ولم يتولَّد بعضُ تلكَ الأمور عن بعض ، بل اقترانها على ما وقعَ مِنْ غيرِ تأثيرِ ألبتة مِنْ بعضها في بعض إنَّما هو بمخضِ خلقِهِ تعالى ، ونفوذ مشيئته ؟! وقس على هذا سائر الأحكام .
For example, the prohibition of drinking wine: if the efficient cause made for it is intoxication which includes corruption of the intellect, as the Mu'tazila say, that would be invalid from two aspects. One of them: that drinking wine is an act of God Most High, meaning it is created by Him, Glorious and Exalted, just as drinking water is likewise, and the servant has absolutely no effect in either of them originally. So the fact that that (wine) was set as a sign for deserving punishment, while this (water) was not set for that, has no obligator except pure choice. The second: that intoxication, according to Ahl al-Haqq, does not arise from drinking wine, and wine has absolutely no effect in it, neither by its nature nor by a power placed in it. Rather, intoxication is an accident created by Him Most High without intermediary. He, glorified, has brought about His custom that He creates this accident at the drinking of wine if He wills. So wine, with respect to having no effect in intoxication and corruption of the intellect, is like water, exactly the same. And similarly, the reasoning for the obligation of retaliation for intentional wrongful killing is not validly understood as being the motive for the Lawgiver in the ruling, because of what you have known about the necessity of His sole agency in bringing into existence all things. So there is no giver of life nor taker of life except God Most High. So intentional killing and accidental killing are both created by God Most High, similar in that there is absolutely no effect for anything other than Him in them. How could that be, when the movement of the striker's hand, the movement of his sword, the wounding of the struck person, and the death that occurs after that are all created by God Most High from the beginning without intermediary, and some of these things are not generated from others. Rather, their conjunction in the manner that occurred, with absolutely no influence of some upon others, is only by the pureness of His creation and the penetration of His will? And measure all other rulings on this.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that specific attributes of actions (like intoxication in wine) are the efficient causes (al-'illah al-bā'ithah) for legal rulings. Sanusi presents two objections against the Mu'tazilite reasoning for prohibiting wine. First objection: Drinking wine is an act of God – He creates it – just like drinking water; neither servant has any effect in them. So why is one prohibited and the other not? This difference has no cause except God's pure choice (maḥḍ al-ikhtiyār). Second objection: According to Ahl al-Haqq (the Ash'aris), intoxication is not produced by wine; wine has no intrinsic or endowed power to cause it. Rather, intoxication is an accident (ʻaraḍ) created directly by God at the moment of drinking, as per His custom. Therefore wine and water are identical in having no causal influence. Sanusi then applies the same reasoning to the obligation of retaliation (qiṣāṣ) for intentional killing. The Mu'tazilites might say that the killing is the cause. But Sanusi argues that such a causal understanding is invalid because God alone creates all existents. No one kills except God; the killer's hand, sword, wound, and death are all directly created by God without any causal chain (tawallud). Their conjunction occurs purely by God's creation and will, not by any influence of one upon another. Thus all rulings follow the same pattern: they are based on divine command, not on intrinsic properties of acts.

Key Terms
العلة الباعثة
الإسكار
أهل الحق
العرض
التوليد
محض الاختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that Ash'aris deny any connection between actions and their consequences (e.g., that drinking wine never leads to intoxication).
Correction: Sanusi does not deny the habitual conjunction; he affirms that God creates intoxication at the time of drinking as per His custom. The connection is not causal but a direct creation by God.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why is the Mu'tazilite causal explanation for the prohibition of wine invalid?
A) Because wine is not intoxicating.
B) Because intoxication is a substance created by God directly.
C) Because both wine and water are acts of God, and the difference in ruling has no cause except divine choice.
D) Because the servant's choice is the cause.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes causal explanations for rulings by affirming that all actions are directly created by God, and any connection between an action and its consequence is merely by God's habitual custom, not by inherent causality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you blame the circumstances, your environment, or your own inclinations? Knowing that even your actions are created directly by God, how does this reshape your sense of personal responsibility and your dependence on Him for righteousness? Will you, starting today, immediately turn to Allah in repentance and ask Him to create in you obedience?
?
If Allah's rulings are based on His pure choice and not on the nature of actions, then your obedience or disobedience is not about the act itself but about your submission to His command. Is your worship driven by a desire to fulfil His command, or by a feeling that your actions inherently deserve reward? Examine your heart: do you obey because He is the Sovereign, or because you think your good deeds obligate Him?
?
Sanusi argues that intoxication is not caused by wine but created by God at the moment of drinking. This means that even the effects you see in your daily life—whether good or bad—are directly from Allah. How does this truth affect your fears and hopes? Are you more afraid of the apparent causes (like illness, poverty, or people) or of the One who alone creates them? Commit today to fear only Allah and to trust only in His decree.

Causality and Created Things
beginner
4 min
Refutation of Natural Causation
Sanusi refutes efficient causality and affirms direct divine creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ash'ari theologians deny causal efficacy to created things and affirm that Allah alone creates each event directly.

Core Idea
Allah directly creates every event; apparent causes are mere conjunctions.

This concise passage from Sanusi's creed dismantles the common assumption that events cause other events. Using the example of a sword strike and death, he shows that each moment is independently created by Allah. The lesson will walk through the example and the principle it establishes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the concrete example of the striker, sword, wound, and death.
2
Identify the four events
List the four specific events mentioned: hand movement, sword motion, wound, and death. Observe that they are presented as a sequence.
The author uses these to test our assumptions about causality.
3
Understand the key claim: no generation
Sanusi explicitly denies that these events are generated (توليد) from one another. This is the central negation of natural causality.
The Arabic term 'توليد' refers to one thing producing another by its own power.
4
Grasp the alternative: conjunction
Instead of causation, Sanusi affirms that the events merely coincide (اقتران) by Allah's direct creation and will. No influence exists between them.
Conjunction means they happen together but one does not cause the other.
5
Apply the principle generally
The passage ends with 'قس على هذا سائر الأحكام' — analogize this to all other rulings. Think of other examples like fire burning or food satisfying hunger.
This generalization makes the refutation universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يد الضارب، وحركة سيفه، وجرح المضروب، والموت الكائن بعد ذلك .. مخلوقة كلها الله تعالى بدءاً بلا واسطة، ولم يتولد بعض تلك الأمور عن بعض، بل اقترانها على ما وقع من غير تأثير ألبتة من بعضها في بعض إنما هو بمحض خلقه تعالى، ونفوذ مشيئته. وقس على هذا سائر الأحكام.
The hand of the striker, the movement of his sword, the wound of the struck, and the death that occurs thereafter ... all are created by Allah the Exalted from the beginning without intermediary, and some of these matters are not generated from others, rather their conjunction as it occurred without any influence at all from some of them upon others is only by the pure creation of Him the Exalted and the effectiveness of His will. And analogize upon this the rest of the rulings.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a proof against natural causality. The author gives a concrete example: the striker's hand, the sword's motion, the wound, and the subsequent death—none of these are caused by the preceding event. Instead, Allah creates each directly without mediation. The apparent sequence is a conjunction, not a causal generation. The principle is then generalized to all events.

Key Terms
خلق مباشر
اقتران
توليد
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many people think that events like cutting cause death, or that fire burns by its own nature.
Correction: This passage clarifies that Allah alone creates each effect directly; no created thing has inherent causal power. They only appear together by Allah's will.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the relationship between the striker's hand movement and the subsequent death?
A) The hand movement causes the death.
B) The death is generated from the wound.
C) Allah creates both independently and they merely coincide.
D) The sword's movement is the cause of the wound.

Key Takeaway
Allah directly creates every event; created things have no causal power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you take medicine or seek a cure, do you internally rely on the medicine's natural properties or on Allah's direct creation of healing? If you truly believed that no created thing has any influence, how would your heart's reliance change?
?
Think of a recent event that caused you fear—an accident, an illness, or a loss. Did your fear stem from believing that secondary causes could harm you independently? How can this lesson transform that fear into a deeper trust in Allah's sole control?
?
If every event is created directly by Allah, then every moment of your life—from the beating of your heart to the success of your work—is an immediate act of Allah. Does your daily gratitude reflect this? Commit to pausing after every blessing today to say, 'This is directly from Allah, not from any cause.'

The Metaphorical Causality of Human Actions
Sanusi explains why causal language for human actions is metaphorical, not literal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why scriptural references to human actions as causes of reward or punishment are metaphorical, preserving God's absolute sovereignty.

Core Idea
Human actions are signs, not causes, of divine reward or punishment.

In this passage, Sanusi tackles a subtle theological point: how to interpret verses that seem to attribute causality to human actions. He argues that such language is metaphorical, not literal, because nothing can compel God. The verse about reward for patience serves as a key example. Understanding this principle helps avoid anthropomorphism and upholds Divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage carefully
First, read the Arabic text and its translation. Pay attention to the technical terms like 'sabab' (cause) and 'majāz' (metaphor).
2
Identify the main claim
Sanusi states that the term 'cause' when applied to human actions does not imply urging God, but mere sequence. Locate where he makes this distinction.
Look for the phrase 'lā fī al-baʿthi ʿalayhi'.
3
Examine the Qur'anic example
Analyze the verse 'And He rewarded them for their patience...' Why can't patience be a literal cause? Sanusi explains that reward is purely by God's favor and actions are created by God.
The key point: 'lā sababa li-dhālika ʿaqlan'.
4
Understand the metaphorical interpretation
Sanusi says that deeds are 'amārāt sharʿiyyah' (legal indicators) of God's will to reward or punish. Causality is applied metaphorically. Grasp this shift from literal to metaphorical.
The phrase 'ʿalā ṭarīqi al-majāz' is crucial.
5
Apply the principle generally
The author concludes by urging the reader to apply this interpretive principle to all similar texts in the Qur'an, Sunnah, and imams' sayings. Consider how this method protects Divine transcendence.
This is a methodological rule for all apparent causal attributions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في مطلق الترتب على الشيء، حتى أدخلت لام التعليل عليه(١)، لا في البعث عليه؛ لاستحالة أن يبعث مولانا جل وعزَّ على الفعل شيء كما عرفت.
ونحو هذه الآيةِ في وجوبِ التأويل: قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَجَزَيْهُم بِمَا صَبَرُوا جَنَّةً وَحَرِيرًا ﴾ [الإنسان: ١٢] لأنَّ إعطاءهُ تعالى الجنَّةَ لِمَنْ يَشَاءُ إِنَّما هو بمخض فضلِهِ، ولا سببَ لذلك عقلاً؛ إذ الأعمال السابقة في الدنيا هي مخلوقة لهُ تعالى بغيرِ واسطةٍ مِنَ العبد أصلاً، فلم يخترع أحدٌ مِنَ المخلوقاتِ فعلاً مِنَ الأفعالِ حتى يستحق عقلاً أنْ يُثاب عليه أو يُعاقَبَ، لكنَّ تلك الأفعال لمَّا كانَتْ أماراتٍ شرعيَّةً على ما اختار سبحانه وتعالى مِنَ التفضُّل بالثواب أوِ العدلِ بالعقاب.. أُطلق عليها السببيَّةُ لذلكَ على طريقِ المجازِ.
وقس على هذا ما لا ينحصر في الكتاب والسنَّةِ وكلام الأئمةِ مِنَ الظواهر، والله سبحانه هو الموفّقُ بفضلِهِ، وهو الهادي مَنْ يَشَاءُ إِلى صراط مستقيم
(١) في (د): (أطلقت) بدل (أدخلت).
Concerning absolute dependence on the thing, to the extent that the causal lām (lām al-ta'līl) was applied to it [the verb] — not for urging it; because it is impossible that anything should urge our Master, Majestic and Mighty, to an act, as you have understood. And similar to this verse regarding the necessity of interpretation is His statement, Exalted is He: “And He rewarded them for their patience with a garden and silk” [Qur'an 76:12]; because giving Paradise by Him, Exalted, to whomever He wills is only by the pure flow of His favor, and there is no intellectual cause for that; for the previous deeds in this world are created by Him, Exalted, without any intermediary from the servant at all. So no one among created beings has originated any act at all such that he intellectually deserves to be rewarded or punished for it. But when those deeds were taken as legal indicators (amārāt shar'iyyah) of what He, Glorified and Exalted, has chosen of bestowing reward or justice in punishment, causality (sababiyyah) was applied to them metaphorically (majāz). And measure by this what is countless in the Book, the Sunnah, and the sayings of the imāms of apparent texts. And God, Glorified, is the Grantor of success by His favor, and He is the Guide whom He wills to a straight path. (1) In [copy D]: (uṭliqat) instead of (udkhilat).
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of a broader discussion on causality and the interpretation of scriptural language that attributes causation to human actions. The author begins by clarifying that the term 'cause' (sabab) when applied to human actions in the religious texts does not carry its real meaning of 'urging' (ba'th) God to act; rather, it signifies mere sequence or connection (mutlaq al-taratub). The reason is that it is impossible for any created thing to urge or compel God to do anything, as God is free and unconstrained. The author then provides a concrete example from the Qur'an: the verse 'And He rewarded them for their patience with a garden and silk' (76:12). At first glance, this verse seems to indicate that patience (sabr) is the cause of the reward. However, the author explains that such a literal interpretation is impossible because giving Paradise is purely an act of God's favor (bi-makhḍ faḍlihi), not something earned. Intellectually, no act of a creature can serve as a true cause for reward or punishment, since all acts are created by God without any real intervention from the servant. The connection between the act and the reward is not causal but conventional: God has chosen to make certain acts indicators (amārāt) of His decision to bestow reward or inflict punishment. Therefore, the attribution of causality to these acts is metaphorical (majāz), not literal. The author concludes by urging the reader to apply this interpretive principle to all similar apparent texts in the Qur'an, Sunnah, and the sayings of the imāms, warning that failure to do so would lead to misunderstanding God's transcendence. The footnote indicates a variant reading: in one copy, the word 'udkhilat' (was applied) is replaced by 'uṭliqat' (was launched/used).

Key Terms
سبب
مجاز
أمارة شرعية
لام التعليل
بعث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That human actions are literal causes of reward or punishment in a way that obligates God.
Correction: This passage corrects that by explaining that actions are merely legal indicators of God's unchanging decree, and causality is metaphorical.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why cannot the verse 'And He rewarded them for their patience with a garden and silk' be taken literally as indicating that patience is the cause of Paradise?
A) Because patience is a human act and cannot be a cause for anything.
B) Because giving Paradise is purely by God's favor, and no act of a creature can obligate Him.
C) Because the verse is abrogated.
D) Because patience is only a metaphor for good deeds.

Key Takeaway
Causal language for human actions in scripture is metaphorical; actions are only indicators of God's will, not independent causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses like 'rewarded them for their patience', do you feel a sense of entitlement because of your efforts? Does this lesson's emphasis on God's pure favor (bi-makhḍ faḍlihī) shake that feeling? How will you change your perspective on your good deeds from now on?
?
Sanusi says that no creature originates any act at all. How does that affect your view of your own sins? Do you still feel guilt, or does it make you more reliant on God's mercy? What practical change in your repentance does this demand?
?
This lesson teaches that causal language is metaphorical. Are there areas in your life where you operate as if your actions are the real cause of outcomes (e.g., success in work, health, relationships)? How can you train yourself to see them as mere indicators rather than causes, and to trust in God's will alone? What specific step will you take today to shift that mindset?

The Eye's Insensitivity and the Refutation of Natural Causation
Sanusi uses the eye's temperature insensitivity to deny natural causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses the design of the eye to refute natural causality and affirm that God alone is the true agent.

Core Idea
The eye's indifference to temperature proves natural causes are powerless.

Sanusi presents a subtle argument from the eye's design. He observes that the eye, unlike other body parts, does not sense heat or cold. This points to a deeper truth: the customary causes we associate with temperature have no real effect. The argument is a classic Ash'ari proof that God alone is the true cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's words establish the argument.
2
Identify the key claim about natural causes
Notice the phrase 'لا أثر لها ألبتة' (absolutely no effect). This is the central conclusion: customary causes have no inherent power.
The author explicitly denies both natural disposition (بطبعها) and any power placed in them (بقوة جعلت فيها).
3
Analyze the illustration comparing eye to hands/feet
The contrast between the delicate eye and the hard hands/feet shows that protection from temperature is not due to physical properties but to God's direct preservation.
Even though hands and feet are hard, they still require protection, while the eye, though soft, is unharmed.
4
Reflect on the teleological reason
Consider why God made the eye insensitive: for universal benefit across all times and conditions. This design wisdom reinforces the argument.
The face's proximity to the eye is also mentioned as necessary for exposure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وألطفُ مِنْ هذا : أَنَّهُ تعالى جعلَ هذا العضو معَ ما وصفناهُ مِنْ عادماً لحس الحرّ والبرد ؛ حتى يعلم العاقل بذلكَ أنَّ أسبابَ الحر والبرد العاديَّةَ لا أثر لها ألبتة في حرّ ولا بردٍ ، لا بطبعها ولا بقوَّةٍ جُعِلَتْ فيها ، حتى إنَّ الإنسانَ ليستر يديه ورجليه ويقيهما مِنَ الحرِّ والبردِ معَ شدَّةِ صلابتهما ، والعينُ غيرُ متضررة بشيءٍ مِنْ ذلكَ ، وجعلها سبحانه أيضاً كذلك ليكمل عموم المنفعة فيها في سائر الحالاتِ واختلافِ الأزمنة ، وجعل الوجه قريباً منها في ذلك للاحتياج إلى الاستعانة بكشفه في كثيرٍ مِنَ الأمور .
And more subtle than this: that He, exalted, made this organ, with what we have described of it, devoid of the sense of heat and cold, so that the intelligent person may know by that that the customary causes of heat and cold have absolutely no effect in heat or cold, neither by their nature nor by a power placed in them. So much so that man covers his hands and feet and protects them from heat and cold despite their intense hardness, while the eye is not harmed by anything of that. And He, glorified, also made it thus to complete universal benefit in it in all conditions and different times. And He made the face close to it in that because of the need to use its exposure in many matters.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage draws a theological conclusion from the eye's insensitivity to temperature. The author states that God made the eye without the sense of heat and cold. This is even more subtle than the previous wisdoms. The purpose is for the intelligent person to realize that the customary causes (أسباب عادية) have absolutely no effect (لا أثر لها ألبتة) in producing heat or cold — neither by their own nature nor by any power placed in them. This is a clear refutation of natural causality. To illustrate, the author contrasts the eye with hands and feet: even though hands and feet are hard, people still protect them from temperature, but the eye, despite its delicacy, is not harmed. This shows that the real agent is God alone. Furthermore, this design provides universal benefit across all times and conditions. The face is also close to the eye to allow uncovering it when needed. This passage is a classic Ash'ari argument that God is the only true agent and that natural causes are mere customs ('عادةً') with no inherent power.

Key Terms

حس البرد
أسباب عادية
لا أثر لها
بطبعها
بقوة جعلت فيها

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that heat and cold naturally affect the eye, but the passage argues they have no real effect; it is God who preserves the eye.
Correction: The text explicitly states that customary causes have absolutely no effect (لا أثر لها ألبتة) and that the eye is not harmed by heat or cold, disproving the idea of natural causation.

Checkpoint
What does the passage conclude about the 'customary causes' of heat and cold?
A) They have some effect
B) They have no effect at all
C) They affect only hard surfaces
D) They affect the eye

Key Takeaway
The eye's insensitivity to temperature proves that natural causes have no inherent power; only God acts as the true agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel the heat of the sun or the chill of a breeze, do you instinctively attribute it to 'nature' or 'physics'? How often do you pause to recognize that every sensation is directly from God, not from any independent cause? What would it mean for your daily life if you truly lived this belief? Will you consciously remind yourself of this truth the next time you feel a change in temperature?
?
The eye is delicate yet unharmed by extremes that affect harder organs. Are there areas of your life where you trust in your own strength or the 'hardness' of your plans, rather than relying on the One who protects without means? What is one specific situation where you need to shift your trust from secondary causes to the sole Agent? Write down a reminder today.
?
Sanusi says the eye's insensitivity is for 'universal benefit across all conditions.' Can you see a situation in your own life where God's design — even if it seems paradoxical — works for your benefit in a way natural causes could not? How can you respond with gratitude and reliance rather than complaint?

Eye's Insensitivity to Heat and Cold: A Proof of Divine Causality
Sanusi argues the eye's lack of heat/cold sensation refutes natural causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the eye's lack of heat/cold sensation serves as a subtle proof that God alone causes sensations, not natural causes.

Core Idea
The eye's delicate composition yet immunity to heat and cold disproves inherent natural causality.

Sanusi presents a second, more subtle argument to overturn the assumption that natural causes have inherent efficacy. He points to the eye—a moist, delicate organ—which does not feel heat or cold, while tougher hands and feet must be shielded. This counterintuitive fact exposes the fallacy of natural causality and points directly to divine agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic source passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the contrast Sanusi draws between the eye and the hands/feet.
The passage begins with 'وألطف من هذا' indicating a second, more subtle argument.
2
Identify the key contrast
Observe how Sanusi describes the eye (moist, receptive to harm) versus hands and feet (solid, need protection). Yet the eye is unaffected by heat and cold.
This contrast is the heart of the argument.
3
Understand the conclusion
Sanusi concludes that ordinary causes of heat and cold have absolutely no effect—not by their nature nor by any power placed in them. All sensation is directly from God.
The practical purpose (eye function in all conditions) reinforces divine wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وألطفُ مِنْ هذا : أَنَّهُ تعالى جعلَ هذا العضو معَ ما وصفناهُ مِنْ حالِهِ مِنَ الرطوبة وسرعة القبولِ لما يتضرَّرُ بهِ عادماً لحس الحرّ والبرد ؛ حتى يعلم العاقل بذلكَ أنَّ أسبابَ الحر والبرد العاديَّةَ لا أثر لها ألبتة في حرّ ولا بردٍ ، لا بطبعها ولا بقوَّةٍ جُعِلَتْ فيها ، حتى إنَّ الإنسانَ ليستر يديه ورجليه ويقيهما مِنَ الحرِّ والبردِ معَ شدَّةِ صلابتهما ، والعينُ غيرُ متضررة بشيءٍ مِنْ ذلكَ ، وجعلها سبحانه أيضاً كذلك ليكمل عموم المنفعة فيها في سائر الحالاتِ واختلافِ الأزمنة ، وجعل الوجه قريباً منها في ذلك للاحتياج إلى الاستعانة بكشفه في كثيرٍ مِنَ الأمور .
And more subtle than this: that He, exalted, made this organ, despite what we described of its state of moisture and quick receptivity to what harms it, devoid of the sensation of heat and cold; so that the intelligent person may know by that that the ordinary causes of heat and cold have absolutely no effect in heat or cold, not by their nature nor by a power placed in them. Even though man covers his hands and feet and protects them from heat and cold, despite their strong solidity, the eye is not harmed by any of that. And He, glorified, also made it thus to complete general benefit in it in all conditions and varying times, and He made the face close to it in that, due to the need to use its exposure in many matters.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument (aṭaffu min hādhā) to refute natural causality. Despite the eye's delicate composition (moisture, quick receptivity), it is insensitive to heat and cold, while the hands and feet, though tougher, require protection. This shows that ordinary causes (asbāb ʿādiyya) have no effect by their own nature or any inherent power. Rather, God directly controls sensations. The eye's lack of sensitivity also serves a practical purpose: it allows the eye to function in all conditions without being harmed, and the face is exposed for various needs. The argument underscores divine transcendence over natural laws.

طبع

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think heat and cold naturally affect all sensitive organs, and that natural causes have inherent power to produce sensations.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the eye, a sensitive organ, does not feel heat or cold, proving that ordinary causes have no effect by themselves; God directly creates all sensations.

Key Takeaway
The eye's immunity to heat and cold, despite its delicacy, shows that ordinary causes have no inherent effect—only God directly creates sensations.

?

?

In what area of your life have you built thick barriers (like gloves for the hands) when you could have trusted the One who guards even the vulnerable?
?
