تنبیه
على أقوال أعلام مِنْ أهل السنَّةِ ليس عليها المُعوَّلُ
ما اقتصرنا عليه في أصلِ العقيدةِ مِنْ عدم التأثير للقدرة الحادثة ألبتة هو المعروفُ المشهورُ عندَهم ، الذي لا يصح عقلاً ولا شرعاً خلافه ، وبعضُ مَنْ أُولعَ بنقل الغثّ والسمينِ مِنَ الأقوال .. ينقلُ هنا أقوالاً أُخَرَ ينسبها لأهل السنَّةِ أيضاً
فمنها ما نُقِلَ عن القاضي أبي بكر الباقلاني أنَّ القدرة الحادثةَ تُؤثر في أخص وصف الفعل ؛ ككونه صلاةً أو غَصْباً أو زناً أو نحو ذلك ، لا في وجود أصل الفعل
ونُقِلَ عن الأستاذ أبي إسحاق مثلُهُ ، إِلا أَنَّهُ لَمَّا كَانَ يَقولُ بنفي الأحوالِ عبَّرَ عن أخص وصفِ الفعلِ بالوَجْهِ والاعتبارِ ، فقال : القدرة الحادثةُ تُؤثر بوجه واعتبار
ومنها ما نُقِلَ عن إمام الحرمين في آخر أمرِه : أنَّ القدرة الحادثة تُؤثر في وجودِ الفعل على وَفْق مشيئة الله تعالى
Warning
Concerning the statements of notables from Ahl al-Sunna that are not relied upon.
What we have confined ourselves to in the original treatise (aṣl al-ʿaqīda) — that created power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha) has absolutely no effect — is what is well-known and famous among them, which cannot be valid intellectually or legally to contradict. And some of those who are obsessed with transmitting the bad and the good of statements transmit here other statements that they also attribute to Ahl al-Sunna.
Among them is what was transmitted from al-Qāḍī Abū Bakr al-Bāqillānī that the created power affects the most specific description (akhaṣṣ waṣf) of the act — such as its being prayer, usurpation, fornication, or the like — not the existence of the act itself.
And it was transmitted from al-Ustādh Abū Isḥāq the like of it, except that since he held the negation of states (aḥwāl), he expressed the most specific description of the act as "aspect and consideration" (al-wajh wa al-iʿtibār), and said: "The created power affects by way of an aspect and a consideration."
And among them is what was transmitted from Imām al-Ḥaramayn at the end of his life: that the created power affects the existence of the act in accordance with the will of God, exalted.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage is a warning (tanbīh) about variant opinions attributed to some Ash'ari scholars concerning the effect of created power (al-qudra al-ḥāditha). The author begins by reiterating the correct and well-known Ash'ari position: created power has absolutely no effect at all. This is the standard Ash'ari teaching, and any deviation is invalid both intellectually and legally. However, some individuals who indiscriminately transmit various statements attribute other opinions to Ahl al-Sunna. The author then lists three such opinions. First, from al-Qāḍī Abū Bakr al-Bāqillānī: created power affects the most specific description (akhaṣṣ waṣf) of an act — e.g., making it prayer, usurpation, or fornication — but not the existence of the act. Second, from al-Ustādh Abū Isḥāq al-Isfarāyīnī: a similar view, but because he rejects the theory of states (aḥwāl), he frames the specific description as 'aspect and consideration' (wajh wa iʿtibār). Third, from Imām al-Ḥaramayn al-Juwaynī in his later opinion: created power affects the existence of the act in conformity with God's will. The author implies that these opinions are not to be relied upon despite being attributed to major figures.

Key Terms
القدرة الحادثة (al-qudra al-ḥāditha)
أخص وصف (akhaṣṣ waṣf)
الأحوال (al-aḥwāl)
الوجه والاعتبار (al-wajh wa al-iʿtibār)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did al-Qāḍī Abū Bakr al-Bāqillānī believe about created power?
A) It affects the existence of the act
B) It affects the specific description of the act
C) It has no effect at all
D) It affects both existence and description

Key Takeaway
The three variant opinions — al-Bāqillānī, al-Isfarāyīnī, and al-Juwaynī — are not the standard Ash'ari position; the correct view is that created power has no effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a minority opinion from a great scholar like al-Bāqillānī or al-Juwaynī, does your heart lean toward following it because of their status, or do you submit to the consensus of Ahl al-Sunna as taught by the reliable scholars? Examine your own tendency to follow personalities over established truth.
?
The author warns against those who transmit 'the bad and the good of statements.' Think of a time when you accepted or shared a religious opinion without verifying its reliability from the mainstream tradition. How can you guard yourself from being one who indiscriminately spreads weak views?
?
If you were to stand before Allah tomorrow and be asked about your belief in His sole causation, would you be certain that you held the correct position, or would you realize you had unknowingly adopted a variant opinion that diminishes true tawḥīd? Commit today to seeking the sound ‛aqīdah from its authentic sources.

Refuting the Qadariyya on Created Power
Al-Tilimsānī refutes the Qadariyya's attribution of acts to the servant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Tilimsānī refutes the Qadariyya's attribution of some actions to the servant's power by using their own principle of possibility.

Core Idea
The Qadariyya's attempt to avoid jabr actually proves jabr.

After listing three erroneous opinions on how the servant's power produces acts, the author dismisses them as corrupt and quotes Sharaf al-Dīn ibn al-Tilimsānī. His logical argument shows that the Qadariyya’s own basis—that all contingents are attributed to God by possibility—forces them into the very compulsion they sought to escape.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the author’s strong condemnation of the opinions and the transition to al-Tilimsānī's argument.
The passage ends mid-sentence; that is fine—the core argument is complete.
2
Identify al-Tilimsānī's starting point
He takes the Qadariyya's own principle: all contingents (mumkināt) are attributed to God based on their possibility. There is no reason to specialize some to the servant.
3
Follow the dilemma on the 'aspect'
The concept of 'wajh' is either possible or impossible. If possible, it must be attributed to God's power. If impossible, it cannot be attributed to any power at all. Either way, it cannot be attributed to the servant.
This is the core logical move.
4
Understand the charge of jabr
Al-Tilimsānī then argues that the state (ḥāla) they posit cannot be independently intended by the servant, so their own view entails jabr.
The passage cuts off but the conclusion is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يخفى فسادُ هذهِ الأقوالِ ، وأنَّها مُتشعبةٌ عن مذاهب القدريَّةِ مجوس هذه الأمة
قالَ شرفُ الدين بنُ التِّلِمْساني في ردُّ ما نُسِبَ منها إلى القاضي والأستاذ : ( إنَّ معتمد القاضي وأصحابه في نسبة سائر الممكناتِ إلى الله تعالى إمكانها ، فليس تخصيص بعضها بأولى مِنْ بعض ، وذلكَ يطَّردُ فيما أضافوه للعبدِ ، فإنَّ هذا الوجْهَ إِمَّا أَنْ يكونَ ممكناً أو لا ؛ فإنْ كانَ ممكناً وجبَ إضافته إلى قدرة الله تعالى ، وإن لم يكن ممكناً امتنع نسبته إلى قدرة ما .
وما فرُّوا عنهُ مِنَ الجبرِ لازم لهم ؛ لأنَّ تلك الحالة لا يُتصوَّرُ القصد إلى اختراعها على حيالها ، فلا يتأتّى مِنَ العبد فعلها ما لم
And the corruption of these statements is not hidden, and that they branch out from the doctrines of the Qadariyya, the Magians of this nation.
Sharaf al-Dīn ibn al-Tilimsānī said in refuting what was attributed from them to al-Qāḍī and al-Ustādh: 'Indeed, the basis of al-Qāḍī and his companions in attributing all contingents (mumkināt) to God, exalted, is their possibility (imkān), so specializing some over others is not preferable, and that applies to what they attribute to the servant. For this aspect (wajh) is either possible or not; if it is possible, its attribution to the power of God, exalted, becomes necessary, and if it is not possible, its attribution to any power is impossible.
And what they flee from — namely jabr (compulsion) — is incumbent upon them, because that state (ḥāla) cannot be intended for invention in itself, so the servant cannot perform it unless...'
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage presents the refutation of the three opinions listed earlier. The author begins by declaring that the corruption of these statements is obvious, and they derive from the doctrines of the Qadariyya, whom he calls 'the Magians of this nation' (a common Ash'ari epithet). He then quotes Sharaf al-Dīn ibn al-Tilimsānī, who specifically refutes the opinions attributed to al-Bāqillānī and al-Isfarāyīnī. Al-Tilimsānī argues from their own principle: they hold that all contingent things (mumkināt) are attributed to God based on their possibility (imkān). If that is so, then there is no reason to specialize some contingents as attributable to the servant. He then focuses on their concept of 'aspect' (wajh): either that aspect is possible (mumkin) or it is not. If it is possible, then it must be attributed to God's power, because all possibles are within God's power. If it is not possible, then it cannot be attributed to any power at all. Therefore, their attempt to attribute the aspect to the servant fails. Moreover, the charge of jabr (compulsion) that they try to avoid actually applies to their own view, because the 'state' (ḥāla) they posit cannot be independently intended or invented by the servant; the servant cannot produce it without divine specification, which amounts to jabr. The sentence is cut off but the argument is clear.

Key Terms
الفساد (al-fasād)
القدريَّة (al-Qadariyya)
مجوس هذه الأمة (Majūs hādhihi al-umma)
الوجه (al-wajh)
الإمكان (al-imkān)
الجبر (al-jabr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Qadariyya successfully avoid jabr by attributing only the 'aspect' of an act to the servant.
Correction: Al-Tilimsānī shows that the aspect itself must be possible or impossible; if possible it belongs to God, if impossible it belongs to none. Thus the servant's power is not involved, and the state required for the act is not within the servant's control, leading to jabr.

Checkpoint
According to al-Tilimsānī, if the 'aspect' (wajh) of an act is possible, what follows?
A) It must be attributed to the servant's power
B) It must be attributed to God's power
C) It cannot be attributed to any power
D) It is a created attribute of the act

Key Takeaway
Al-Tilimsānī refutes the Qadariyya by showing that their own principle of possibility forces them into either attributing everything to God or denying any attribution to the servant, ultimately proving jabr.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own actions, do you ever find yourself feeling a sense of self-sufficiency, as if your deeds originate from your own independent power? How does the fact that your 'aspect' (wajh) is either possible (thus from God) or impossible (thus nonexistent) shake that feeling? Commit today to mentally attribute every good act back to the One who made it possible.
?
The Qadariyya claimed that positing a servant's power avoids jabr, but al-Tilimsānī demonstrates that their very system entails jabr. Are there areas of your life where you resist divine predestination because you fear it implies compulsion? What false dichotomy might you be holding that this passage exposes? Pray that God grants you acceptance of His decree without compromising accountability.
?
This passage calls the Qadariyya the 'Magians of this nation' because they dualistically assign some effects to God and some to others. Examine your heart: are there 'small matters' you habitually think you control apart from God (e.g., your success in work, your good mood, your ability to speak kindly)? List one such area today and consciously thank God for enabling it.

Ibn al-Tilmisani's Refutation of the Qadariyya
Ibn al-Tilmisani refutes the positions attributed to al-Qadi and al-Ustadh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn al-Tilmisani's refutation of the Qadariyya positions on causality and why they necessarily lead to compulsion (jabr) rather than avoiding it.

Core Idea
The 'aspect' theory cannot avoid compulsion; it either forces divine attribution or is impossible.

In this passage, Sharaf al-Din ibn al-Tilmisani directly takes on two prominent Qadariyya figures, al-Qadi (Abd al-Jabbar) and al-Ustadh (Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini). He argues that their attempts to carve out a role for human power through the concept of an 'aspect' (wajh) fail logically. By showing that their premises either lead to attributing all acts to God or to complete impossibility, he demonstrates that their position actually entails the very compulsion they sought to escape.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two targets and their shared principle
Notice that Ibn al-Tilmisani refutes both al-Qadi and al-Ustadh. Understand that both rely on the idea of an 'aspect' (wajh) to attribute acts to human power.
Al-Qadi here likely refers to al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar; al-Ustadh to Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini.
2
Understand the argument against al-Qadi regarding possibility
Ibn al-Tilmisani applies a dilemma: if the aspect is possible, it must be attributed to God's power; if impossible, it cannot be attributed to any power. Either way, human agency is negated.
3
See how this leads to compulsion (jabr)
Since the aspect cannot be intended independently (it is not a separate entity), the servant cannot act unless God creates the essence. Once God creates it, the servant cannot avoid the act. Thus compulsion is unavoidable.
This is a reductio ad absurdum: their position yields exactly what they deny.
4
Understand the additional argument against al-Ustadh
For al-Ustadh, the aspect exists only in the mind. Intention cannot be directed to something with no external reality, so the act cannot be performed at all.
This is an even stronger refutation because it attacks the ontological basis of the aspect.
5
Reflect on the overall refutation
Consider how Ibn al-Tilmisani uses logical necessity (wujub) and impossibility (imtina') to dismantle the Qadariyya position. Recognize that this is a classic Ash'arite argument preserving divine omnipotence.
The key takeaway: any via media between jabr and tafwiz fails if it introduces an independent entity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ شَرَفُ الدِّينِ بْنُ التِّلِمْسَانِي فِي رَدِّ مَا نُسِبَ مِنْهَا إِلَى الْقَاضِي وَالْأُسْتَاذِ : ( إِنَّ مُعْتَمَدَ الْقَاضِي وَأَصْحَابِهِ فِي نِسْبَةِ سَائِرِ الْمُمْكِنَاتِ إِلَى اللَّهِ تَعَالَى إِمْكَانُهَا ، فَلَيْسَ تَخْصِيصُ بَعْضِهَا بِأَوْلَى مِنْ بَعْضٍ ، وَذَلِكَ يَطَّرِدُ فِيمَا أَضَافُوهُ لِلْعَبْدِ ، فَإِنَّ هَذَا الْوَجْهَ إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ مُمْكِنًا أَوْ لَا ; فَإِنْ كَانَ مُمْكِنًا وَجَبَ إِضَافَتُهُ إِلَى قُدْرَةِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ مُمْكِنًا امْتَنَعَ نِسْبَتُهُ إِلَى قُدْرَةِ مَا . وَمَا فَرُّوا عَنْهُ مِنَ الْجَبْرِ لَازِمٌ لَهُمْ ; لِأَنَّ تِلْكَ الْحَالَةَ لَا يُتَصَوَّرُ الْقَصْدُ إِلَى اخْتِرَاعِهَا عَلَى حِيَالِهَا ، فَلَا يَتَأَتَّى مِنَ الْعَبْدِ فِعْلُهَا مَا لَمْ يَفْعَلِ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى تِلْكَ الذَّاتَ ، وَمَتَى فَعَلَ الذَّاتَ فَلَا يُتَصَوَّرُ مِنَ الْعَبْدِ تَرْكُهَا عَلَى زَعْمِهِمْ ، فَكَانَ الْجَبْرُ لَازِمًا لَهُمْ وَهَذَا عَلَى الْأُسْتَاذِ أَشَدُّ إِلْزَامًا ; فَإِنَّ الْوَجْهَ وَالِاعْتِبَارَ يَكُونُ فِي الْعَقْلِ ، فَكَيْفَ يَصِحُّ تَوَجُّهُ الْقَصْدِ إِلَى فِعْلِ مَا لَيْسَ لَهُ وُجُودٌ فِي الْخَارِجِ ؟! )
Sharaf al-Din ibn al-Tilmisani said in refutation of what was attributed from them to al-Qadi and al-Ustadh: 'Indeed, the reliance of al-Qadi and his followers in attributing all possible things to God the Exalted is their possibility, so specifying some of them is not more appropriate than others, and that applies consistently to what they attribute to the servant. For this aspect (wajh) is either possible or not; if it is possible, then its attribution to the power of God is necessary; and if it is not possible, then its attribution to any power is impossible. And that which they fled from—namely compulsion (jabr)—is necessarily binding on them; because that state cannot be intended to be invented by itself, so the servant cannot perform it unless God the Exalted creates that essence. And when He creates the essence, then the servant cannot abandon it according to their claim. Thus compulsion is necessarily binding on them. And this is even more binding on al-Ustadh; for the aspect and consideration are in the mind, so how can the intention be directed to doing something that has no existence in the external world?!'
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage is a direct quotation from Sharaf al-Din ibn al-Tilmisani, who refutes the positions attributed to al-Qadi (likely al-Qadi Abd al-Jabbar) and al-Ustadh (Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini). He begins by stating the principle of al-Qadi and his followers: all possible things are equally attributable to God, and there is no reason to specify some to the servant. He then applies this to the 'aspect' (wajh) that is claimed to be the way the created power affects the act. If the aspect is possible, then it must be attributed to God's power; if impossible, it cannot be attributed to any power at all. Thus the attempt to avoid compulsion (jabr) fails; it actually leads to compulsion. He then argues that the specific state (the aspect) cannot be the object of intention because it is not an independent entity; it must accompany an essence created by God. Once God creates that essence, the servant is compelled to act. This is even more problematic for Abu Ishaq, because the 'aspect and consideration' exist only in the mind, so directing intention to something mental with no external reality is impossible.

Key Terms
وجه
اعتبار
إمكان
جبر
قدرة حادثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that the 'aspect' (wajh) theory provides a middle ground between divine compulsion and human free will, allowing genuine human agency.
Correction: Ibn al-Tilmisani shows that the theory collapses into compulsion: all possible aspects are attributable to God, and the aspect cannot be intended independently, leaving no room for human power.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn al-Tilmisani, what is the result if the 'aspect' (wajh) is possible?
A) It must be attributed to the servant's power
B) It must be attributed to God's power
C) It cannot be attributed to any power
D) It is a mental entity only

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-Tilmisani demonstrates that any attempt to posit an independent 'aspect' for human power leads either to divine attribution or impossibility, thus entailing the very compulsion it seeks to avoid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your own actions, do you ever feel a tension between acknowledging God's complete control and feeling personally responsible? How does Ibn al-Tilmisani's argument challenge you to reconcile these? What would it mean for you to abandon any 'aspect' of your actions that you cling to as your own independent contribution?
?
Consider a sin you struggle with. Are you tempted to think, 'I try my best, but God creates the act' as a way to avoid full responsibility? How does the reality of compulsion (jabr) being necessarily binding on those who separate an 'aspect' from God's creation force you to confront your own accountability before Allah?
?
The 'aspect' theory was an intellectual attempt to preserve human dignity. Are there areas of your faith where you rely on mental constructs or 'considerations' to make God's decree more palatable? What would it look like to submit to the absolute reality of God's power over every atom of your existence, without any mental escape?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
10 min
Refuting Imam al-Haramayn on Created Power
Author's logical refutation of al-Juwayni on created power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma the author uses to refute Imam al-Haramayn's position on the causality of created power.

Core Idea
Created power cannot be the true cause of an act without contradiction.

In this passage, the author directly refutes a specific claim attributed to Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) regarding the role of created power in producing acts. The argument is a tight logical dilemma: either the act's existence is intrinsic to the power or it is not. Both options lead to impossible consequences, undermining the position. Pay close attention to the structure of the refutation, as it is a model of kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once through, then the English translation. Let the author's argument unfold without analysis first.
2
Identify the two main horns of the dilemma
The author presents two possibilities: either the act's existence is an intrinsic property (ṣifatu nafsihā) of the created power, or it is not. Notice how each horn is further unpacked.
The first horn has two sub-cases, both impossible.
3
Analyze the first horn and its sub-cases
If the act's existence is intrinsic, then either the power does not effect the act (negating its property) or it does (conflicting with God's power). Both are impossible. Understand why each is impossible.
4
Analyze the second horn and infinite regress
If the act's existence is not intrinsic, then the power needs a separate meaning (ma'nā) to cause the effect. This leads to an infinite regress (tasalsul) and the subsistence of a meaning in a meaning, which is absurd.
The author uses the principle that no regress can be accepted in kalam.
5
Summarize the refutation
The author concludes that the position is corrupt both rationally and textually. Reflect on how this dilemma strengthens the Ash'ari view of direct divine causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قُلْتُ : وَأَمَّا الْقَوْلُ الَّذِي نُقِلَ عَنِ الْإِمَامِ فَلَا يَخْفَى أَيْضًا فَسَادُهُ عَقْلًا وَنَقْلًا ; لِأَنَّ الْقُدْرَةَ الْحَادِثَةَ عِنْدَ تَعَلُّقِهَا بِوُجُودِ الْفِعْلِ عَلَى مُقْتَضَى هَذَا الْقَوْلِ : إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ مِنْ صِفَةِ نَفْسِهَا إِيجَادُ الْفِعْلِ الَّذِي تَتَعَلَّقُ بِإِيجَادِهِ أَوْ لَا . فَإِنْ كَانَ الْأَوَّلَ لَزِمَ : إِمَّا سَلْبُ صِفَتِهَا النَّفْسِيَّةِ إِنْ لَمْ تُؤَثِّرْ فِي الْفِعْلِ ، وَكَانَ الْمُوجِدُ لَهُ هُوَ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى ، أَوْ غَلَبَتُهَا لِقُدْرَتِهِ تَعَالَى إِنْ هِيَ الَّتِي أَثَرَتْ فِي الْفِعْلِ ، وَالْفَرْضُ أَنَّ قُدْرَتَهُ تَعَالَى تَعَلَّقَتْ بِإِيجَادِ ذَلِكَ الْفِعْلِ أَيْضًا ، وَكِلَا الْأَمْرَيْنِ مُحَالٌ . وَإِنْ كَانَ الثَّانِي - وَهُوَ أَنَّ التَّأْثِيرَ لَيْسَ صِفَةً نَفْسِيَّةً لِلْقُدْرَةِ الْحَادِثَةِ -: لَزِمَ أَنْ تَفْتَقِرَ إِلَى مَعْنًى يَقُومُ بِهَا وَيُوجِبُ لَهَا التَّأْثِيرَ ، وَيُنْقَلُ الْكَلَامُ إِلَى ذَلِكَ الْمَعْنَى الَّذِي أَوْجَبَ لَهَا التَّأْثِيرَ ، وَهَلْ ذَلِكَ أَيْضًا مِنْ صِفَةِ نَفْسِهِ أَوْ لِمَعْنًى قَامَ بِهِ ؟ وَيَلْزَمُ مَا سَبَقَ وَالتَّسَلْسُلُ ، وَقِيَامُ الْمَعْنَى بِالْمَعْنَى .
I say: As for the statement transmitted from al-Imam, its corruption is also not hidden, rationally and textually. Because the originated power, when it is related to the existence of the act according to the implication of this statement: either the bringing into existence of the act to which it is related is from its own property (ṣifatu nafsihā) or not. If the first, then either its own property is negated if it does not effect the act, and the agent of it is God the Exalted; or it dominates over His power if it is the one that effected the act, while the assumption is that His power is also related to bringing that act into existence. And both matters are impossible. And if the second—that effect (ta'thīr) is not an intrinsic property (ṣifatu nafsihā) of the originated power—then it follows that it is in need of a meaning (ma'nā) that subsists in it and necessitates effect for it, and the discussion is transferred to that meaning which necessitates effect for it: is that also from its own property or due to a meaning subsisting in it? And what preceded would follow, along with infinite regress (tasalsul) and the subsistence of a meaning in a meaning.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage is the author's own refutation of the position attributed to Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni). The refutation is structured as a logical dilemma. The author first states that the claim is corrupt both rationally and textually. He then analyzes the implication of the statement: when the created power (qudrah ḥadithah) is related to the existence of the act in accordance with this view, either the production of the act is an intrinsic property (ṣifatu nafsihā) of the power or it is not. If it is intrinsic, then two sub-possibilities arise: either the power does not actually effect the act—in which case its own property is negated and God is the agent—or it does effect the act—in which case it dominates over God's power, but God's power is also related to that act, leading to conflict. Both are impossible. If it is not intrinsic, then the power requires some other meaning (ma'nā) that subsists in it and grants it efficacy. This leads to an infinite regress (tasalsul) because that meaning would itself need to be analyzed similarly. Thus the position is absurd.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the author rejects all created causality outright, but he is specifically refuting al-Juwayni's innovation that the created power is the direct cause of the act, while mainstream Ash'aris hold that God alone creates the act and the power is merely a locus of acquisition (kasb).
Correction: The refutation targets a particular view (attributed to al-Juwayni) that the created power itself brings about the act. This is not the standard Ash'ari position.

Checkpoint
What is the first possibility the author considers for the created power's relation to the act?
A) The act's existence is an intrinsic property of the power
B) The act's existence is caused by God alone
C) The power has no relation to the act
D) The act exists by accident

Key Takeaway
The author's dilemma shows that any attempt to attribute independent causal power to created beings leads to logical contradictions, reinforcing the Ash'ari doctrine of exclusive divine causality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you succeed in a task, do you truly believe that God is the sole creator of that success, or do you take pride in your own independent ability? How would your gratitude change if you internalized that your power is not intrinsic? Pause now and thank God for every ability you used.
?
Think of a sin you committed. Did you blame it on your own weakness or circumstances, forgetting that God creates all actions? How does the refutation of independent created power reshape your understanding of repentance? Will you repent today with the conviction that your power is a gift, not your own?
?
The argument shows that claiming independent causality leads to absurdity. In what areas of your life do you still act as if you are the ultimate controller—your health, wealth, or relationships? Commit to one concrete reminder tomorrow that all power belongs to God alone.

The First Horn: Created Power's Effect as Essential Attribute
Sanusi presents the first horn of the dilemma and its two impossible outcomes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that created power produces its effect by its own essential nature leads to two impossible consequences.

Core Idea
If created power inherently produces acts, either it is ineffective or it overpowers Allah.

Sanusi continues his refutation of a problematic view attributed to al-Juwayni. He analyzes the first of two logical possibilities: that the temporally originated power brings about its effect by its own essential attribute. He then shows how this leads to two impossibilities, thereby undermining the view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sub-possibilities
Read the passage and find the two outcomes that follow if the act's effect is from the power's own essence.
The two sub-possibilities are introduced by 'إِمَّا ... أو'.
2
Understand the first impossibility
If the power does not affect, its essential attribute is negated, which is contradictory.
3
Understand the second impossibility
If the power does affect, it would overpower God's power, which is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلتُ : وأمَّا القولُ الذي نُقِلَ عن الإمام فلا يخفى أيضاً فسادُهُ عقلاً ونقلاً؛ لأنَّ القدرة الحادثة عند تعلُّقها بوجود الفعل على مقتضى هذا القول : إمَّا أَنْ يكونَ مِنْ صفةِ نفسها إيجاد الفعل الذي تتعلق بإيجاده أو لا. فإنْ كانَ الأَوَّلَ لزمَ : إِمَّا سلْبُ صفتها النفسيَّةِ إِنْ لم تُؤثر في الفعل ، وكان الموجِدُ له هو الله تعالى (٢) ، أو غَلَبَتُها لقدرتِهِ تعالى إِنْ هي التي أثَرَتْ في الفعل ، والفَرْضُ أَنَّ قدرتهُ تعالى تعلَّقَتْ بإيجادِ ذلكَ الفعل أيضاً ، وكلا الأمرين محال. وإنْ كانَ الثاني - وهو أنَّ التأثير ليس صفة نفسيَّة للقدرة الحادثة - : لزم أن تفتقر إلى معنى يقوم بها ويُوجِبُ لها التأثير ، ويُنقَلُ الكلام إلى ذلك المعنى الذي أوجب لها التأثيرَ ، وهل ذلك أيضاً مِنْ صفةِ نفسِهِ أو لمعنى قام به ؟ ويلزمُ ما سبق والتسلسل (٣) ، وقيام المعنى بالمعنى.
I say: And as for the statement that was transmitted from the Imam, its corruption is not hidden, intellectually and by transmission; because the temporally originated power, when it is related to the existence of an act according to the implication of this statement: either the bringing into existence of the act to which it is related to bring into existence is from its own essential attribute or not. If it is the first, it follows: either the negation of its essential attribute if it does not affect the act, and the one who brings it into existence is God Almighty (2), or its overwhelming of His power Almighty if it is the one that affected the act, and the assumption is that His power Almighty is also related to bringing that act into existence, and both matters are impossible. And if it is the second - namely that the effect is not an essential attribute of the temporally originated power - it follows that it is in need of a meaning subsisting in it that necessitates for it the effect, and the discourse is transferred to that meaning which necessitated for it the effect, and is that also from its own essential attribute or from a meaning subsisting in it? And what preceded and infinite regress follow (3), and the subsistence of a meaning by a meaning.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a view transmitted from the Imam (likely al-Juwayni) concerning the role of temporally originated power. The author states that the view is clearly corrupt both rationally and by transmitted evidence. He then presents a logical dilemma: The temporally originated power, when it is related to the existence of an act, either has the ability to bring that act into existence as an essential attribute of itself, or it does not.

First possibility: If bringing the act into existence is an essential attribute of the created power, then one of two sub-possibilities occurs: either the created power does not actually affect the act, in which case its essential attribute is negated and God is the true agent; or it does affect the act, but that would mean its effect overpowers God's power, which is impossible because both powers are related to the same act, but God's power cannot be overcome. Both sub-possibilities are impossible.

Second possibility: If the effect is not an essential attribute of the created power, then the created power requires some other attribute or meaning (ma'na) that subsists in it and gives it the ability to affect. This then leads to an infinite regress (tasalsul) because the same question is asked about that meaning: Is its being effective an essential attribute or does it require another meaning? This regress is impossible. The passage concludes that the subsistence of a meaning by a meaning (i.e., an accident in an accident) is also impossible. Thus the original view is refuted from both angles.

Key Terms
قدرة حادثة
صفة نفسية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what two impossibilities follow if created power's effect is from its own essential attribute?
A) Either the power does not affect (negating its attribute) or it overpowers God's power.
B) Either the power does not exist or it is eternal.
C) Either the power is attributed to God or to the human.
D) Either the act does not occur or it occurs by compulsion.

Key Takeaway
Attributing inherent causal power to creation leads to either negating that power or challenging Allah's exclusive agency—both impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform any action, do you genuinely feel that Allah is the one who brings it into existence, or do you assume your own ability is sufficient? How can you cultivate a constant awareness of your complete dependence on Allah's power in every moment?
?
If you truly believed that your power has no inherent effect and that Allah is the sole creator of your deeds, how would that change your attitude towards success, failure, and your own efforts? Would you still strive, or would you resign?
?
This lesson argues that claiming created power can produce acts independently leads to impossible consequences. Has there been an area in your life where you acted as if your own will was independent of Allah's will? What would it mean to submit that area to the reality that Allah alone is the creator of all acts?

The Second Horn: Non-Essential Effect and Infinite Regress
Sanusi outlines the infinite regress arising from the second horn of the dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that if created power's effect is not essential, then it requires an additional attribute, leading to an infinite regress and other impossibilities.

Core Idea
If effect is not from power's essence, any support attribute also requires explanation, leading to infinite regress.

Sanusi now examines the second possibility: that the created power's effect is not essential but comes from some other attribute. This too leads to an impossibility because it requires an infinite chain of attributes, and it also involves the invalid notion of an accident subsisting in another accident.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second horn
Notice that the second possibility is that the effect is not an essential attribute of the created power.
2
Trace the regress argument
If the effect is not essential, the power needs a meaning (ma'na) to cause the effect. That meaning itself must either be essential or need another meaning, leading to an infinite regress.
This is the classic infinite regress argument in kalam.
3
Understand the additional impossibility
The regress also implies 'قيام المعنى بالمعنى' (an accident subsisting in another accident), which is invalid in Ash'ari ontology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلتُ : وأمَّا القولُ الذي نُقِلَ عن الإمام فلا يخفى أيضاً فسادُهُ عقلاً ونقلاً؛ لأنَّ القدرة الحادثة عند تعلُّقها بوجود الفعل على مقتضى هذا القول : إمَّا أَنْ يكونَ مِنْ صفةِ نفسها إيجاد الفعل الذي تتعلق بإيجاده أو لا. فإنْ كانَ الأَوَّلَ لزمَ : إِمَّا سلْبُ صفتها النفسيَّةِ إِنْ لم تُؤثر في الفعل ، وكان الموجِدُ له هو الله تعالى (٢) ، أو غَلَبَتُها لقدرتِهِ تعالى إِنْ هي التي أثَرَتْ في الفعل ، والفَرْضُ أَنَّ قدرتهُ تعالى تعلَّقَتْ بإيجادِ ذلكَ الفعل أيضاً ، وكلا الأمرين محال. وإنْ كانَ الثاني - وهو أنَّ التأثير ليس صفة نفسيَّة للقدرة الحادثة - : لزم أن تفتقر إلى معنى يقوم بها ويُوجِبُ لها التأثير ، ويُنقَلُ الكلام إلى ذلك المعنى الذي أوجب لها التأثيرَ ، وهل ذلك أيضاً مِنْ صفةِ نفسِهِ أو لمعنى قام به ؟ ويلزمُ ما سبق والتسلسل (٣) ، وقيام المعنى بالمعنى.
I say: And as for the statement that was transmitted from the Imam, its corruption is not hidden, intellectually and by transmission; because the temporally originated power, when it is related to the existence of an act according to the implication of this statement: either the bringing into existence of the act to which it is related to bring into existence is from its own essential attribute or not. And if it is the second - namely that the effect is not an essential attribute of the temporally originated power - it follows that it is in need of a meaning subsisting in it that necessitates for it the effect, and the discourse is transferred to that meaning which necessitated for it the effect, and is that also from its own essential attribute or from a meaning subsisting in it? And what preceded and infinite regress follow (3), and the subsistence of a meaning by a meaning.

Commentary

The author states that the view is clearly corrupt both rationally and by transmitted evidence. He then presents a logical dilemma: The temporally originated power, when it is related to the existence of an act, either has the ability to bring that act into existence as an essential attribute of itself, or it does not.

Causality and Created Things

The Essential Attribute Argument
First two arguments against the appeal to divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why appealing to divine will does not resolve the contradiction of created power being effective.

Core Idea
If effect is essential to created power, divine will cannot change that.

The author begins by addressing a common defense: that created power acts according to God's will. This might seem to preserve human agency while avoiding conflict with divine omnipotence. However, the author shows that this defense fails because if the effect is an essential attribute of created power, it cannot depend on anything external, even divine will.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem context
Recall that the core issue is whether human acts are truly caused by created power or only by God. The opponent claims they are caused by humans but in accordance with God's will.
This sets up the refutation.
2
Read the first argument
Focus on the statement: 'because if the effect is considered to be an essential attribute... cannot be that its being established for it depends on anything at all.' This is the key logical point.
An essential attribute (sifa nafsiyya) belongs to the essence inherently, not by external cause.
3
Read the second argument
Now examine the argument about will (irada) being particularization (takhsis). The author says the slave already particularizes the act by his own choice, so the act cannot be also particularized by God's eternal will.
This shows a redundancy or contradiction.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider: if the act is already fully particularized by the human, what room is left for divine will to determine its specific manner?
This leads to the conclusion that the defense is incoherent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يدفعُ محذور ما لزمَ مِنْ عجز القدرة القديمة في هذا قوله ( إنَّ تأثير القدرةِ الحادثة إنَّما هو على وَفْقِ إرادتِهِ تعالى ) لأنَّ التأثير إذا قُدْرَ أَنَّهُ صفةٌ نفسيَّة للقدرة الحادثة.. لم يمكن أن يتوقف ثبوتُهُ لها على شيء أصلاً . وأيضاً : فالإرادة تخصيص ، والتخصيص : عبارة عن وقوع الفعل على وجه مخصوص ، والعبد يوقعه على الوجه المخصوص ، فلا يصح أن يكون مخصصاً بالإرادة الأزلية. وإرادة فعل الغير إذا حُقَّقَتْ تمن وشهوة ، لا أنَّها إرادة وتخصيص حقيقة ؛ إذ القصد الذي هو معنى الإرادة الحقيقيَّةِ إنَّما يتعلَّق بفعل القاصدِ ، فقد لزمَ إذاً خروجُ فعل العبدِ عن مشيئة الله تعالى وإرادتِهِ الحقيقيَّةِ كما خرج عن قدرته (٣) ، والإجماع على أنَّ ما شاءَ اللهُ كَانَ وما لم يشأ لم يكن . وأيضاً : فانصرافُ القدرةِ الحادثة إلى إيجادِ شيء لا يعلمه العبد ولا يقدر أن يوجدَهُ إِنْ لم يردِ الله تعالى وجودَه . . هو جبر هربَ الإمامُ مِنَ الجبر إليه. فأنت ترى فساد هذهِ الأقوالِ واختلالها في غاية ، ويُخشى على مُعتقِدِ صحةٍ واحدٍ منها أنْ يكونَ مِنَ القدريَّةِ الذين ورد فيهم ما ورد .
And the statement (that the effect of the temporally originated power is only in accordance with His will Almighty) does not remove the harm of what follows from the incapacity of the eternal power in this, because if the effect is considered to be an essential attribute of the temporally originated power, it cannot be that its being established for it depends on anything at all. Also: will (irada) is particularization (takhsis), and particularization is an expression for the occurrence of the act in a specific manner, and the slave makes it occur in a specific manner, so it is not correct that he be particularized by the eternal will. And the will of another's act, when analyzed, is desire (tamanni) and wish (shahwa), not that it is will and particularization in reality; for the intention (qasd) which is the meaning of real will only relates to the act of the one who intends. So it follows then that the slave's act exits from God's will and His real volition just as it exited from His power (3), and there is consensus that whatever God wills occurs and whatever He does not will does not occur. Also: the turning of the temporally originated power to bringing into existence something that the slave does not know and cannot bring to existence unless God wills its existence is a compulsion (jabr) that the Imam fled to from compulsion. So you see the corruption and disorder of these statements is extreme, and it is feared for anyone who believes the validity of even one of them that he be from the Qadariyya about whom what has been transmitted.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the attempt to defend the idea that created power is effective but in accordance with divine will. The author presents several arguments to show this does not solve the problem and actually leads to worse consequences.

First argument: If the effect is an essential attribute of created power, then it cannot depend on anything else, including divine will. So appealing to will does not remove the impossibility.

Second argument: Divine will is particularization (takhsis), meaning it determines the specific manner of an act. But the human agent already does that by his own choice, so the act cannot be particularized by both the human and God's eternal will simultaneously.

Third argument: When analyzed, willing an act of another is actually just wishing ('tamanni' and 'shahwa'), not genuine will (irada) and particularization (takhsis). Real will relates to one's own act. Thus, if human acts are truly caused by created power, they would be outside God's real will and power, contradicting the consensus that nothing occurs outside God's will.

Fourth argument: The alternative—that created power brings about something the human does not know and cannot bring about unless God wills it—is actually a form of determinism (jabr). The Imam fled from one kind of determinism only to fall into another.

Conclusion: These views are deeply corrupt, and believing any of them puts one in danger of being among the Qadariyya, a sect condemned in hadith.

Key Terms
إرادة
تخصيص
قدرة حادثة
صفة نفسية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that appealing to divine will solves the problem of created power's effectiveness without contradicting divine omnipotence.
Correction: The author shows that if the effect is essential to created power, it cannot depend on divine will; and if will is particularization, the act cannot be particularized by both human and divine will.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument against the appeal to divine will?
A) If the effect is an essential attribute of created power, it cannot depend on divine will.
B) Divine will is not particularization.
C) The human cannot particularize any act.
D) The eternal power is incapable.

Key Takeaway
Appealing to divine will does not resolve the original contradiction because an essential attribute cannot depend on external factors, and will as particularization conflicts with human agency.

?

The Nature of Will and the Charge of Determinism
Third and fourth arguments, and the warning about Qadariyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the further arguments against the appeal to divine will, including the analysis of will as mere desire and the accusation that the opponent's view leads to determinism.

Core Idea
Willing another's act is not real will but desire, and the alternative is determinism.

The author continues the refutation by analyzing what it means for God to will another's act. He argues that genuine will (irada) relates only to one's own act; willing another's act reduces to wish (tamanni) and desire (shahwa). Thus, if human acts are truly caused by humans, they would be outside God's real will and power, contradicting consensus. Moreover, the opposite view—that created power brings about something unknown—is a form of determinism (jabr).

Guided Path
1
Read the third argument
Focus on the analysis of will: 'the will of another's act, when analyzed, is desire and wish, not that it is will and particularization in reality.' Understand why real will must be about one's own act.
This is a key conceptual point about the nature of irada.
2
Follow the consequence
If real will relates only to one's own act, then if humans truly cause their acts, those acts are not willed by God with His real will, and thus exit His power—contradicting the consensus that everything occurs by God's will.
The author cites the consensus: 'whatever God wills occurs, and whatever He does not will does not occur.'
3
Read the fourth argument
The opponent might say: created power brings about something the human does not know and cannot bring about unless God wills it. The author calls this 'a compulsion that the Imam fled to from compulsion.'
This highlights the ironic outcome: escaping one determinism leads to another.
4
Consider the conclusion
The author warns that believing any of these arguments to be sound could put one among the Qadariyya, a condemned sect.
This underscores the gravity of the theological error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يدفعُ محذور ما لزمَ مِنْ عجز القدرة القديمة في هذا قوله ( إنَّ تأثير القدرةِ الحادثة إنَّما هو على وَفْقِ إرادتِهِ تعالى ) لأنَّ التأثير إذا قُدْرَ أَنَّهُ صفةٌ نفسيَّة للقدرة الحادثة.. لم يمكن أن يتوقف ثبوتُهُ لها على شيء أصلاً . وأيضاً : فالإرادة تخصيص ، والتخصيص : عبارة عن وقوع الفعل على وجه مخصوص ، والعبد يوقعه على الوجه المخصوص ، فلا يصح أن يكون مخصصاً بالإرادة الأزلية. وإرادة فعل الغير إذا حُقَّقَتْ تمن وشهوة ، لا أنَّها إرادة وتخصيص حقيقة ؛ إذ القصد الذي هو معنى الإرادة الحقيقيَّةِ إنَّما يتعلَّق بفعل القاصدِ ، فقد لزمَ إذاً خروجُ فعل العبدِ عن مشيئة الله تعالى وإرادتِهِ الحقيقيَّةِ كما خرج عن قدرته (٣) ، والإجماع على أنَّ ما شاءَ اللهُ كَانَ وما لم يشأ لم يكن . وأيضاً : فانصرافُ القدرةِ الحادثة إلى إيجادِ شيء لا يعلمه العبد ولا يقدر أن يوجدَهُ إِنْ لم يردِ الله تعالى وجودَه . . هو جبر هربَ الإمامُ مِنَ الجبر إليه. فأنت ترى فساد هذهِ الأقوالِ واختلالها في غاية ، ويُخشى على مُعتقِدِ صحةٍ واحدٍ منها أنْ يكونَ مِنَ القدريَّةِ الذين ورد فيهم ما ورد .
And the statement (that the effect of the temporally originated power is only in accordance with His will Almighty) does not remove the harm of what follows from the incapacity of the eternal power in this, because if the effect is considered to be an essential attribute of the temporally originated power, it cannot be that its being established for it depends on anything at all. Also: will (irada) is particularization (takhsis), and particularization is an expression for the occurrence of the act in a specific manner, and the slave makes it occur in a specific manner, so it is not correct that he be particularized by the eternal will. And the will of another's act, when analyzed, is desire (tamanni) and wish (shahwa), not that it is will and particularization in reality; for the intention (qasd) which is the meaning of real will only relates to the act of the one who intends. So it follows then that the slave's act exits from God's will and His real volition just as it exited from His power (3), and there is consensus that whatever God wills occurs and whatever He does not will does not occur. Also: the turning of the temporally originated power to bringing into existence something that the slave does not know and cannot bring to existence unless God wills its existence is a compulsion (jabr) that the Imam fled to from compulsion. So you see the corruption and disorder of these statements is extreme, and it is feared for anyone who believes the validity of even one of them that he be from the Qadariyya about whom what has been transmitted.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the attempt to defend the idea that created power is effective but in accordance with divine will. The author presents several arguments to show this does not solve the problem and actually leads to worse consequences.

First argument: If the effect is an essential attribute of created power, then it cannot depend on anything else, including divine will. So appealing to will does not remove the impossibility.

Second argument: Divine will is particularization (takhsis), meaning it determines the specific manner of an act. But the human agent already does that by his own choice, so the act cannot be particularized by both the human and God's eternal will simultaneously.

Third argument: When analyzed, willing an act of another is actually just wishing ('tamanni' and 'shahwa'), not genuine will (irada) and particularization (takhsis). Real will relates to one's own act. Thus, if human acts are truly caused by created power, they would be outside God's real will and power, contradicting the consensus that nothing occurs outside God's will.

Fourth argument: The alternative—that created power brings about something the human does not know and cannot bring about unless God wills it—is actually a form of determinism (jabr). The Imam fled from one kind of determinism only to fall into another.

Conclusion: These views are deeply corrupt, and believing any of them puts one in danger of being among the Qadariyya, a sect condemned in hadith.

شهوة

القدرية

Misconception: One might think that 'God wills all human acts' means He actively desires each act, but the author shows this is impossible for acts of others.
Correction: Real will relates only to one's own act; willing another's act is merely wish or desire.

Checkpoint

B) It is particularization (takhsis).
C) It is desire (tamanni) and wish (shahwa).
D) It is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Appealing to divine will fails because it either removes acts from God's real will or leads to determinism, and such errors place one among the Qadariyya.

Refutation of Created Power's Dependence on Divine Will
Sanusi refutes the argument that created power acts in accordance with divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the claim that the created power's effect is merely in accordance with divine will, and why this claim fails to resolve the problem of divine omnipotence and human agency.

Core Idea
Sanusi shows that linking created power to divine will leads to contradictions, not solutions.

In this passage, Sanusi tackles a sophisticated argument from opponents who try to protect divine omnipotence by making the effect of human power dependent on divine will. However, he demonstrates that this move backfires, resulting in either the power's effect being independent or human acts falling outside God's will, or even a hidden compulsion. Let's examine his three objections carefully.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's argument
Identify the claim that the created power's effect is only in accordance with divine will, and why it is presented as a solution to preserve divine power.
This argument tries to avoid the problem of divine impotence.
2
Follow Sanusi's first objection
Notice how Sanusi argues that if the effect is essential to created power, it cannot depend on anything, not even divine will.
Essential attributes are independent.
3
Examine the second objection concerning will
Understand the definition of will as specification and why willing another's act is not genuine will.
This leads to the act being outside God's will.
4
Analyze the third objection about hidden compulsion
See how the opponent's view results in a form of compulsion, contradicting their intention.
Sanusi warns about falling into Qadariyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يدفعُ محذور ما لزمَ مِنْ عجز القدرة القديمة في هذا قوله (١): ( إنَّ تأثير القدرةِ الحادثة إنَّما هو على وَفْقِ إرادتِهِ تعالى ( لأنَّ التأثير إذا قُدْرَ أَنَّهُ صفةٌ نفسيَّة للقدرة الحادثة.. لم يمكن أن يتوقف ثبوتُهُ لها على شيء أصلاً . وأيضاً : فالإرادة تخصيص ، والتخصيص : عبارة عن وقوع الفعل على وجه مخصوص ، والعبد يوقعه على الوجه المخصوص ، فلا يصح أن يكون مخصصاً بالإرادة الأزلية . وإرادة فعل الغير إذا حُقَّقَتْ تمن وشهوة ، لا أنَّها إرادة وتخصيص حقيقة ؛ إذ القصد الذي هو معنى الإرادة الحقيقيَّةِ إنَّما يتعلَّق بفعل القاصدِ ، فقد لزمَ إذاً خروجُ فعل العبدِ عن مشيئة الله تعالى وإرادتِهِ الحقيقيَّةِ كما خرج عن قدرته (٣) ، والإجماع على أنَّ ما شاءَ اللهُ كَانَ وما لم يشأ لم يكن . وأيضاً : فانصرافُ القدرةِ الحادثة إلى إيجادِ شيء لا يعلمه العبد ولا يقدر أن يوجدَهُ إِنْ لم يردِ الله تعالى وجودَه . . هو جبر هربَ الإمامُ مِنَ الجبر إليه . فأنت ترى فساد هذهِ الأقوالِ واختلالها في غاية ، ويُخشى على مُعتقِدِ صحةٍ واحدٍ منها أنْ يكونَ مِنَ القدريَّةِ الذين ورد فيهم ما ورد .
And the objectionable consequence that followed from the incapability of the eternal power is not repelled by his statement (1): 'That the effect of the created power is only in accordance with His will, may He be exalted,' because the effect, if it is considered to be an essential attribute of the created power, it cannot be that its establishment for it depends on anything at all. Also: the will is specification, and specification is an expression of the occurrence of the act in a particular manner, and the servant causes it to occur in that particular manner, so it is not valid that it be specified by the eternal will. And the willing of another's act, when realized, is desire and lust, not that it is actually will and specification; since the intent, which is the meaning of true will, only pertains to the act of the one who intends. So it necessarily follows that the servant's act is excluded from the will of God and His true will, just as it was excluded from His power, and there is consensus that what God wills comes to be and what He does not will does not come to be. Also: the direction of the created power to bring into existence something that the servant does not know and cannot bring into existence unless God wills its existence is a compulsion from which the Imam fled into compulsion. So you see the corruption of these statements and their disorder is extreme, and it is feared for one who believes in the soundness of any one of them that he may be among the Qadariyya, about whom what has been said has been said.
Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an argument that attempts to avoid the necessary conclusion that the created power (qudra haditha) is ineffective. The opponent argues that the effect of the created power is merely 'in accordance with' divine will, not independent. Sanusi presents three objections. First, if the effect is an essential attribute of the created power, it cannot depend on anything else, not even divine will, because essential attributes are not contingent. Second, the will is specification (takhsis) of an act to a particular manner; the servant already causes the act in that manner, so it cannot be specified again by the eternal will. Moreover, willing another's act is not true will but desire; true will only relates to one's own act. Therefore, the servant's act would be outside God's true will and power, contradicting the consensus that what God wills is and what He does not will is not. Third, if the created power only acts when God wills, and the servant does not know or have the ability to bring about that act unless God wills it, this is a form of compulsion (jabr) that the Imam (al-Juwayni) was trying to avoid. Sanusi concludes that these arguments are corrupt and believes that accepting any of them puts one in danger of being among the Qadariyya, who are condemned.

قدرة قديمة
إرادة
تخصيص

قدريّة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that linking the created power's effect to divine will solves the problem of human agency and divine omnipotence.
Correction: Sanusi shows it leads to contradictions: either the effect is independent of divine will, or human acts fall outside God's will, or it results in hidden compulsion.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi reject the claim that the created power's effect is 'in accordance with' divine will?
A) Because the created power cannot have any effect at all.
B) Because if the effect is an essential attribute of the created power, it cannot depend on divine will, and the argument leads to the act being outside God's will or to compulsion.
C) Because the divine will is not involved in human acts.
D) Because the created power is a metaphor and not real.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi demonstrates that the attempt to make created power dependent on divine will collapses under its own contradictions, preserving neither divine omnipotence nor genuine human agency.

Causality and Created Things

Denial of Causal Efficacy in Secondary Causes
Author asserts that created causes have no real effect; all is directly from God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari occasionalist denial of causal efficacy in secondary causes and how all effects are directly from Allah.

Core Idea
Created causes have no real effect; all is directly from Allah.

One of the most distinctive doctrines of Ash'ari theology is its denial of causal efficacy in created things. In this passage, Sanusi asserts that the relationship between causes and their effects is merely habitual, not necessary. He provides a series of concrete examples to illustrate this principle, from food and satiety to fire and burning, and concludes that all originates from Allah without any intermediary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full literal translation to grasp the author's argument.
Notice the use of 'عادية' (habitual) – this is the key term.
2
Identify the central claim and examples
The passage begins with a universal denial: 'ليس للأسباب أي أثر في مسبباتها'. Then it lists many examples. Identify the pattern across them.
Each example pairs a cause with its supposed effect, then denies the causal link.
3
Understand the theological conclusion
The final sentence emphasizes that all comes directly from Allah, without any real effect from the accompanying objects. Reflect on why this matters for divine omnipotence.
This is the foundation of Ash'ari occasionalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليس للأسباب أي أثر في مسبباتها ، وإنَّما العلاقة بينهما عاديَّةٌ ] وكذا لا أثر للطعام في الشبع ، ولا للماء في الرّي والنباتِ والنظافة ، ولا للنارِ في الإحراق أو التسخين أو نضْحِ الطعام ، ولا للثوبِ أوِ الجدارِ في السَّتْرِ أو دفع الحرّ والبرد ، ولا للشجرة في الظل ، ولا للشمس وســائـر الكواكب في الضوء ، ولا للسكين في القطع ، ولا للماء البارد في كَسْرِ قوَّةِ حرارةِ ماءٍ آخر ، كما لا أثر لذلك الآخر في كَسْرِ قوَّةِ بردِهِ ، وقِسْ على هذا كل ما أجرى الله تعالى عادته أنْ يُوجد عنده شيئاً . ولتعلم أنَّهُ مِنَ اللهِ تعالى بدءاً ، بلا واسطة ولا أثر فيه لتلكَ الأشياء المقارنة ، لا بطبعها ، ولا بقوَّةٍ أو خاصة
There is no effect for causes in their effects; rather the relationship between them is habitual. Likewise, there is no effect for food in satiety, nor for water in irrigation, plants, and cleanliness, nor for fire in burning, heating, or cooking food, nor for a garment or a wall in covering or repelling heat and cold, nor for a tree in shade, nor for the sun and other planets in light, nor for a knife in cutting, nor for cold water in breaking the intensity of heat of another water, just as there is no effect for that other [water] in breaking the intensity of its cold. And analogize to this everything that Allah the Exalted has made His custom that something occurs at its presence. And know that it is from Allah the Exalted as a beginning, without intermediary and without any effect in those accompanying things, neither by their nature nor by a power or a particular property.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This is a concise but forceful assertion of the Ash'ari occasionalist position on causality. The passage opens with a universal denial: causes have no effect whatsoever in their effects. The relationship between a cause and its effect is merely 'habitual' ('ādiyya), meaning that God habitually creates the effect upon the existence of the cause, but the cause itself has no causal power. The author then lists a series of concrete examples to remove any doubt: food does not cause satiety, water does not cause irrigation, fire does not cause burning, etc. Each example is chosen to cover different domains: nourishment, growth, heat, covering, shade, light, cutting, and temperature changes. The list closes with an instruction to analogize all similar cases. The final sentence drives the theological point home: all originates from Allah directly—without intermediary and without any effect from the accompanying objects, whether by their own nature or by a power or property inherent in them. This is the classic Ash'ari refutation of natural causality in favor of divine omnipotence.

مسببات
عادية
أثر
بدءا
طبع
قوة
خاصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this denies observable regularity; but the regularity is affirmed as God's custom (عادة), not as necessary causal connection.
Correction: The passage affirms that the relationship is habitual (عادية), meaning God consistently creates the effect upon the cause, but the cause has no inherent power.

Checkpoint

A) Necessary and intrinsic.
B) Habitual (عادية) without causal necessity.
C) Unrelated and random.
D) Dependent on the cause's power.

Key Takeaway
The passage teaches that nothing in creation has inherent causal power; everything is directly from Allah.

By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ash'ari theologians deny inherent causal efficacy in natural objects and affirm that all effects are directly from Allah.

Core Idea
Natural objects have no real causal power; all effects are from Allah alone.

Sanusi begins by stating a fundamental principle of Ash'ari theology: there is no necessary connection between what we call causes and their effects. The relationship is merely customary, established by Allah's direct creation. He then provides a series of concrete examples to illustrate this point.

3

This is the takeaway: nothing in creation has independent causal power.

جعلها الله فيها كما يعتقده كثيرٌ مِنَ الجهلة
There is no effect for causes in their effects; rather the relationship between them is customary. Likewise, there is no effect for food in satiety, nor for water in quenching thirst and plant growth and cleanliness, nor for fire in burning or heating or cooking food, nor for clothing or a wall in covering or repelling heat and cold, nor for a tree in shade, nor for the sun and other planets in light, nor for a knife in cutting, nor for cold water in breaking the strength of another water's heat, just as there is no effect for that other in breaking the strength of its cold. And analogize to this everything that Allah Almighty has made it His custom that something should exist at its occurrence. And know that it is from Allah Almighty from the beginning, without intermediary and without any effect in it from those concurrent things, neither by their nature nor by a power or special property that Allah has placed in them, as many of the ignorant believe.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is a denial of any inherent causal efficacy in natural objects. The author states clearly that there is no effect for causes in their effects; the relationship is merely customary (عادي). He then lists numerous examples: food does not cause satiety, water does not cause quenching or growth, fire does not cause burning, etc. All these are merely occasions upon which Allah directly creates the effect. He emphasizes that the effect comes from Allah alone, without any intermediary, and not by the nature of the object nor by any power or special property placed in it, as many ignorant people believe. This is the Ash'ari doctrine of occasionalism.

Key Terms
أثر
الأسباب
المسببات
عاديَّة
بطبعها
قوَّة
خاصة
الجهلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Causes have inherent power to produce their effects.
Correction: The relationship is only customary; Allah alone creates the effect at the time of the cause.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between causes and effects?
A) Necessary and inherent
B) Customary and by Allah's direct creation
C) By the power of the cause itself
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
According to Sanusi, no created agent has any real causal effect; all effects are directly from Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel hungry and eat food, do you truly believe that only Allah creates satiety, or does your heart rely on the food itself? In what real situation this week can you consciously remind yourself that the food has no effect and ask Allah directly for satiety?
?
The passage says that even the special properties Allah places in objects have no causal effect. Is there a person, medicine, or means you have been depending on as if it had independent power? What step will you take today to shift your trust from that means to Allah alone?
?
If you genuinely lived by this belief—that no created thing has any effect—how would your dua change in the next hour? Write down one specific need and ask Allah for it without referencing any means, acknowledging that He alone creates the outcome.

Consensus and Disagreement on Causal Beliefs
Sanusi reports consensus and disagreement on kufr for causal beliefs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nuanced Ash'ari position on causality: the consensus that belief in natural causation is kufr, and the disagreement regarding belief in divinely-bestowed power or property.

Core Idea
Belief in inherent causal nature is kufr by consensus; divinely-bestowed power is debated.

Sanusi summarizes a key doctrinal ruling that distinguishes different ways of attributing causal efficacy to created objects. The verifying imams agree that believing objects act by their own nature is unbelief, but they differ on whether believing they act by a power or property placed by Allah is also unbelief. This passage invites us to reflect on the boundaries of tawhid regarding causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation carefully. Notice the two key terms: 'by their nature' and 'by a power or special property'.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the two rulings
The passage presents two distinct rulings: one of consensus (it is kufr to believe in natural causation) and one of disagreement (it may not be kufr to believe in divinely-bestowed power). Underline the words 'consensus' and 'disagreement'.
Consensus is on the first; disagreement on the second.
3
Reflect on the theological nuance
Ask yourself why the second belief is not universally considered kufr. How does attributing power to Allah's placement preserve tawhid?
This connects to Ash'ari occasionalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد ذكرَ غيرُ واحدٍ مِنْ مُحققي الأئمة الاتفاق على كفْرِ مَنِ اعتقد تأثير تلك الأشياء بطبعها ، والخلافُ فِي كُفْرِ مَنِ اعتقد أنَّ تأثيرها بقوَّةٍ أو خاصَّةٍ جعلها الله تعالى فيها ، وإن نزعَها لم تُؤثر .
And more than one of the verifying imams have mentioned the consensus on the unbelief of one who believes in the effect of those things by their nature, and the disagreement concerning the unbelief of one who believes that their effect is by a power or special property that Allah Almighty placed in them, and if He removed it they would not effect.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage presents a doctrinal ruling on causality. The author reports that more than one of the verifying imams have stated a consensus (اتفاق) that believing that objects affect by their nature (بطبعها) is unbelief (كفر). However, there is disagreement (خلاف) regarding whether believing that they affect by a power (بقوة) or special property (خاصة) that Allah placed in them, such that if Allah removed it they would not effect, is also unbelief. This highlights the nuanced Ash'ari position: the essentialist view (nature) is definitely kufr, but the view that grants temporary divinely-bestowed power may be disputed.

Key Terms
الاتفاق
الخلاف
كفر
بطبعها
بقوَّة
خاصَّة
محققو الأئمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any belief in secondary causality is kufr.
Correction: The passage shows that belief in divinely-bestowed power or property is debated, not universally considered kufr.

Checkpoint
On what point is there consensus among the verifying imams?
A) Believing objects affect by a power placed by Allah is kufr
B) Believing objects affect by their nature is kufr
C) Both are kufr
D) Neither is kufr

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position on causality distinguishes between inherent natural causation (kufr by consensus) and divinely-granted causal power (disputed).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a cause-and-effect relationship in your daily life (e.g., medicine heals, fire burns), do you subconsciously ascribe the effect to the object itself, or do you truly believe it is Allah's power at work? Examine your heart: does your reliance on means reflect actual trust in the Creator of means?
?
If you were to lose a ‘means’ that you depend on—like your health or a skill—would your faith in Allah's power remain unshaken, or would you feel abandoned? This passage forces you to ask: do you believe the power is loaned by Allah or owned by the object?
?
The scholars disagree on whether believing in divinely-bestowed power is kufr. Does this disagreement make you feel safe in your own belief, or does it drive you to investigate your own conviction with greater care? What would it mean for you if the stricter opinion turned out to be correct?

Ruling on Attributing Effect to Created Things
Author states consensus and disagreement on kufr for causal beliefs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the consensus and disagreement among scholars regarding attributing causal effect to created things, and the distinction between natural causality and God-granted causality.

Core Idea
Belief in independent natural causality is disbelief; God-granted causality is disputed.

This lesson addresses a critical issue in tawḥīd: can created things truly cause effects? Scholars have a clear consensus that attributing effect to a thing's own nature is kufr. However, there is disagreement on whether attributing effect to a power or property that God placed in the thing, which God could remove, constitutes disbelief. Understanding this distinction helps clarify God's sole agency in creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the two rulings mentioned: the consensus on disbelief for natural causality, and the disagreement on God-granted causality.
The passage also mentions how philosophers and others went astray.
2
Identify the two rulings
Identify the consensus (that belief in effect by nature is kufr) and the disagreement (about effect by a power/property placed by God). Note the condition: if God removed that power, the thing would have no effect.
This distinction preserves God's sole agency while acknowledging secondary causality by God's grant.
3
Reflect on the broader proof
The author says the proof is clear from the principle that God alone has effect and creation. Reflect on how this passage reinforces tawḥīd in causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جعلها الله فيها كما يعتقده كثيرٌ مِنَ الجهلة. وقد ذكرَ غيرُ واحدٍ مِنْ مُحققي الأئمة الاتفاق على كفْرِ مَنِ اعتقد تأثير تلك الأشياء بطبعها، والخلافُ فِي كُفْرِ مَنِ اعتقد أنَّ تأثيرها بقوَّةٍ أو خاصَّةٍ جعلها الله تعالى فيها، وإن نزعَها لم تُؤثر. فقد عرفت بهذهِ الجُمَل ما يجب في حقه تعالى وما يستحيلُ. أشار هنا إلى جزئيَّاتٍ ضلَّتْ بها الفلاسفة والطبائعيُّونَ، وتبعهم على فسادها كثيرٌ ممَّنْ جهلَ هذا العلم ممَّنْ يدَّعي التقدُّمَ في غيرِهِ مِنَ العلومِ، فضلاً عمَّنْ دونَهم مِنْ مُحْضِ عوام المسلمين. وبرهان جميع ذلك: وما ذكرَهُ فيها واضحُ لا يحتاجُ إلى شرحٍ، وبـ ما سبق في وجوب انفرادِهِ تعالى بالتأثير والاختراع، وما ذكرَهُ مِنَ الاتفاقِ والاختلافِ في كفرِ مَنِ اعتقد التأثير لغيره تعالى . . هو مشهور بين العلماء، منصوص عليهِ في كتبهم
God placed them in them as many ignorant people believe. And more than one of the verifying imams have mentioned the consensus on the disbelief of whoever believes in the effect of those things by their nature, and the disagreement regarding the disbelief of whoever believes that their effect is by a power or property that God Almighty placed in them, and if He removed it they would not have effect. So by these sentences you have known what is necessary regarding Him Almighty and what is impossible. He pointed here to particulars by which the philosophers and naturalists went astray, and many of those who are ignorant of this science among those who claim advancement in other sciences followed them in their corruption, let alone those below them among the plain lay Muslims. And the proof of all that: what he mentioned therein is clear and does not need explanation, and by what preceded regarding the necessity of His Almighty being alone in effect and creation, and what he mentioned of the consensus and disagreement on the disbelief of whoever believes in effect for other than Him Almighty... it is well-known among scholars, recorded in their books.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage is a ruling (ḥukm) regarding those who attribute causal efficacy to created things. The author first notes the consensus of verifying imams that whoever believes created things have effect by their own nature (ṭabʿ) is a disbeliever (kāfir). However, there is a disagreement on whether one who believes they have effect by a power (quwwa) or property (khāṣṣa) placed in them by God is a disbeliever, provided that if God removed that power, they would no longer have effect. This distinction is crucial: it preserves God's sole agency while acknowledging that secondary causality is only by God's grant. The author then says these sentences clarify what is necessary and impossible for God. He points out specific errors that philosophers and naturalists fell into, and many who lack this knowledge followed them. The proof of all this is clear from the preceding principle that God alone has effect and creation, and the consensus and disagreement are well-known among scholars and recorded in their books. The passage thus defends tawḥīd in causality and warns against attributing any independent effect to creatures.

Key Terms
تأثير
طبيعة
خاصّة
كفر
اختراع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any belief in secondary causality is permissible without distinction.
Correction: The passage distinguishes: belief in natural inherent causality is kufr, while belief in God-granted causality (power/property placed by God) is a matter of disagreement among scholars.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consensus among scholars regarding someone who believes that created things have effect by their own nature (ṭabʿ)?
A) He is a sinner but not a disbeliever.
B) He is a disbeliever.
C) He is excused due to ignorance.
D) It depends on intention.

Key Takeaway
Believing that created things have effect by their own nature is disbelief; believing they have effect by a power God placed in them is a disputed matter among scholars.

?
If you believe that a created thing has effect only by God's grant, does your heart still attribute success or failure to that thing? What would it take for you to truly rely on God alone?
