Attributing Actions to Celestial Bodies
Sanusi defines the third kind of shirk and its first subtype: attributing actions to celestial bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third kind of shirk, specifically the error of attributing causal influence to celestial bodies.

Core Idea
Claiming stars cause events is shirk, not science.

Sanusi now identifies a third kind of shirk: ascribing actions to other than Allah. One form is the belief that celestial bodies (planets, stars) influence earthly events. This was the view of philosophers and their followers. Sanusi condemns this as blind imitation leading to disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the source passage carefully. Notice the structure: definition of the kind, then first type with its description and condemnation.
The author uses strong language: 'عمي القلوب'.
2
Understand the first type
The first type is attributing action to celestial spheres. They are said to influence the lower world and generate things. This is shirk because it gives them creative power.
3
Reflect on the condemnation
Sanusi calls them blind-hearted and says they disbelieved by imitation. Why is imitation so dangerous?
Imitation without evidence leads to false beliefs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصنف آخر من الشرك: وهو إضافة الفعل لغير الله سبحانه وهذا الصنف على ثلاثة أنواع أحدها: إضافة الفعل إلى الأفلاك؛ بأنها تؤثر في العالم السفلي تأثيرات في الأجسام والنبات والمركبات، وأن البعض يتولد من البعض، وهذا النوع يختص به الفيلسوفي ومن تابعه من عامتهم. عمي القلوب عمي عن كل فائدة لأنهم كفروا بالله تقليدا. الثاني: ما أضيف من بعض الأفعال إلى البعض؛ من أن النار تحرق، والطعام يشبع، والثوب يستر، إلى غير ذلك من ربط العادات حتى ظنوها واجبة، وتلك ضلالة تبع الفيلسوفي فيها كثير من عامة المسلمين، وهم فيها على اعتقادات: فمن قال: بطبعها تفعل .. فلا خلاف في كفره. ومن قال: تفعل بقوة جعلها الله تعالى فيها .. كان مبتدعا وقد اختلفوا في تكفيره. ومن قال: الأكل مثلا دليل عقلي على الشبع دون أن يكون معتادا .. كان جاهلا بمعنى الدلالة العقلية. ومن علم أن الله سبحانه ربط بعض أفعاله ببعض أفعاله؛ فكلما فعل سبحانه هذا فعل هذا الآخر باختياره جل وعز، وإذا شاء خرق هذه العادة، وهذا هو المؤمن الذي سلم من هذه الآفة بفضل الله سبحانه.
And another kind of shirk: it is attributing action to other than Allah, and this kind is of three types: the first: attributing action to the celestial spheres; that they influence the lower world with effects in bodies, plants, and compounds, and that some generate from others. This type is specific to the philosopher and those who follow him among their commoners. Blindness of hearts, they are blind to every benefit because they disbelieved in Allah by imitation. The second: what is attributed of some actions to some things: that fire burns, food satiates, clothing covers, and so forth of the linking of customs until they thought they were necessary, and that is a misguidance in which many common Muslims followed the philosopher, and they are in it upon beliefs: whoever said: it acts by its nature... there is no disagreement about his disbelief. And whoever said: it acts by a power that Allah placed in it... he is an innovator, and they disagreed about his disbelief. And whoever said: eating, for example, is a rational indication of satiety without it being customary... he is ignorant of the meaning of rational indication. And whoever knows that Allah tied some of His actions to some of His actions; so whenever He does this, He does that by His choice, and if He wills He breaks this custom, and this is the believer who is safe from this affliction by the grace of Allah.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage describes the third kind of shirk: attributing actions to other than Allah. It is divided into three types, but here the author explains the first two. The first type is attributing effects to celestial bodies, which is the belief of philosophers that the celestial spheres influence the sublunar world. The author condemns them as blind and disbelieving by imitation. The second type is attributing customary effects to natural causes, such as fire burning, food satiating, etc. Many common Muslims followed philosophers in this. The author then lists four positions people hold on this: (1) saying that fire burns by its own nature – this is clear disbelief. (2) saying that it burns by a power placed in it by Allah – this is innovation (bid'a) with disagreement on takfir. (3) saying that eating is a rational indication of satiety without custom – this is ignorance of rational indication. (4) the correct belief: that Allah habitually links one action to another by His choice, and He can break this custom – this is the believer's stance.

Key Terms
إضافة الفعل
الأفلاك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Celestial bodies cause events on earth independently.
Correction: This is shirk because it ascribes Allah's exclusive creative power to them.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who specifically holds the belief that celestial bodies influence the lower world?
A) The common Muslims
B) The philosophers and their followers
C) The astronomers
D) The theologians

Key Takeaway
Believing that celestial bodies independently cause events on earth is a form of shirk because it ascribes Allah's exclusive power to creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you check your horoscope or read about how a certain planetary alignment affects your mood, do you recognize that even entertaining such ideas is a form of shirk? What will you do to purge that from your heart?
?
How often do you attribute good or bad events in your life to 'luck', 'coincidence', or 'the stars'? How can you train yourself to see Allah's hand in every event, as this lesson demands?
?
The author calls the philosophers 'blind of hearts' for their belief in celestial causation. Do you have any attachment to explanations of the world that exclude Allah's immediate agency? What step will you take today to strengthen your tawheed in this area?

Attributing Effects to Natural Causes (Part 1)
Sanusi presents the second type of attributing actions and the first two erroneous positions on natural causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second type of attributing actions to other than Allah: ascribing customary effects to natural causes, and the first two erroneous positions on this.

Core Idea
Saying fire burns by its nature is kufr; saying it burns by a power Allah placed in it is bid'a.

Sanusi now explains the second type of the third kind of shirk: the common mistake of attributing the effects of natural causes to the causes themselves. Many Muslims fell into this by following philosophers. Sanusi then lists four positions on this issue; this lesson covers the first two: the belief that things act by their own nature (clear disbelief) and the belief that they act by a power Allah placed in them (innovation).

Guided Path
1
Understand the second type
Read the passage where Sanusi describes the second type: attributing customary effects to natural causes. Note examples: fire burns, food satiates.
These are called 'ربط العادات' - linking of customs.
2
Identify the first position
The first position: acting by nature. This is clear disbelief. Why?
Because it denies Allah's exclusive agency.
3
Identify the second position
The second position: acting by a power placed by Allah. This is innovation with disagreement on takfir. Note the nuance.
Sanusi does not declare takfir but says they disagreed.

The Correct Belief: Allah's Habitual Action
Sanusi concludes the second type with the third erroneous position and the correct belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the last two positions on natural causality: the error of calling it 'rational indication' and the correct belief that Allah habitually links actions and can break customs.

Core Idea
The believer knows that Allah ties actions together by His will and can break the custom at any time.

Sanusi continues his discussion on attributing actions to natural causes. After presenting two erroneous positions (acting by nature, acting by delegated power), he addresses a third sophisticated error: the claim that eating is a 'rational indication' of satiety without it being customary. Lastly, he states the correct belief: Allah habitually links one action to another, but He can break the custom whenever He wills. Only the believer who holds this is safe from the affliction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the third position
Read the part where Sanusi mentions the third position: claiming rational indication without custom. Note that he calls it ignorance.
This position tries to avoid shirk but still implies necessity.
2
Learn the correct belief
The fourth position: Allah ties actions by His choice, can break custom. This is the belief of the believer.
This allows for miracles and confirms Allah's omnipotence.
3
Reflect on the outcome
Sanusi ends by saying the believer is safe from this affliction by Allah's grace. How does this belief protect from shirk?
Because it attributes all causal power to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصنف آخر من الشرك: وهو إضافة الفعل لغير الله سبحانه وهذا الصنف على ثلاثة أنواع أحدها: إضافة الفعل إلى الأفلاك؛ بأنها تؤثر في العالم السفلي تأثيرات في الأجسام والنبات والمركبات، وأن البعض يتولد من البعض، وهذا النوع يختص به الفيلسوفي ومن تابعه من عامتهم. عمي القلوب عمي عن كل فائدة لأنهم كفروا بالله تقليدا. الثاني: ما أضيف من بعض الأفعال إلى البعض؛ من أن النار تحرق، والطعام يشبع، والثوب يستر، إلى غير ذلك من ربط العادات حتى ظنوها واجبة، وتلك ضلالة تبع الفيلسوفي فيها كثير من عامة المسلمين، وهم فيها على اعتقادات: فمن قال: بطبعها تفعل .. فلا خلاف في كفره. ومن قال: تفعل بقوة جعلها الله تعالى فيها .. كان مبتدعا وقد اختلفوا في تكفيره. ومن قال: الأكل مثلا دليل عقلي على الشبع دون أن يكون معتادا .. كان جاهلا بمعنى الدلالة العقلية. ومن علم أن الله سبحانه ربط بعض أفعاله ببعض أفعاله؛ فكلما فعل سبحانه هذا فعل هذا الآخر باختياره جل وعز، وإذا شاء خرق هذه العادة، وهذا هو المؤمن الذي سلم من هذه الآفة بفضل الله سبحانه.
And another kind of shirk: it is attributing action to other than Allah, and this kind is of three types: the first: attributing action to the celestial spheres; that they influence the lower world with effects in bodies, plants, and compounds, and that some generate from others. This type is specific to the philosopher and those who follow him among their commoners. Blindness of hearts, they are blind to every benefit because they disbelieved in Allah by imitation. The second: what is attributed of some actions to some things: that fire burns, food satiates, clothing covers, and so forth of the linking of customs until they thought they were necessary, and that is a misguidance in which many common Muslims followed the philosopher, and they are in it upon beliefs: whoever said: it acts by its nature... there is no disagreement about his disbelief. And whoever said: it acts by a power that Allah placed in it... he is an innovator, and they disagreed about his disbelief. And whoever said: eating, for example, is a rational indication of satiety without it being customary... he is ignorant of the meaning of rational indication. And whoever knows that Allah tied some of His actions to some of His actions; so whenever He does this, He does that by His choice, and if He wills He breaks this custom, and this is the believer who is safe from this affliction by the grace of Allah.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage describes the third kind of shirk: attributing actions to other than Allah. It is divided into three types, but here the author explains the first two. The first type is attributing effects to celestial bodies, which is the belief of philosophers that the celestial spheres influence the sublunar world. The author condemns them as blind and disbelieving by imitation. The second type is attributing customary effects to natural causes, such as fire burning, food satiating, etc. Many common Muslims followed philosophers in this. The author then lists four positions people hold on this: (1) saying that fire burns by its own nature – this is clear disbelief. (2) saying that it burns by a power placed in it by Allah – this is innovation (bid'a) with disagreement on takfir. (3) saying that eating is a rational indication of satiety without custom – this is ignorance of rational indication. (4) the correct belief: that Allah habitually links one action to another by His choice, and He can break this custom – this is the believer's stance.

Key Terms
دلالة عقلية
خرق العادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eating is a rational indication of satiety, implying a necessary connection.
Correction: This is ignorance; the connection is customary, not logical.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of someone who says eating is a rational indication of satiety without it being customary?
A) Disbeliever
B) Innovator
C) Ignorant of the meaning of rational indication
D) Believer

Key Takeaway
The correct belief is that Allah habitually links actions, and He can break the habit; this is the foundation of belief in miracles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah can break the custom in your own life, or do you rely on natural processes as if they are necessary? What would change if you lived with that certainty?
?
Have you ever attributed an outcome purely to natural causes while forgetting that Allah is the One Who habitually connects actions? How can you actively remind yourself of His direct involvement?
?
If Allah broke the custom for you right now—allowing a seemingly impossible good to happen—would you recognize it as His direct action, or would you seek a natural explanation? What does your answer reveal about the state of your heart?

Divine Custom: Rejecting Erroneous Views on Causation
Sanusi enumerates two erroneous views on causation and affirms the correct belief in divine custom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between erroneous views on causation (creaturely power or rational proof without custom) and the correct Ash'ari belief in divine custom (Allah links actions by His choice and can break custom).

Core Idea
True belief in divine causality affirms Allah's unmediated power and freedom.

This passage from Sanusi’s Umm al-Barahin corrects two common misunderstandings about how causes and effects relate. Some claim creatures act by their own power; others treat natural sequences as rational proofs independent of divine habit. Sanusi clarifies that the believer recognizes Allah’s direct linkage of events by His choice, while upholding His ability to break custom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note how Sanusi presents three distinct positions in a compact form.
2
Identify the three positions
Find the first error (creature acts by a power placed in it), the second error (event as rational proof without habit), and the correct view (Allah links events by choice and may break custom).
The terms 'innovator' and 'ignorant' signal the errors.
3
Contrast errors with correct view
Understand why the errors are problematic: the first attributes causal efficacy to creatures, the second misapplies rational proof. The correct view preserves divine omnipotence and the order of custom without necessitation.
This contrast is central to Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومَنْ قالَ : تفعلُ بقوَّةٍ جعلَها اللهُ تعالى فيها . . كان مبتدعاً ، وقد اختلفوا في تكفيره . ومَنْ قالَ : الأكلُ مثلاً دليلٌ عقلي على الشبع دون أن يكونَ معتاداً . . كان جاهلاً بمعنى الدَّلالة العقليَّةِ . ومَنْ علم أنَّ الله سبحانهُ ربط بعض أفعالِهِ ببعض أفعالِهِ ؛ فكلَّما فعل سبحانه هذا فعل هذا الآخرَ باختيارِهِ جلَّ وعزَّ ، وإذا شاءَ خرق هذهِ العادة ، وهذا هو المؤمنُ الذي سَلِمَ مِنْ هذِهِ الآفة بفضْلِ اللهِ سبحانَهُ
And whoever says: 'It acts by a power which Allah placed in it' ... is an innovator, and they differed concerning declaring him a disbeliever. And whoever says: 'Eating, for example, is a rational proof of satiety without it being habitual' ... is ignorant of the meaning of rational proof. And whoever knows that Allah has linked some of His acts to some of His acts, so whenever He does this, He does that other by His choice, and if He wills, He breaks this custom, and this is the believer who is saved from this affliction by the favor of Allah.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage presents three positions regarding the relationship between actions and divine custom. The first error is the view that creatures act by a power Allah placed in them, implying independent causation. The second error is that a natural cause like eating is a rational proof of its effect without considering habitual custom. Both misunderstand divine causality. The correct view is that Allah has linked actions by His choice but can break custom whenever He wills. The one who affirms this is the true believer saved from these afflictions.

Key Terms
العادة (al-‘āda, custom)
دليل عقلي (dalīl ‘aqlī, rational proof)
اختيار (ikhtiyār, choice)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'rational proof' means direct causation without reference to divine custom.
Correction: The text clarifies that rational proof operates within the context of established custom; ignoring custom is ignorance.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the status of the one who says that eating is a rational proof of satiety without it being habitual?
A) He is a true believer
B) He is an innovator
C) He is ignorant of the meaning of rational proof
D) He is a disbeliever

Key Takeaway
The correct position on causality is that Allah links events by His choice and can break custom; belief in this saves one from innovation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every outcome you achieve—every success, every recovery from illness—is directly from Allah's choice, or do you unconsciously credit your own efforts or natural processes as independent causes? Examine your heart: where do you place your trust?
?
When you pray for something that seems impossible, do you honestly expect that Allah can break His custom to answer you? Or are you, without realizing, limited by the illusion that 'things always happen this way'? Let this lesson challenge your certainty.
?
Consider a habit you rely on daily—like eating to be full. Does your mind see the food as the cause of satiety, or do you see it as a sign of Allah's consistent will that He could suspend at any moment? Repent if you have attributed power to anything other than Him.

The Reality of Sorcery: Illusion as Created Effect
Sanusi answers the objection that sorcery is mere illusion, affirming its effect as Allah's creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defends the reality of sorcery's effect while maintaining that only Allah creates, and how he answers the objection from the Quranic verse.

Core Idea
Sorcery's effect is real because Allah creates it, even if it is an illusion.

Sanusi is building a consistent theology of causality: only Allah is the true creator. In this passage, he responds to a common objection that the Quranic story of Musa (AS) and the magicians proves sorcery is pure illusion. He argues that even if sorcery only produces an illusion in the mind, that illusion is itself a real effect created by Allah, thus preserving both divine sovereignty and the empirical reality of sorcery's impact.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection from the Quran
The objector cites verse 20:66 to claim sorcery has no reality. Note the phrase 'it seemed to him' which implies illusion.
The verse is about the magicians' ropes and staffs appearing to move.
2
Identify Sanusi's answer
He concedes that sorcery may produce an illusion, but emphasizes that Allah creates that illusion at the moment of the sorcerers' action.
The illusion is a real created entity in the observer's mind.
3
Understand the subtle point
Even if the only effect is a mental image, that still constitutes a reality (haqiqa). The footnote clarifies that the least reality is the insertion of the image into the imagination.
4
Connect to the overarching principle
This preserves the principle that Allah is the sole creator of all effects, including those associated with sorcery.
No independent causal power is attributed to the sorcerer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المؤثر والخالقَ إِنَّما هو الله تعالى وحدَهُ
فإن قيل : قوله تعالى في قصَّةِ موسى عليهِ السلامُ : ﴿ يُخَيَّلُ إِلَيْهِ مِن سِحْرِهِمْ أَنَّهَا تَسْعَى ﴾ [طه : ٦٦] يدلُّ على أنَّه لا حقيقة للسحر ، وإِنَّما تخييل وتموية .
أجيب عنه : بجواز أنْ يكونَ سحرهم إيقاع ذلك التخييل ، وقد خلقه الله تعالى عندَ ذلك الفعل الذي وقع على أيديهم ، ولو سُلَّمَ فكونُ أثره في تلك الصورة هو التخييلَ لا يدلُّ على أنَّهُ لا حقيقة لهُ أصلاً (۱) وأما إصابة العين - وهو أن يكون لبعض النفوس خاصيَّةٌ ؛ وهي أنَّها إذا استحسنَتْ شيئاً لحقته الآفة - : فبمحض خلْقِ اللهِ تعالى ، ولا أثرَ لتلك النفس العائنةِ أصلاً ، وإِنَّما استحسانها مُجرَّدُ أمارةٍ عاديَّةٍ فقط فثبوتها يكاد يجري مجرى المشاهدات التي لا تفتقر إلى حُجَّةٍ ، وقد قال النبيُّ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ : « الْعَيْنُ حَقٌّ (۲) ، وقال : « الْعَيْنُ تُدْخِلُ الرَّجُلَ الْقَبْرَ ، وَالْجَمَلَ الْقِدْرَ (٣)
نسأله سبحانه السلامة إلى المماتِ مِنْ شرِّ أَنفسنا ، ومِنْ شَرِّ كُلَّ ذي شرّ ، بمنه وكرمه .
(۱) إذ يقال : أقلُّ حقيقة له حينئذ إدخال هذه الصورة المرئية إلى خيال الرائي
(۲) رواه البخاري ( ٥٧٤٠ ) ، ومسلم (۲۱۸۷) من حديث سيدنا أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه .
(۳) رواه أبو نعيم في ( الحلية ) ( ٧ ٩٠ ) من حديث سيدنا جابر رضي الله عنه .
The effective agent and creator is only Allah Almighty alone. If it is said: His statement in the story of Moses, peace be upon him: 'It seemed to him, from their sorcery, that they were moving' [Taha:66] indicates that sorcery has no reality, but rather it is illusion and deception. The answer to it: It is possible that their sorcery was causing that illusion, and Allah created it at that action that occurred at their hands. And even if it is conceded, then its effect in that form being the illusion does not indicate that it has no reality at all (1). As for the evil eye – which is that some souls have a property, that when they admire something, affliction befalls it – then it is solely by Allah's creation, and there is no effect for that envious soul at all. Rather, its admiration is merely a customary sign only. So its establishment is almost like matters of observation that do not need a proof. The Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said: 'The eye is real' (2), and said: 'The eye causes the man to enter the grave and the camel to enter the pot' (3). We ask Him, glorified, for safety until death from the evil of ourselves and from the evil of every possessor of evil, by His grace and generosity. (1) It is said: The least reality for it then is the insertion of this visible image into the imagination of the seer. (2) Narrated by al-Bukhari (5740) and Muslim (2187) from the hadith of our master Abu Hurayra, may Allah be pleased with him. (3) Narrated by Abu Nu'aym in al-Hilya (7:90) from the hadith of our master Jabir, may Allah be pleased with him.
Source pages p. 526

Commentary
This passage refutes a specific objection about sorcery and then affirms the reality of the evil eye. The author first states the fundamental principle: only Allah is the true effecter and creator. Then an objection is raised: the Quranic verse about Moses and the sorcerers suggests that sorcery is mere illusion (takhyl wa tamwiyya). The author answers by granting that sorcery may produce an illusion, but that illusion is itself created by Allah at the moment of the sorcerers' action. Even if the only effect is an illusion in the mind of the beholder, that still constitutes a reality—namely, the mental image. The author then addresses the evil eye ('ayn). He defines it as a special property in some souls: when they admire something, harm befalls that thing. However, the effect is purely by Allah's creation; the soul has no independent causal power. Its admiration is merely a customary sign (amara 'adiyya) that typically accompanies the harm. The reality of such effects is so well-attested as to be nearly observational, and the Prophet confirmed it with explicit hadiths. The passage concludes with a supplication for safety.

Key Terms
سحر (sihr)
تخييل (takhyl)
تموية (tamwiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if sorcery only produces an illusion, it has no reality whatsoever, and thus sorcery is not a real phenomenon that can affect people.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that an illusion is a real mental effect created by Allah. Therefore, sorcery does produce a real effect (in the perception), even if no physical change occurs. This preserves the Quranic description while upholding divine causality.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the reality (haqiqa) of sorcery?
A) Sorcery has no reality at all; it is pure deception.
B) Sorcery has reality because the illusion it produces is a real created effect.
C) Sorcery is real only when it causes physical harm.
D) Sorcery's reality is limited to the time of Moses.

Key Takeaway
Sorcery's reality lies in the illusion it produces, which is a genuine created effect, not in any independent causal power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed something as 'just an illusion' or 'not real' to avoid taking responsibility for its effects? How does Sanusi's argument challenge your tendency to minimize the reality of what Allah creates?
?
When you encounter something that seems to have power—like sorcery or even your own skills—do you instinctively attribute the effect to the means or to Allah? Which of these two do you truly believe in your heart, and how does this lesson push you to realign?
?
If sorcery's illusion is a real created effect, then every perception—including your own fears and desires—is also a creation of Allah. What false 'reality' are you currently attributing to yourself or others that you need to surrender as Allah's creation?

The Reality of the Evil Eye: Created Harm with a Customary Sign
Sanusi affirms the reality of the evil eye while denying any independent causal power to the soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reality of the evil eye as a special property in some souls, its effect being solely by Allah's creation, and the evidence from hadith.

Core Idea
The evil eye is a real effect, but its cause is merely a customary sign from Allah.

After discussing sorcery, Sanusi turns to another common concern: the evil eye. He defines it as a special property in some souls, but immediately clarifies that the harm that befalls the admired object is created solely by Allah, not by the soul itself. The admiration is only a customary sign (amara ‘adiyya) that typically accompanies the affliction. He then provides hadith evidence and states that the reality of such effects is practically observational.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the evil eye
It is a property in some souls that when they admire something, harm occurs. Note the term 'khassiyya' (special property).
2
Understand the qualification
The harm is created by Allah alone; the soul has no effect. Its admiration is merely a customary sign (amara 'adiyya).
This preserves divine sovereignty.
3
Note the epistemological status
Sanusi says the reality of the evil eye is almost like observation, needing no separate proof.
He relies on widespread experience and hadith.
4
Review the hadith proofs
Two hadith: 'The eye is real' and 'The eye causes the man to enter the grave and the camel to enter the pot.' These confirm both reality and severity.
These are well-known hadith in Islamic tradition.

Source Text

Even if the only effect is an illusion in the mind of the beholder, that still constitutes a reality—namely, the mental image. The author then addresses the evil eye ('ayn). He defines it as a special property in some souls: when they admire something, harm befalls that thing. However, the effect is purely by Allah's creation; the soul has no independent causal power. Its admiration is merely a customary sign (amara 'adiyya) that typically accompanies the harm. The reality of such effects is so well-attested as to be nearly observational, and the Prophet confirmed it with explicit hadiths. The passage concludes with a supplication for safety.

Key Terms
إصابة العين (isabat al-ayn)
خاصية (khassiyya)
أمارة عادية (amara 'adiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the evil eye implies that the admirer's soul has independent causal power to inflict harm, which would challenge divine sovereignty.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that the harm is directly created by Allah, and the soul's admiration is only a customary sign, not a cause.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the role of the admiring soul in causing harm through the evil eye?
A) The soul directly creates the harm.
B) The soul's admiration is a customary sign that accompanies harm created by Allah.
C) The soul has no connection to the harm; it is random.
D) The soul causes harm only if it is sinful.

Key Takeaway
The evil eye's harm is a real phenomenon, but it is directly created by Allah, not by the admirer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you admire something beautiful, do you instinctively fear the evil eye more than you trust Allah's protection? How will you train your heart to see Allah as the sole creator of harm and benefit, not the look of a person?
?
Have you ever attributed a misfortune to someone's evil eye, forgetting that it is Allah who decreed it? What step will you take today to correct your speech and reliance on Allah alone?
?
The Prophet warned that the eye can cause death. Does this knowledge make you anxious, or does it drive you to seek refuge in Allah? What specific du'a will you commit to reciting regularly to protect yourself and your loved ones?
